COACHING BIBLE — FULL OUTLINE
ALL THIS NEEDS REVIEWING
Section 0 — Client Intake
Overview
Section 0 defines every input the system collects from the client across Stage 1 and Stage 2. It documents what is asked, in what order, what the available options are, and how the AI uses each input when building the plan. This section is the foundation — nothing in Sections 1 through 10 can be applied correctly without the client data defined here.
The intake flow is split into two stages. Stage 1 is free and acts as the lead magnet — it collects the minimum information required to deliver the maintenance calculator output and captures the client's email address. Stage 2 is the full paid intake — it collects all remaining information required to build the personalised 12 week plan. Every input from Stage 1 is carried forward into Stage 2 so the client never repeats themselves.
0.1 — Stage 1: Free Calculator Intake
Stage 1 is the free maintenance calculator — the lead magnet that sits at the top of the Plus 4 Performance funnel. It is available without payment and without account creation. Its purpose is to deliver genuine value to the client, capture their email address, and warm them into the purchase of the full plan.
Stage 1 collects five data points. These five inputs are sufficient to calculate BMR using the Mifflin St Jeor equation, apply the activity multiplier to produce TDEE, and calculate BMI. All three outputs are displayed to the client on the results page before any payment is requested.
The email address is captured before the results are shown — the client enters their email to receive their results. This is standard lead generation practice. The email is added to the Klaviyo list and the automated email sequence begins immediately regardless of whether the client proceeds to purchase.
Stage 1 Input Fields
Field 1 — First Name
Type: Text input
Required: Yes
Purpose: Personalisation throughout the results page and all subsequent email communications. Never used in calculations.
Validation: Letters only, minimum 2 characters, maximum 30 characters.
Field 2 — Email Address
Type: Email input
Required: Yes
Purpose: Lead capture. Email is collected before results are displayed. Added to Klaviyo immediately. Used for plan delivery, onboarding sequence and all future communications.
Validation: Standard email format validation. Duplicate emails accepted — system checks for existing contact and updates rather than creating a duplicate.
Placement: Shown after the calculator inputs are completed but before the results are displayed. The CTA is: Enter your email to receive your results.
Field 3 — Biological Sex
Type: Single select — two options
Options: Male / Female
Required: Yes
Purpose: Used in the Mifflin St Jeor BMR calculation. The male formula adds 5 and the female formula subtracts 161. Also used in plan generation — female clients receive at least one glute focused movement on every leg day by default. Male clients receive glute movements only when specifically requested.
Validation: Must select one option before proceeding.
Field 4 — Age
Type: Number input
Required: Yes
Range: 16 to 80 years
Purpose: Used in the Mifflin St Jeor BMR calculation. Age is multiplied by 5 and subtracted from the BMR formula.
Validation: Whole numbers only. Minimum 16 — clients under 16 are not supported by this product and a message is displayed directing them to seek age appropriate guidance. Maximum 80 — clients over 80 are shown a message advising them to consult a medical professional before beginning a training programme.
Field 5 — Height
Type: Number input with unit toggle
Required: Yes
Units: Centimetres (default) or feet and inches — client selects their preferred unit. All values converted to centimetres for calculation.
Range: 140cm to 220cm (approximately 4ft 7in to 7ft 3in)
Purpose: Used in the Mifflin St Jeor BMR calculation. Height in centimetres is multiplied by 6.25.
Validation: Numeric input only. Values outside the range trigger a validation message asking the client to check their entry.
Field 6 — Current Weight
Type: Number input with unit toggle
Required: Yes
Units: Kilograms (default) or pounds — client selects their preferred unit. All values converted to kilograms for calculation.
Range: 40kg to 250kg (approximately 88lbs to 551lbs)
Purpose: Used in the Mifflin St Jeor BMR calculation. Weight in kilograms is multiplied by 10. Also used to calculate BMI alongside height. Carried forward to Stage 2 as the starting weight for nutrition calculations.
Validation: Numeric input only. Decimal values accepted to one decimal place. Values outside the range trigger a validation message.
Field 7 — Activity Level
Type: Single select slider — five levels
Required: Yes
Options and multipliers are as follows:
Level 1 — Sedentary: Desk job, no exercise, mostly sitting. Multiplier 1.2.
Level 2 — Lightly active: Light walking, on feet at work, no structured exercise. Multiplier 1.375.
Level 3 — Moderately active: 1 to 3 workouts per week, moderately active day. Multiplier 1.55.
Level 4 — Very active: 3 to 5 workouts per week, active lifestyle. Multiplier 1.725.
Level 5 — Extremely active: Training daily or physical job on top of training. Multiplier 1.9.
Purpose: Multiplied by BMR to produce TDEE. The activity level selected here reflects the client's current lifestyle before the plan begins — not their intended training frequency on the plan. A note is displayed alongside the slider making this distinction clear.
Validation: Must select one level before proceeding.
Stage 1 Outputs
Once all Stage 1 fields are completed and the email is captured the following outputs are displayed on the results page:
BMR: Basal Metabolic Rate in kilocalories. Calculated using the Mifflin St Jeor equation. Displayed with a brief explanation of what BMR means.
TDEE: Total Daily Energy Expenditure in kilocalories. BMR multiplied by the activity level multiplier. Displayed as the number of calories the client needs to maintain their current weight. Explained as the starting point for their nutrition plan.
BMI: Body Mass Index. Calculated as weight in kilograms divided by height in metres squared. Displayed with the standard BMI category — underweight, healthy weight, overweight, obese. A note is included acknowledging that BMI is a limited metric and does not account for muscle mass — it is one data point not a complete picture.
Below the results a clear CTA is displayed: Get your full 12 week personalised plan — £19.99. Clicking this takes the client to the payment flow. All Stage 1 data is carried forward automatically.
0.2 — Stage 2: Full Plan Intake
Stage 2 is the full client intake completed after payment. It collects all remaining information the AI needs to build the personalised 12 week training and nutrition plan. Stage 1 data is pre-filled and confirmed at the top of Stage 2 — the client can correct any entries before proceeding.
Stage 2 inputs are organised into five groups: personal details confirmation, goal and timeline, training preferences, nutrition preferences, and health and injury information. The client moves through each group in sequence. The AI uses all Stage 2 inputs combined with Stage 1 data to generate the plan.
Every input in Stage 2 that has a default value will auto-populate that default if the client does not make a selection. Default values are chosen based on what the coaching philosophy establishes as the optimal starting point for the majority of clients. Where a default is applied it is always displayed to the client with a brief explanation of why — the client is never left with a value they did not knowingly choose.
Group 1 — Personal Details Confirmation
The first group of Stage 2 displays all Stage 1 inputs pre-filled and asks the client to confirm or correct them. This group does not add new fields — it validates the data before plan generation begins.
Displayed fields: First name, biological sex, age, height, current weight, activity level, email address.
Target weight: A new field added at this stage. The client enters their target weight in the same unit as their current weight. Used in the personal summary page of the plan and in setting the appropriate calorie deficit or surplus. Not used in the Mifflin St Jeor calculation — the plan is always built around current weight, not target weight.
Target weight validation: Must be a realistic target. If the client enters a target weight that implies more than 1kg loss per week over 12 weeks the system displays a message: This target is very ambitious for 12 weeks. Your plan will be built to maximise your results safely — the target shown is aspirational and the process will get you as close as possible. The plan proceeds regardless — no target weight is rejected outright.
Group 2 — Goal and Timeline
Field — Primary Goal
Type: Single select — three options
Options are as follows:
Fat loss: The client wants to lose body fat. The plan applies a calorie deficit, higher protein target, higher cardio volume and higher step goal. Training structure remains the same as muscle building.
Muscle building: The client wants to build muscle and increase size. The plan applies a calorie surplus, moderate protein target, lower cardio volume and lower step goal.
Maintenance and recomposition: The client wants to maintain their current weight while improving body composition — losing fat and gaining muscle simultaneously. The plan applies maintenance calories, moderate protein target and moderate cardio. Positioned as a transition phase or a long term sustainable approach.
Required: Yes — no default. The client must actively select their goal.
Purpose: The primary goal determines the entire nutrition structure — calorie target, macro split, training day vs rest day calories, cardio volume and step goal. It does not significantly change the training structure. See Sections 5, 6 and 7 for how each goal is implemented.
Field — Experience Level
Type: Single select — three options
Options are as follows:
Beginner: New to training or less than 6 months of consistent training history. May have been to the gym casually but does not yet have established movement patterns or a baseline of strength.
Intermediate: 6 months or more of consistent training. Understands the basics, can execute fundamental movements with reasonable form, has a baseline of strength.
Advanced: Multiple years of consistent structured training. Strong movement patterns across all fundamental exercises, developed mind muscle connection, history of progressive overload.
Returning after a break note: A tick box sits below the three options: I am returning to training after a break of 6 months or more. If ticked the client is automatically classified as intermediate regardless of which experience level they selected. A brief explanation is shown: Muscle memory means you will progress significantly faster than a true beginner — your plan starts at intermediate level.
Required: Yes — no default. The client must actively select their experience level.
Purpose: Experience level affects split selection, exercise complexity, rep ranges, exercise exclusions, progression expectations and the beginner specific content included in the plan. See Sections 1.6, 2.1, 2.5 and Section 4 for full detail.
Field — Plan Start Date
Type: Date picker
Required: No — defaults to today's date if left blank
Purpose: Used to populate the plan start and end dates on the personal summary page. Also used to time the email automation sequence correctly — onboarding emails, week 6 check-in and week 12 completion email are all triggered from this date.
Validation: Cannot be more than 30 days in the future. If the client selects a date more than 30 days ahead a message is displayed: Your plan will be ready immediately — you can start whenever you are ready. We recommend starting within the next 2 weeks to maintain momentum.
Group 3 — Training Preferences
Field — Training Days Per Week
Type: Single select — four options
Options: 3 days / 4 days / 5 days / 6 days
Default: 4 days — displayed with the note: 4 days per week is the recommended sweet spot for most people. Enough stimulus to drive results, enough recovery to sustain them.
Required: Yes — defaults to 4 days if no selection is made
Purpose: The primary driver of split selection alongside experience level. See Section 2.1 for the full split selection logic by training days and experience level.
Note: 2 days per week is not available as an option — the minimum programmed is 3 days as established in Section 1.3. If a client contacts support requesting a 2 day plan they are advised that 2 days does not provide sufficient stimulus for meaningful results and a 3 day plan is recommended instead.
Field — Session Length
Type: Single select — four options
Options: 45 minutes / 60 minutes / 90 minutes / 2 hours
Default: 60 minutes — displayed with the note: 60 minutes gives you enough time to train effectively without the session becoming a part-time job.
Required: Yes — defaults to 60 minutes if no selection is made
Purpose: Determines the number of exercises and sets per session. See Section 2.3 for the full volume structure by session length. Session length includes warm up time — the plan is built to fit within the selected time including the warm up.
Field — Training Schedule Preference
Type: Single select — two options
Options: Fixed schedule — same days every week / Rolling programme — follow sessions in sequence regardless of day
Default: Fixed schedule
Purpose: Determines how the plan is presented. Fixed schedule plans show specific days of the week — Monday Push, Wednesday Pull etc. Rolling plans show Day 1, Day 2, Day 3, Rest and repeat. PPL and higher day splits naturally lend themselves to rolling programmes — the system flags this to the client when a split that works better as rolling is selected with a fixed preference, and offers to switch.
Field — Preferred Training Days (Fixed Schedule Only)
Type: Multi-select — days of the week. Only shown when Fixed schedule is selected
Options: Monday / Tuesday / Wednesday / Thursday / Friday / Saturday / Sunday
Required: Only required when Fixed schedule is selected. Client selects the same number of days as their chosen training frequency.
Purpose: Determines which days of the week appear in the plan. The system checks spacing automatically — if the selected days violate the consecutive day rules established in Section 2.1 a warning is shown with a suggested alternative spacing.
Validation: The number of days selected must match the training frequency selected. If the client selects 4 training days they must select exactly 4 days of the week. If spacing is poor — for example 4 consecutive days — the system flags this and suggests a better arrangement but does not prevent the client from proceeding.
Field — Gym Type
Type: Single select — five options
Options are as follows:
Specialist gym: A serious lifting or powerlifting gym with extensive free weights, competition equipment and specialist machines. All equipment is auto-ticked. This gym type unlocks every exercise in the library.
Commercial gym: A standard commercial gym — Virgin, PureGym, Fitness First or similar. Standard machines, cables, free weights and cardio equipment. Specialist machines not auto-ticked — client ticks up anything their gym has.
Budget or basic gym: A budget gym or basic facility. Dumbbells, barbells, cables and standard resistance machines. Specialist and some commercial machines not auto-ticked.
Home gym: Training at home with equipment. Dumbbells and adjustable bench auto-ticked as a minimum. Client ticks up any additional equipment they have such as a barbell, rack or cables.
Bodyweight only: No equipment. Home training using bodyweight movements only. Exercise selection limited to bodyweight progressions as established in Section 3.4.
Required: Yes — no default. The client must select their gym type.
Purpose: Determines the equipment available for exercise selection. Directly filters the exercise library — equipment dependent exercises are only selected when the corresponding equipment is confirmed as available. See Section 4.2 Rule 7.
Field — Equipment Confirmation
Type: Multi-select tick list — auto-populated based on gym type selection. Shown for all gym types except Bodyweight only
The full equipment list with auto-tick logic by gym type is as follows:
Standard Equipment — ticked for Specialist, Commercial and Budget gyms:
Barbell and plates
Dumbbells — full rack
Flat bench
Incline bench
Squat rack
Cable machine — dual pulley
Leg press machine
Leg extension machine
Lying hamstring curl machine
Seated hamstring curl machine
Lat pulldown machine
Seated cable row machine
Chest supported row machine
Pec fly machine
Lateral raise machine
Adductor machine
Abductor machine
Standing calf raise machine
Seated calf raise machine
Pull up bar
Dip bars
Smith machine
Incline treadmill
Stationary bike
Specialist Equipment — ticked for Specialist gym only, unticked by default for all others:
Hack squat machine
Pendulum squat machine
MagGrip lat pulldown attachment
Cybex row machine
Hip thrust machine
T bar row machine
Home Gym Equipment — ticked for Home gym only:
Dumbbells — fixed or adjustable
Adjustable bench
Barbell and plates — tick if available
Squat rack — tick if available
Pull up bar — tick if available
Resistance bands — tick if available
Purpose: The confirmed equipment list is the definitive filter applied to the exercise library before plan generation. Only exercises with all required equipment confirmed are eligible for selection. If a piece of equipment is unticked the system selects the next highest priority alternative from the same muscle group that uses available equipment.
Client instruction: A note is shown above the equipment list: These boxes are pre-ticked based on your gym type. Untick anything your gym does not have. If you are unsure whether your gym has a piece of equipment it is better to untick it — the plan will substitute a suitable alternative.
Field — Split Preference
Type: Single select — optional. Only shown after training days and experience level are confirmed
Options: Recommended for me — let the system choose based on my days and experience level / I want to choose my own split
Default: Recommended for me
Purpose: If the client selects Recommended for me the system applies the split selection logic from Section 2.1 automatically and explains the recommendation. If the client selects I want to choose my own split a secondary selector appears showing the available splits for their chosen training days and experience level. The client selects from this filtered list — they cannot select a split that is inappropriate for their days or experience level.
Group 4 — Nutrition Preferences
Field — Meal Frequency
Type: Single select — four options
Options: 3 meals per day / 4 meals per day / 5 meals per day / 6 meal per day
Default: 5 meals per day — displayed with the note: 5 meals per day gives the best protein distribution for muscle growth and recovery.
Purpose: Determines how daily calorie and macro targets are divided across meals in the nutrition plan. See Section 7.7 for the full meal structure logic by meal frequency. If 2 meals per day is selected a note is displayed: 2 meals makes hitting your protein target very challenging. We recommend adding at least 1 protein shake between meals. The plan proceeds with 2 meals but the note is included in the plan document.
Field — Meal Plan Type
Type: Single select — two options
Options: Full 7 day meal plan — give me specific foods and portions for every meal / Macros only — give me my daily targets and I will choose my own foods
Default: Full 7 day meal plan
Purpose: Determines whether the plan includes a specific meal plan with food suggestions and a grocery list, or just the macro targets. Clients who select macros only do not receive a grocery list. See Section 7.7 and 7.9.
Field — Dietary Preferences and Restrictions
Type: Multi-select tick list
Options are as follows:
No restrictions — I eat everything
Vegetarian — no meat or fish
Vegan — no animal products
Dairy free
Gluten free
Halal
Kosher
Default: No restrictions selected — the client must actively tick any that apply.
Purpose: Filters the food library before meal plan generation. All ticked dietary restrictions remove the relevant foods from the available pool. The AI never suggests foods that conflict with the client's stated preferences. See Section 7.6 for full dietary adjustment logic.
Field — Foods You Will Not Eat
Type: Free text input
Required: No
Placeholder text: List any specific foods you dislike or will not eat — e.g. eggs, fish, broccoli
Purpose: Any foods listed here are removed from the food library for this client before meal plan generation. The AI substitutes alternatives with similar macro profiles. This is in addition to the dietary preference filters above and handles individual food dislikes that do not fall into a dietary category.
Field — Supplement Stack
Type: Single select — two options
Options: Recommend supplements for me based on my goal / I already have my supplements sorted — just show me what I need
Default: Recommend supplements for me
Purpose: Determines the supplement recommendations section of the plan. If Recommend supplements is selected the plan includes the full goal specific supplement stack from Section 6.4. If I already have my supplements sorted is selected the plan includes only the non-negotiables — protein powder and creatine monohydrate — with a note that the client can refer to Section 6.4 of the coaching bible for the full stack if needed.
Group 5 — Health and Injury Information
Group 5 is the most important group for plan safety. The information collected here directly affects exercise selection, exercise order, programme intensity and whether the plan proceeds at all. The client is shown the following message before this group: This section ensures your plan is safe and appropriate for your body. Be honest — the plan works better when we know your limitations.
Field — Injury or Physical Limitation
Type: Single select — two options with conditional expansion
Options: No injuries or limitations — I am good to go / I have an injury or physical limitation
Default: No injuries or limitations
Purpose: If No injuries or limitations is selected the plan proceeds with the standard exercise library. If I have an injury or physical limitation is selected the following fields expand.
Field — Injury Location (shown when injury is flagged)
Type: Multi-select tick list
Options are as follows:
Knee — one or both
Lower back
Shoulder — one or both
Hip — one or both
Wrist — one or both
Elbow — one or both
Ankle — one or both
Calf or Achilles
Other
Purpose: Identifies which areas of the body are affected. Used alongside the free text description to filter the exercise library and apply the injury protocols from Section 9. Multiple injury locations can be selected simultaneously.
Field — Injury Description (shown when injury is flagged)
Type: Free text input
Required: Yes — when an injury location is ticked
Placeholder text: Describe your injury and what it stops you doing in the gym. For example: I have a knee injury that means I cannot squat below parallel or do deep lunges. I can leg press and use the hamstring curl machine fine.
Purpose: The free text description carries more weight than the location tick in determining how the plan is modified. The AI reads both inputs and responds to the more conservative interpretation. If the description suggests greater severity than the location tick implies a warning is triggered. See Section 9.1 for the full injury processing logic.
Field — Injury Severity (shown when injury is flagged)
Type: Single select — three options
Options are as follows:
Mild: Occasional discomfort. I can train through it with some modifications.
Moderate: Regular pain during training. Some exercises are not possible and I need alternatives.
Severe: Significant pain or very limited movement. I am not sure if I should be training at all.
Purpose: Used alongside the location and description to determine the severity of plan modification required. If Severe is selected the system displays a message: Based on what you have described we strongly recommend getting medical clearance before starting this programme. Your plan will be built around your limitations but please consult a physiotherapist or doctor before beginning. The plan still proceeds — the client is not blocked — but the severity flag is carried into the plan generation prompt.
Field — Red Flag Check
Type: Single select — two options
Question displayed to client: Do you experience any of the following during exercise: chest pain or tightness, dizziness or loss of consciousness, severe shortness of breath disproportionate to effort, numbness or tingling in the arms or legs?
Options: No — none of the above / Yes — I experience one or more of these
Default: No
Purpose: If Yes is selected the plan is blocked from proceeding. The following message is displayed: The symptoms you have described require medical assessment before you begin any training programme. Please consult your doctor before purchasing or beginning a Plus 4 Performance plan. For your safety we are unable to proceed until you have medical clearance. A full refund is issued if payment has already been taken. This is a non-negotiable safety gate — no plan is generated for a client who flags red flag symptoms.
Field — Additional Notes
Type: Free text input
Required: No
Placeholder text: Anything else we should know before building your plan. For example: I train early morning before eating, I have limited access to a gym on weekends, I am a shift worker with an irregular schedule.
Purpose: A catch-all field for any information not captured by the structured inputs above. This text is passed directly into the plan generation prompt and the AI uses it to make any additional adjustments not covered by the standard input fields. Information provided here may affect session timing notes, meal timing recommendations, or plan presentation.

0.3 — Equipment Selection Logic
The equipment confirmation from Group 3 determines which exercises are available for plan generation. The logic operates through a two-step process. First the client selects their gym type from four options. Second the system auto-populates a tick list of available equipment based on that selection. The client reviews the list, unticks anything their gym does not have, and confirms. The confirmed list is then locked before plan generation begins.
The four gym types are as follows:
Tier 1 — Specialist Gym
A serious lifting or bodybuilding facility with plate loaded specialist machines and premium equipment brands. Examples include gyms equipped with Hammer Strength, Nautilus, Cybex, Nytram, Watson, Panatta, Atlantis, Precor or Life Fitness plate loaded ranges. If your gym has dedicated pendulum squat machines, belt squat machines, reverse hypers or glute ham raise stations it is a specialist gym.
All equipment is auto-ticked when this tier is selected. The full exercise library is unlocked including all specialist equipment dependent exercises.
Auto-ticked equipment for Specialist Gym — everything in the Full Commercial Gym list plus:
Hack squat machine — plate loaded
Pendulum squat machine
Hip thrust machine
T-bar row machine — dedicated or landmine
MagGrip lat pulldown attachment
Cybex row machine
Belt squat machine
Glute ham raise
Reverse hyper machine
Chest supported T-bar row machine
Tier 2 — Full Commercial Gym
A standard well-equipped commercial gym. Examples include PureGym, Virgin Active, Fitness First, JD Gyms, Everlast, The Gym Group, David Lloyd and similar chains. Has all standard machines, cables, free weights and cardio equipment. Specialist plate loaded machines are not assumed — client ticks up anything their specific gym has beyond the standard list.
Auto-ticked equipment for Full Commercial Gym:
Barbell and plates
Dumbbells — full rack
Flat bench
Incline bench
Squat rack
Cable machine — dual pulley
Leg press machine
Leg extension machine
Lying hamstring curl machine
Seated hamstring curl machine
Lat pulldown machine
Seated cable row machine
Chest supported row machine
Pec fly machine
Lateral raise machine
Adductor machine
Abductor machine
Standing calf raise machine
Seated calf raise machine
Pull up bar
Dip bars
Smith machine
Incline treadmill
Stationary bike
Not auto-ticked — client ticks if available:
Hack squat machine
Pendulum squat machine
Hip thrust machine
T-bar row machine
MagGrip lat pulldown attachment
Cybex row machine
Tier 3 — Home Gym
Training at home with personal equipment. Dumbbells and an adjustable bench are auto-ticked as the minimum assumed setup. A secondary checklist appears for the client to confirm any additional equipment they have.
Auto-ticked for Home Gym:
Dumbbells — fixed or adjustable
Adjustable bench
Secondary checklist — client ticks what they have:
Barbell and plates
Squat rack
Pull up bar
Resistance bands
Cables — if home cable setup exists
Exercise selection is filtered entirely to what the client confirms. If only dumbbells and bench are confirmed the plan uses only dumbbell exercises. Each additional piece of equipment unlocks additional exercises.
Tier 4 — Bodyweight Only
No equipment. Home training using bodyweight movements only. Exercise selection is limited to the bodyweight progressions established in Section 3.4.
Available exercises for bodyweight only clients:
Press ups and progressions
Bodyweight squats
Lunges
Step ups using a chair or step
Glute bridge
Tricep dips between chairs or a sofa
Pull ups — if a doorframe bar is available, client ticks this separately
Hanging leg raises — if pull up bar confirmed
Crunches
Leg raises
Decline sit ups using a sofa or step
A note is displayed to bodyweight only clients: Your plan will be built around what is available. For the best results access to at least a set of dumbbells is strongly recommended. A home gym tier upgrade is available at any time.
Equipment confirmation rules
When the gym type is selected the equipment list is auto-populated with the appropriate defaults. The client reviews and corrects the list — unticking anything their gym does not have or ticking up anything their gym does have that was not auto-ticked.
A note is shown above the tick list: These boxes are pre-ticked based on your gym type. Untick anything your gym does not have. If you are unsure whether your gym has a piece of equipment it is better to untick it — the plan will substitute a suitable alternative.
The confirmed equipment list is locked before plan generation begins. The AI reads this list as the definitive record of available equipment and never selects an exercise that requires equipment not on the confirmed list.
When an exercise is excluded due to unavailable equipment the system selects the next highest priority alternative from the same muscle group in the exercise library. The substitution always stays within the available equipment. If no suitable alternative exists for a muscle group the volume is redistributed to other exercises in the session that use confirmed equipment.
The equipment list does not change mid-plan. If a client gains access to new equipment — for example their gym installs a hack squat machine — this is handled at plan renewal in week 12 when Stage 2 is re-confirmed for the next plan cycle.
0.4 — How the AI Uses the Inputs
Every Stage 1 and Stage 2 input feeds into one or more elements of the plan. The table below summarises which inputs drive which plan components.
Inputs that drive training plan structure:
Training days — split selection, session spacing, total weekly volume
Session length — exercise count and set volume per session
Experience level — split complexity, exercise selection, rep ranges, exercise exclusions, progression expectations
Goal — cardio volume, step targets, rest day structure
Training schedule preference and preferred days — plan presentation format, day of week assignment
Split preference — training split used
Equipment confirmed — exercise library filtering
Injury location, description and severity — exercise substitutions, session order modifications, injury warnings in plan
Additional notes — any session timing or lifestyle adjustments
Inputs that drive nutrition plan structure:
Current weight — protein targets, calorie calculations
Target weight — calorie deficit or surplus sizing, personal summary page
Biological sex — BMR calculation, glute movement inclusion on leg day
Age — BMR calculation
Height — BMR calculation, BMI
Activity level — TDEE calculation
Goal — calorie target, macro split, training day vs rest day calories, cardio prescription
Meal frequency — how daily targets are divided across meals
Meal plan type — full meal plan with grocery list or macros only
Dietary preferences — food library filtering
Foods will not eat — additional food library filtering
Supplement stack preference — supplement recommendations section
Inputs that drive plan presentation:
First name — personalisation throughout the plan document
Goal — plan title on the personal summary page
Plan start date — start and end dates on the personal summary page, email sequence timing
Experience level — beginner specific content included or excluded
Training schedule preference — fixed day labels vs rolling day labels
0.5 — Input Validation Rules and Edge Cases
The following validation rules and edge case handling apply across both Stage 1 and Stage 2.
Age Edge Cases
Client age under 16 — plan blocked. Message displayed: This programme is designed for adults aged 16 and over. Please seek age appropriate guidance from a qualified coach.
Client age over 80 — plan proceeds with a warning. Message displayed: Please consult your doctor before beginning this or any training programme. Your plan will be built conservatively with your health and safety as the priority.
Weight and Height Edge Cases
BMI under 17.5 — plan proceeds with a warning. Message displayed: Your BMI suggests you may be significantly underweight. Please consult a doctor before beginning this programme. A muscle building goal is strongly recommended.
BMI over 40 — plan proceeds with a warning. Message displayed: Your plan will be built conservatively. We recommend consulting your doctor before beginning. Fat loss goal is applied by default if not already selected.
Target weight implies more than 1kg loss per week — warning displayed but plan proceeds. See Group 1 above.
Training Days and Split Edge Cases
Client selects 6 training days and is a beginner — system overrides to 4 days and displays a message: 6 training days is not recommended for beginners. Your plan has been set to 4 days to allow adequate recovery while you build your foundation. The client can override this if they insist but the message remains in the plan.
Client selects fixed schedule with poor day spacing — warning displayed with suggested alternative. Plan proceeds with client's chosen days if they confirm.
Client selects a split that conflicts with their experience level — system redirects to the appropriate split and explains why. For example a beginner selecting a 5 day PPL is redirected to Full Body x3 plus Upper plus Lower as the appropriate 5 day option for their level.
Injury Edge Cases
Multiple simultaneous injuries — all injury locations are processed independently and the cumulative effect on the exercise library is assessed. See Section 9.9 for multiple injury handling.
Injury description suggests severity greater than the tick box selection — the more conservative interpretation is always applied. A note is added to the plan: Based on your description we have applied conservative modifications. Please consult a physiotherapist if you are unsure whether training is appropriate.
Red flag symptoms flagged — plan blocked, refund issued, message displayed. See Field — Red Flag Check above.
Severe injury flagged — plan proceeds with significant modifications and a strong recommendation to seek medical clearance before starting.
Nutrition Edge Cases
Calculated calorie target falls below the minimum floor — 1,500 calories for males, 1,200 calories for females — the floor is applied and the deficit is reduced accordingly. A note is included in the plan: Your calculated deficit has been adjusted to protect muscle mass and ensure adequate nutrition. For faster results focus on increasing your step count and cardio before reducing calories further.
Vegan client — protein target increased to 2.4g per kg for fat loss and 2.2g per kg for muscle building to compensate for lower plant protein quality. Vitamin B12 supplement added to recommendations automatically.
2 meals per day selected — protein shake recommended between meals. Note included in plan.
Client flags both vegetarian and foods including all common plant proteins — system flags a conflict and asks the client to confirm their preferences before proceeding.
General Validation Rules
All required fields must be completed before the plan generates — the system does not proceed with missing required inputs.
All defaults are displayed to the client before plan generation — no default is applied silently.
The client can return to Stage 2 and amend inputs before generating the plan — but once the plan is generated the inputs are locked. Changes require a new plan to be generated which is handled through the subscription upgrade or support contact.
All Stage 2 data is stored against the client's account in Supabase and is used to pre-populate Stage 2 at plan renewal in week 12.
Section 1 — Your Training Philosophy This is your beliefs about training. How you think about progress, consistency, effort. What matters to you as a coach. The AI needs to understand your voice and values before it builds anything.
Cover:
What results do you believe are achievable in 12 weeks realistically
Your view on progressive overload — how aggressive, how often
How you feel about rest and recovery
Your stance on cardio alongside weight training
What you think about training frequency for different goals
How you approach beginners vs experienced lifters differently
What consistent means to you and what someone needs to do to get results
1.1 - What's realistic in 12 weeks — the honest answer
Fat loss 0.5–1lb per week is the gold standard for sustainable fat loss that preserves muscle. So over 12 weeks a consistent person can expect 6–12lbs lost. Heavier people and complete beginners tend to lose faster early on. Lighter, more experienced people slower. You never guarantee a number — you guarantee the process.
Muscle building Beginners can gain 1–2lbs of actual muscle per month in optimal conditions — so 3–6lbs in 12 weeks is realistic and that's genuinely visible. Intermediates are looking at half that. Advanced lifters even less. But everyone gets stronger, and strength is visible even when the scale doesn't move much.
The honest sell 12 weeks isn't a transformation — it's a foundation. The people who get dramatic results are the ones who keep going past 12 weeks. Your job is to get them results good enough in 12 weeks that they want to continue. That's why the subscription exists.
1.2 - Your view on progressive overload — how aggressive, how often
Progressive overload is the non-negotiable foundation of every plan. Week 1 is used to establish baseline weights — finding the right load for each exercise within the given rep range. From week 2 onwards, when a client hits the top of their given rep range, weight goes up the following week — no exceptions. Increases are small and deliberate: 1.25–2.5kg per side to ensure form is never compromised chasing numbers. Clients follow the same exercise order, same split, same structure each week so progression is always measurable against the previous session. Sets increase as a secondary progression tool — primarily in weeks 5–8 — to add training volume without overloading the joints. Beginners can expect to progress most weeks. Intermediate and advanced lifters progress week to week with smaller jumps. All working sets are performed with 1–2 reps in reserve — close enough to failure to drive adaptation, far enough to protect against injury and maintain consistency across the full 12 weeks. On final sets of low-risk isolation exercises, clients may push closer to failure.
1.3 - How you feel about rest and recovery
Rest and recovery is not optional — it's where adaptation happens. Training days break the body down, rest days build it back stronger. Plans are structured around the client's chosen training frequency: 3, 4 or 5 days per week. The recommended sweet spot for a busy person is 4 days. 3 days works well if sessions are focused and effort is high. 2 days or fewer is not enough stimulus to drive meaningful change and will not be programmed. If a client does not select a training frequency, 4 days is assigned by default with an explanation of why. Rest days are active — a target step count is maintained and light movement encouraged. Sitting completely still on rest days increases soreness and slows recovery. For clients wanting to train 6 days, the sixth day is cardio only. A seventh day is strongly discouraged but if chosen, must be genuinely low intensity. On sleep: 7–9 hours is the target. Sleep is when muscle is built and fat is metabolised. Consistently poor sleep will undermine results regardless of training and nutrition quality. Clients are advised to treat sleep as part of the programme, not separate from it. On soreness: general muscle achiness is normal and training through it is fine — the warmup resolves it. Sharp, localised or worsening pain is a signal to rest and if persistent, seek professional advice. Clients are coached to understand the difference between discomfort and pain. Recovery tools recommended: foam rolling for 5–10 minutes post session, and walking as the primary active recovery method.
1.4 - Your stance on cardio alongside weight training
Cardio is non-negotiable regardless of goal. Whether a client is building muscle or losing fat, cardiovascular health matters and cardio supports both. It is never optional. Cardio is not programmed as separate dedicated sessions — it lives inside the weight training days, tagged onto the end of every session as 10–15 minutes of low intensity work. This keeps the programme clean and prevents total training days exceeding 5. Preferred post-session cardio methods in order: incline treadmill walk, cycling, stairs. On top of this, a daily step goal runs throughout the entire 12 weeks as background activity — this is separate from structured cardio and treated as a daily habit not a session. For fat loss clients: 10–15 mins post-session cardio every weight day, daily step goal of 8,000–10,000 steps, and one optional dedicated cardio session on a rest day if fat loss needs accelerating — this is the only circumstance where a dedicated cardio day is added, bringing the maximum structured days to 5. For muscle building clients: 10–15 mins post-session cardio every weight day, daily step goal of 7,000–8,000 steps, no dedicated cardio days — preserving the calorie surplus needed for growth. HIIT is a last resort — reserved only for clients with severely limited time. For all other clients steady state is prescribed. HIIT demands recovery just like a weights session and the recovery cost is too high alongside a structured lifting programme.
1.5 - What you think about training frequency for different goals
Training frequency does not change significantly based on goal — whether cutting, bulking or maintaining, the training stimulus must remain consistent to preserve or build muscle. What changes with goal is nutrition and cardio, not programme structure. Every plan is built around hitting each muscle group a minimum of twice per week — this is non-negotiable regardless of training days or experience level. Split selection is driven by training days and experience level as follows: 3 days — full body sessions for all experience levels, the frequency of full body training ensures each muscle is hit multiple times per week within a lower volume structure. 4 days — Upper Lower Upper Lower for intermediate and advanced clients, full body for beginners. 5 days — PPL plus Upper Lower, or PPL plus Push plus Posterior chain for intermediate and advanced clients. 6 days — PPL twice per week or Arnold Split for advanced clients only. Beginners are always directed toward full body or upper lower splits regardless of how many days they select — movement pattern practice and compound lift frequency matters more than split complexity at this stage. Isolation work is minimal for beginners and increases with experience level. Preferred splits in order: PPL, Upper Lower, Arnold Split, Chest and Back plus Shoulders and Arms. Clients may be offered the option to choose or change their split within the plan but the default recommendation is always made based on their days and experience level with a clear explanation of why.
1.6 - How you approach beginners vs experienced lifters differently
Beginners and experienced lifters require fundamentally different approaches. With beginners the priority order is always: form before load, consistency before intensity, basics before complexity. A beginner with perfect form and light weight will always outperform a beginner lifting heavy with poor technique — and will stay injury free doing it. Exercise selection for beginners is built around the five fundamental movement patterns: squat, hinge, push, pull and carry. Compound movements form the backbone of every beginner session with isolation work included to support muscle development and body awareness — the balance shifts toward more isolation work as experience increases. The plan never prescribes specific weights — clients are given rep ranges and coached to find the right load for themselves within that range. Week 1 is used to find those numbers. As experience increases, isolation volume and exercise complexity increases accordingly. Beginners progress faster than any other group — their nervous system adapts rapidly and weight can increase almost every session in the early weeks. Week 1 establishes baseline and form. Weeks 2–4 expect frequent progression. Weeks 5–8 weekly progression. Weeks 9–12 week to week with increasing focus on form quality as loads get heavier. Every beginner plan includes: a movement primer explaining the key patterns and why they matter, a first session guide removing gym anxiety, a form checklist for every major exercise, gym etiquette guidance, and video links for every exercise in the plan. A disclaimer is built into every beginner plan acknowledging that self-assessed experience levels vary — if movements feel unfamiliar, drop the weight and prioritise form. If week 1 feels too easy, progress faster. The video library self-corrects form without requiring direct coaching. Common beginner mistakes the plan actively addresses: poor form chasing heavy weight, inconsistent effort, skipping warm ups, chasing soreness as a measure of success, comparing themselves to advanced lifters, neglecting sleep and nutrition, and programme hopping. The plan keeps things simple, progressive and sustainable — complexity is the enemy of consistency for a beginner. The specific exercise library is curated by the coach — only approved exercises are used in plan generation.
1.7 - What consistent means to you and what someone needs to do to get results
Consistency is the single most important factor in getting results — more important than the perfect programme, the perfect diet, or the perfect split. Consistency means showing up when you don't feel like it and training to your maximum capacity when you do. Motivation is temporary and unreliable. Discipline is what gets you there on a Tuesday evening when you're tired and stressed. The people who get results are the ones who train on the bad days, don't let one missed session become two, and treat sleep and nutrition with the same seriousness as training. On missed sessions: missing one session changes nothing. Missing ten changes everything. The plan includes light check-in prompts to identify when someone is struggling and whether anything can be adjusted to bring them back on track. The minimum threshold for results is training with 1–2 reps in reserve on every working set, hitting nutrition targets 80% of the time, and maintaining sleep quality. This is not about perfection — it is about doing enough of the right things often enough. The 80/20 principle applies: hit 80% of sessions, hit nutrition targets 80% of the time, and results will follow. On time: every plan is built around the client's available schedule. A 3 day plan requires a minimum of 2.25 hours of training per week based on 45 minute sessions — but sessions may run longer depending on the client's chosen session length. Time is rarely the real barrier — priority is. The plan is designed to remove every other obstacle so that showing up is the only thing required. Habit beats motivation every time. Training at the same time every day, having kit ready the night before, and knowing exactly what to do when you walk in removes the decision entirely. The plan eliminates the guesswork — the client's only job is to show up and execute. Discipline over motivation, every single time.
Section 2 — Programme Structure Rules
This tells the AI exactly how to build a training week. Be specific.

2.1 — How you structure a 3, 4, 5 and 6 day split
Programme structure is determined first by training days selected and second by experience level. Two days per week is not programmed — the minimum is three. Day spacing is as important as split selection — sessions are never scheduled more than three consecutive days in a row regardless of split or experience level. After any three consecutive training days a minimum of one full rest day is required. The plan auto-spaces sessions to respect this rule and flags poor spacing choices with a suggested alternative.
Before the plan is built the client is asked whether they prefer a fixed weekly schedule — same days every week — or a rolling programme followed in sequence regardless of the day. This determines how the plan is presented. Fixed plans show specific days of the week. Rolling plans show Day 1, Day 2, Day 3, Rest and repeat. PPL and other higher day splits naturally lend themselves to rolling programmes but the choice is always the client's.
Split recommendations by training days are as follows:
Three days — Full Body x3 or Upper Lower Upper. Full Body x3 is the default for beginners. Upper Lower Upper is recommended for intermediates and above. Ideal spacing: Monday, Wednesday, Friday or Tuesday, Thursday, Saturday. Never three consecutive days.
Four days — Upper Lower Upper Lower as the primary recommendation for all experience levels. PPL plus Upper as a strong alternative for intermediates wanting more upper body frequency. Full Body x4 is available for true beginners who have never trained before and benefit from movement pattern repetition — this is rarely the best option and better alternatives exist at 4 days but is kept as an option for complete novices. Ideal spacing: Monday, Tuesday, Thursday, Friday — two on, one off, two on, two off. Never more than two consecutive days.
Five days — PPL plus Upper Lower as the primary recommendation. PPL plus Chest and Back plus Shoulders and Arms for intermediates wanting a more hypertrophy focused approach. PPL plus Push plus Posterior for clients with a weak posterior chain. ULULX for advanced clients wanting high frequency. Full Body x3 plus Upper plus Lower for beginners transitioning to splits. Ideal spacing: Monday, Tuesday, Thursday, Friday, Saturday. Never three consecutive heavy days — if three days run consecutively the middle session should be the lightest of the week.
Six days — PPL twice per week as the primary recommendation, hitting every muscle group twice. Arnold Split — Chest and Back, Shoulders and Arms, Legs repeated twice — for clients focused on hypertrophy. ULULUL for advanced clients only. Some six day splits are naturally rolling — this is flagged to the client and their preference for fixed or rolling is respected. One full rest day is always programmed — never seven consecutive days. The rest day ideally falls after the most demanding sessions of the week.
All splits can be interchanged and clients may be offered alternatives within their day range. The splits listed are foundations and defaults — not rigid rules. The priority in all cases is that every muscle group is hit a minimum of twice per week and that spacing allows adequate recovery between sessions targeting the same muscle groups.
2.2 — Which muscle groups pair together and why
Muscle group pairing follows the split structure selected but the principles below apply across all variations.
Push days train chest, shoulders and triceps in that order. Chest is the primary mover and is trained first while fresh. Shoulders follow as a secondary push muscle. Triceps are trained last as they are already pre-fatigued from pressing movements and respond well to isolation work at the end of the session. All shoulder work sits on push days, upper days or dedicated shoulder sessions — never on pull days.
Pull days train back and biceps. Back is broken down into upper back, lats and rear delts depending on exercise selection. Rear delts always sit on pull days — never push — to keep shoulder joint balance and avoid overloading the front of the shoulder. Bicep isolation work finishes every pull session after all back work is complete.
Leg days follow two structures depending on frequency. If one leg day per week is programmed it is a full leg session covering quads, hamstrings, glutes and calves equally. If two leg days per week are programmed one is quad focused and one is hamstring and posterior chain focused — this allows greater volume and specificity for each muscle group without excessive session length.
Upper days have a deliberate alternating focus. The first upper session of the week leads with chest — chest is trained first, back second. The second upper session of the week leads with back — back is trained first, chest second. This ensures neither push nor pull is consistently deprioritised across the training week and both receive equal stimulus over time.
Lower days train the full lower body. Core work is also included on lower days as a natural fit — the lower body session already taxes the posterior chain and core, making it logical to finish with direct core work.
Core is programmed two to three times per week for all clients regardless of goal or experience level. Core work consists of three exercises for two to three sets each — chosen from leg raises, crunches, decline sit ups and standard sit ups. Core is always trained at the end of the session taking five to ten minutes maximum. It never comes before compound work and never interferes with the main session.
Arm isolation follows the split structure. On PPL splits tricep isolation finishes every push session and bicep isolation finishes every pull session — arms are hit twice per week indirectly through compounds and directly through isolation without needing a dedicated arm day. On ULUL splits tricep isolation is added to the end of upper sessions and bicep isolation is added to the end of lower sessions — this spreads arm work across the week naturally and gives lower days a useful upper body finisher. A dedicated arms day is not programmed in standard splits.
Supersets are not used as a primary training method for large muscle groups — straight sets are the default for all compound and main isolation work. Supersets may be used for arm isolation work at the end of sessions where time is limited and as an optional variation for advanced clients.
The Chest and Back pairing as a dedicated session day is used exclusively within the five day PPL plus Chest and Back plus Shoulders and Arms split. It does not appear in any other split structure. In this context chest and back are trained in straight sets — not supersets — with chest leading the session.
2.3 — How session length affects exercise volume
Session length is selected by the client at the input stage with four options: 45 minutes, 60 minutes, 90 minutes or 2 hours. The number of exercises and sets programmed is determined by this selection. The minimum session length is 45 minutes — 30 minutes is not sufficient to deliver meaningful volume across a full muscle group session. All time slots include warm up within the session time.
The standard working set structure across all session lengths and all experience levels is as follows: one heavy working set at the top of the prescribed rep range, followed by one back off set at 10-20% lighter weight for a slightly higher rep range — typically 6-8 reps on the heavy set and 10-12 reps on the back off set. This structure drives both progressive overload through the heavy set and metabolic volume through the back off set. It is efficient, joint friendly and appropriate for all experience levels.
Warm up sets are not counted as working sets. For the first exercise targeting each muscle group the client performs as many warm up sets as needed to feel ready — there is no fixed number. For subsequent exercises targeting the same muscle group one warm up set is performed if needed and skipped entirely if the muscle already feels warm and ready. This keeps session time efficient without compromising joint safety.
Drop sets and rest pause sets are used on the back off set only — not the heavy set. They are interchangeable techniques used to maximise output when time is limited or to push additional stimulus at the end of a set. A maximum of one exercise per muscle group uses a drop set or rest pause technique per session. These are not used on primary compound movements — they are reserved for isolation work or secondary compound movements.
Rest periods are as follows: 3-5 minutes between sets on compound movements, 90 seconds between sets on isolation work. When time is limited rest periods on isolation work can be reduced to 60 seconds. Rest periods on compound movements are never shortened significantly — inadequate rest between heavy compound sets compromises performance and increases injury risk.
Exercise volume by session length is structured as follows. The push day is used as the reference model — the same logic applies proportionally to pull, legs and upper lower sessions:
Session length is selected by the client at the input stage with five options: 45 minutes, 60 minutes, 75 minutes, 90 minutes or 120 minutes. The number of exercises and sets programmed is determined by this selection. All time slots include warm up within the session time. The exercise counts below are based on real world gym conditions — they account for warm up, transitions between exercises, waiting for equipment and general session flow. They are deliberately conservative. A plan that fits comfortably within the time is always better than one that overruns and gets cut short.
45 minutes — 4 to 5 exercises
This is a tight session. After a 5 minute general warm up and exercise specific warm up sets the working time is approximately 35 minutes. At proper rest periods — 2 to 3 minutes on compounds, 90 seconds on isolation — only 4 to 5 exercises can be completed properly. Core work is limited to 1 to 2 exercises at the end if time allows. No post session cardio is programmed for 45 minute sessions — the session itself is the priority. This session length suits clients with genuinely limited time. Primary exercises only — no secondary or tertiary movements.
60 minutes — 5 to 6 exercises
The most common and practical session length. After warm up the working time is approximately 48 minutes. This allows 5 to 6 exercises with proper rest periods and 2 to 3 core exercises at the end. Post session cardio is programmed at 10 minutes for fat loss clients. This is the recommended default for most clients. Primary and secondary exercises available.
75 minutes — 6 to 7 exercises
A comfortable session length that gives slightly more breathing room than 60 minutes without significantly extending the gym visit. After warm up the working time is approximately 63 minutes. Allows 6 to 7 exercises with full rest periods and a complete core section. Post session cardio at 10 to 15 minutes fits comfortably within the total time. All priority levels available.
90 minutes — 7 to 8 exercises
A full session for intermediate and advanced clients who train efficiently. After warm up the working time is approximately 78 minutes. Allows 7 to 8 exercises with full rest periods, a complete core section and post session cardio. Assumes the client is focused and not spending excessive time between exercises on their phone. All priority levels available. A note is included in plans at this session length: To complete this session in 90 minutes keep rest periods disciplined — 3 minutes maximum on compounds, 90 seconds on isolation.
120 minutes — 9 to 10 exercises
High volume training for advanced clients only. After warm up the working time is approximately 105 minutes. Allows 9 to 10 exercises with full rest periods, a complete core section and post session cardio. This session length assumes good gym access — if key machines are consistently busy this session will overrun. A note is included in all 120 minute plans: This is high volume training. Nutrition and recovery must match the workload — if you are not eating and sleeping to support this volume the session length should be reduced. Advanced clients only.
Important note on all session lengths
These exercise counts are maximums not targets. A well executed 5 exercise session beats a rushed 8 exercise session every time. If the client is training at peak intensity with full rest periods they should be finishing sessions feeling like they have worked hard — not feeling like they could have done twice as much. The counts above reflect this standard.
The same volume logic applies to all other session types. Pull sessions follow the same structure with back taking the primary volume allocation and biceps finishing the session. Leg sessions allocate the majority of volume to the primary focus — quads or hamstrings depending on whether it is a quad focused or posterior focused day — with the secondary muscle group receiving supporting volume. Upper sessions balance push and pull volume equally with the leading muscle group receiving one additional exercise.
Cool down is optional and not formally programmed. Clients are advised that stretching the muscles trained for 2 to 3 minutes post session aids recovery and reduces soreness but it is not a requirement.
2.4 — How a fat loss programme differs structurally from a muscle building one
The structural difference between a fat loss and muscle building programme is minimal — nutrition is the primary driver of body composition change, not the training structure. The training stimulus required to build muscle and to retain muscle during a calorie deficit is almost identical. Heavy compound movements, progressive overload and high training intensity are non-negotiable regardless of goal. The most common mistake during a fat loss phase is reducing weight and increasing reps — this signals the body to drop muscle mass. Heavy training during a cut tells the body to preserve muscle and burn fat instead. Rep ranges do not change between goals. The same rep ranges are prescribed for fat loss, muscle building and maintenance. Rest periods remain standard across goals — 3 to 5 minutes on compounds, 90 seconds on isolation. The only exception is when a client in a calorie deficit is genuinely struggling to recover between sets — in this case rest periods extend slightly rather than rep ranges or weights being reduced. Exercise selection is largely consistent across goals with one important practical exception. High demand exercises such as conventional deadlifts and heavy barbell squats require significant energy and recovery. In a large calorie deficit the client may lack the fuel to perform these safely or recover from them adequately. These movements are not removed but are monitored — clients in an aggressive deficit are offered modified alternatives such as Romanian deadlifts, trap bar deadlifts or leg press as substitutions if performance or recovery is compromised. Cardio volume is the primary structural difference between goals. Fat loss clients have higher cardio output — more post session cardio, higher step targets, and the option of one additional cardio session on a rest day if needed. Muscle building clients keep cardio low to preserve the calorie surplus required for growth. Session intensity is the same across all goals — there is no such thing as a fat loss intensity or a muscle building intensity. Every client trains as hard as their recovery allows regardless of goal. Maintenance programmes are structurally identical to muscle building programmes with moderate cardio. Maintenance is positioned in the plan as a transition phase and an opportunity for body recomposition — the client is advised that losing fat and gaining muscle simultaneously is achievable at maintenance calories, particularly for beginners and those returning after a break. The scale may not move during maintenance but body composition can change significantly.
2.5 — How a beginner programme differs from intermediate and advanced
Experience level is one of the primary inputs that shapes the entire programme structure. It affects split selection, exercise complexity, rep ranges, isolation volume, phase progression and exercise exclusions. The three levels are defined as follows:
Beginner — someone who is new to training or has minimal consistent training history. They may have been to the gym casually but do not yet have established movement patterns, body awareness or a baseline of strength. Beginner status also applies to anyone returning after a break of six months or more — they are treated as intermediate, never beginner, as muscle memory accelerates their return significantly. Someone returning after six months or more is classified as intermediate and progresses more quickly than a true beginner.
Intermediate — someone who knows the exercises, understands the basics, has trained consistently for roughly six months or more and can execute fundamental movements with reasonable form. They understand progressive overload, have a baseline of strength and are ready for more complex splits and higher volume.
Advanced — someone with multiple years of consistent training, strong movement patterns across all fundamental exercises, a well developed mind muscle connection and a history of structured progressive overload. They require more sophisticated programming, higher volume and more specialised techniques to continue progressing.
Structural differences by experience level are as follows:
Split complexity increases with experience. Beginners are assigned simpler splits — full body or upper lower. Intermediates access PPL and upper lower variations. Advanced lifters access all splits including Arnold and high frequency variations. This is covered in full in section 2.1.
Session length and exercise count increases with experience. Beginners typically train for 45 to 60 minutes with fewer exercises per session. Intermediates train for 60 to 90 minutes. Advanced lifters may train for 90 minutes to 2 hours with higher exercise counts. This is not fixed — it is guided by the client's selected session length — but experience level informs the recommendation.
Exercise complexity increases with experience. Beginners are given movements that are lower risk, easier to learn and forgiving of minor form errors. The following movements are excluded for beginners regardless of what they request: conventional barbell deadlift replaced with Romanian deadlift or trap bar deadlift, barbell back squat replaced with goblet squat or leg press until form is established, barbell overhead press replaced with dumbbell overhead press, barbell bent over row replaced with seated cable row or dumbbell row, and behind the neck press or pull which is excluded entirely for all experience levels. As experience increases exercise complexity increases and barbell movements are introduced progressively.
Rep ranges start slightly higher for beginners. The lowest rep range programmed for a beginner is 8 — for example 8-10 or 8-12 — to encourage learning movement patterns with manageable weight and reduce injury risk. Intermediates and advanced lifters access the full rep range spectrum including 6-8 for strength focused work. Rep ranges do not change dramatically between levels but beginners never go below 8 reps on any exercise.
Isolation volume is approximately 50/50 compound to isolation across all experience levels. The specific exercise selection within that balance is curated by the coach and will be defined in the exercise library. More complex and varied isolation movements are introduced as experience increases.
Phase progression across the 12 weeks differs by experience level. For beginners phase progression is more aggressive — bigger jumps in volume and intensity between the three 4 week phases as their body adapts rapidly. For intermediate and advanced lifters phase progression is more subtle — smaller volume jumps with increasing use of intensity techniques such as drop sets and rest pause sets in later phases. Weekly progression is handled automatically through the rep range and progressive overload system established in section 1.2 — when the top of the rep range is hit consistently weight increases the following week regardless of experience level.
Someone returning after a break of six months or more is classified as intermediate. They are never returned to beginner status — muscle memory means they will regain strength and movement quality significantly faster than a true beginner. Their programme starts at intermediate level but with a note that week 1 should be used to re-establish movement patterns and baseline weights before progressing.
2.6 — Preferred rep ranges for each goal
Rep ranges are fixed for the entire 12 weeks regardless of goal or experience level. Progressive overload is driven exclusively by weight increases week on week — not by changing rep ranges. Phase progression is driven by volume increases through additional sets in phases 2 and 3. Rep ranges do not change between phases.
The science supports training anywhere between 5 and 30 reps for hypertrophy — all rep ranges can build muscle when taken close to failure. The ranges below are selected for their balance of mechanical tension, muscle damage and metabolic stress across a full session.
Standard rep ranges across all goals are as follows:
Heavy working set — 6-8 reps for compounds, 8-10 reps for isolation. This is the primary progressive overload set. When the top of the range is hit consistently weight increases the following week.
Back off set — 8-12 reps for compounds, 10-15 reps for isolation at 10-20% lighter than the heavy set. This drives additional volume and metabolic stress without taxing the joints.
3rd Set can be implemented
Rep ranges by goal:
Muscle building — heavy set 6-8 compounds, 8-10 isolation. Back off set 8-12 compounds, 10-15 isolation. Focus is on progressive overload and mechanical tension.
Fat loss — identical rep ranges to muscle building. The training stimulus for muscle retention during a deficit is the same as for muscle growth. Rep ranges never increase for fat loss.
Maintenance — heavy set 8-10, back off set 10-12. Sits in the middle ground supporting recomposition — losing fat and gaining muscle simultaneously — without pushing maximum strength or maximum volume.
Beginners — the lowest rep range programmed is 8. No beginner works below 8 reps on any exercise. Heavy set 8-10, back off set 10-12 or 12-15. As form and confidence develops rep ranges shift toward the standard ranges above.
High rep finishers — used sparingly on isolation exercises only at the end of a session to fully fatigue the muscle. Rep range 15-20 maximum. Never used on compound movements. Programmed occasionally for intermediate and advanced lifters — not a standard feature of every session. Used as a tool not a default.
Rep ranges are always prescribed as ranges not exact numbers — for example 6-8, 8-10, 10-12, 12-15 or variations such as 6-10 and 8-12. The client selects a weight that challenges them within that range. Hitting the top of the range triggers a weight increase the following week.
Strength specific programming such as 5x5 is not included in the current product. It operates on different principles — lower rep ranges, longer rest periods and different periodisation — and will be considered as a future addition to the product range.
2.7 — Preferred rest periods for each goal
Rest periods are largely fixed across all goals — fat loss, muscle building and maintenance follow the same rest period structure. The training stimulus does not change between goals and neither does the recovery requirement between sets. Rest periods do not change across the 12 weeks.
Rest periods are feel based for intermediate and advanced lifters. The question is simple — are you ready to nail the next set 100%? If yes, go. If not, wait. This produces better performance than rigid timed rest because readiness varies day to day based on sleep, nutrition, stress and accumulated fatigue. For beginners a time guideline is provided as a starting point — beginners are encouraged to adjust based on feel as they gain experience and body awareness.
Rest period guidelines by exercise type are as follows:
Heavy compound movements — squat, deadlift, bench press, barbell row — 3 to 5 minutes feel based. Minimum 2 to 3 minutes when time is limited. These movements tax the central nervous system significantly and require full recovery before the next set to maintain performance and safety.
Light compound movements — overhead press, dumbbell press, Romanian deadlift — 2 to 3 minutes feel based. Minimum 90 seconds when time is limited.
Large isolation movements — leg extension, leg curl, cable row, chest fly — 90 seconds standard. 60 seconds when time is limited.
Small isolation movements — bicep curls, lateral raises, tricep pushdowns, face pulls — 60 to 90 seconds standard. 45 to 60 seconds when time is limited.
Core work — 45 to 60 seconds between sets.
Rest between the heavy working set and the back off set follows the same rest period as the exercise type — full rest is taken between the heavy set and the back off set before moving to the next exercise. The sequence for every exercise is: heavy set, full rest, back off set, full rest, next exercise.
Drop sets require no additional rest between the drop — weight is reduced immediately and the set continues without stopping. Full rest is taken after the drop set is complete before moving to the next exercise.
Rest pause sets are performed closer to failure than standard working sets — 0 to 1 reps in reserve rather than the standard 1 to 2. The structure is: perform set to 0-1 RIR, rest for 10 full breaths approximately 20 to 30 seconds, continue for additional reps to 0-1 RIR. This counts as one extended set. Full rest is taken after the rest pause set is complete before moving to the next exercise.
A note is included in all plans explaining the feel based approach to rest — clients are coached to understand that rushing rest periods compromises performance and results, and that taking the time needed to recover fully between sets is not laziness but smart training.
2.8 — How you use compound vs isolation exercises and in what order
Exercise order within every session follows a clear set of principles. These principles apply across all splits, all goals and all experience levels.
Free weights always come first. Barbells and dumbbells require the most stabiliser muscle activation, coordination and central nervous system demand. They are always programmed at the start of a session when the client is freshest and most capable of handling the load safely. Machines and cables follow once free weight work is complete. Bodyweight movements come last if included. This order is non-negotiable — a client should never arrive at a heavy barbell movement already fatigued from machine work.
Compounds come before isolation within each muscle group. The largest, most demanding movements are always performed first. For chest this means a pressing movement before a fly variation. For back this means a row or pull variation before cable or machine isolation. For shoulders this means an overhead press before lateral raises. For legs this means squats and heavy pressing before leg extensions or leg curls — with the exception below.
The one exception to compounds first is leg day. Hamstring curls are programmed before squats and heavy leg pressing as a deliberate pre activation technique. Warming up the hamstrings before heavy quad dominant compound movements improves knee stability, activates the posterior chain and reduces injury risk. Clients are told why this order is used so they understand the purpose rather than just following it blindly.
Within compounds the heaviest and most technically demanding movement comes first. On a push day this means bench press or dumbbell press before overhead press. On a pull day this means barbell row, dumbbell row or T-bar row before lat pulldown or cable row. The movement that requires the most from the client physically and neurologically is always performed when they are freshest.
No movement pattern is repeated back to back within the same session. The same angle, the same movement pattern or the same stimulus is never programmed consecutively. For chest — flat pressing is never followed by another flat pressing movement. Incline is never followed by incline. For back — horizontal rowing is never followed by another horizontal row. For shoulders — lateral raise variations are never stacked back to back. The rule is simple: vary the angle, vary the equipment, vary the stimulus. One exercise per movement pattern per session. Cables and machines are used to complement free weight work and add variety — never to duplicate the same movement already performed with a barbell or dumbbell.
Supersets are not used as a primary training method for compound movements. Isolation supersets may be used sparingly for arm work or as a time saving technique at the end of a session. This is covered in section 2.2.
For clients with injuries exercise order may be reorganised around the limitation. If a movement early in the session aggravates an injury it is moved, substituted or removed entirely. The overall order principles remain the same but are adjusted to accommodate the client's physical reality. Injury substitutions are covered in full in section 9.
The overall session order rule of thumb is: free weights before machines, compounds before isolation, biggest movements first, no repeated movement patterns, vary angle and equipment throughout.
2.9 — Warm up and cool down for every session
The warm up serves two purposes — raising core body temperature and preparing the specific muscles and joints about to be trained. It is never skipped. The warm up is included within the session time established in section 2.3.
The warm up has two components — a general warm up and an exercise specific warm up. Both are optional in terms of the general component but the exercise specific warm up is always performed.
