General warm up — 5 minutes on a bike or treadmill at low intensity to raise heart rate and increase blood flow to the muscles. This is performed if time allows and is recommended but not mandatory. Clients who are already warm — for example training in a hot environment or later in the day — may skip this and go straight into the exercise specific warm up.
Exercise specific warm up varies by session type as follows:
Push day — rotator cuff warm up is performed before any pressing movement. This is non-negotiable on push days as the rotator cuff is the most commonly injured structure in pressing movements and must be warm before load is applied. The protocol is performed with no rest between exercises: band external rotation 15-20 reps each side, band internal rotation 15-20 reps each side, band pull apart 15-20 reps, cable or band face pull 15-20 reps light weight. This takes 3-5 minutes and is always the first thing done on a push day before touching any weights.
Pull day — no specific rotator cuff warm up required. The first exercise of the session includes feeler sets as described below. Optional pull ups or band work may be used to activate the back before the first working set if the client finds it beneficial.
Leg day — 5 minutes on a bike or treadmill is strongly recommended before leg sessions as the lower body responds particularly well to increased blood flow before heavy loading. The first exercise on leg day is always an isolation movement — hamstring curl — before moving into heavy compound pressing. This serves as both a warm up and a pre activation technique as established in section 2.8.
Feeler sets are performed for the first exercise of each muscle group within a session. They are not counted as working sets and do not contribute to session volume. The number of feeler sets is determined by the weight of the top set — heavier top sets require more feeler sets to prepare the joints and nervous system safely:
For the first exercise of the session — 3 to 4 feeler sets following a percentage based progression:
Set 1 — approximately 40-50% of top set weight, 12-15 reps
Set 2 — approximately 55-65% of top set weight, 8-10 reps
Set 3 — approximately 70-80% of top set weight, 4-6 reps
Set 4 — approximately 85-90% of top set weight, 2-3 reps — only for very heavy top sets
For the first exercise of each subsequent muscle group within the same session — 1 to 2 feeler sets maximum. If the muscle group is already warm from previous work, 1 feeler set is sufficient. If it is the first time that muscle group is being trained in the session, 2 feeler sets may be used.
For all other exercises within the same muscle group — no feeler sets required unless the client feels they need one. The muscle is already warm and ready from previous sets.
The feeler set protocol reduces proportionally for lighter top sets. A beginner lifting lighter weights reaches their working weight in fewer steps and therefore needs fewer feeler sets. Common sense applies — the goal is to be warm and ready without fatiguing the muscle before the working sets.
Cool down is optional and not formally programmed. Clients are advised that stretching the muscles trained for 2 to 3 minutes post session aids recovery, reduces soreness and improves flexibility over time. A suggested stretch for each major muscle group is included in the exercise library for clients who wish to cool down. It is never mandatory.
2.10 — Progressive overload built in across 12 weeks
The 12 week plan operates on a single template model. One session template is produced per training day — the client uses the same template every week and logs their own weights in the progress tracking section of the platform. The plan document does not change week to week. What changes is the weight the client lifts. This keeps the plan clean, simple and fits on one page per session.
The progressive overload structure across 12 weeks is as follows:
Week 1 — baseline week. The client uses week 1 to find their working weights for every exercise. No progression is expected this week. The focus is entirely on finding the right load within the prescribed rep range and executing every movement with good form. Week 1 weights are logged and become the baseline from which all future progression is measured.
Weeks 2 to 11 — progressive overload weeks. Every week the goal is simple — if the client hits the top of their prescribed rep range on the heavy working set, weight increases the following week by the smallest available increment, typically 1.25 to 2.5kg per side. This is the only progression metric that matters. Rep ranges stay fixed for the entire 12 weeks. Sets stay fixed for the entire 12 weeks — 2 working sets per exercise, heavy set and back off set. No extra sets are added across the 12 weeks. The optional third set on isolation movements remains available throughout but is never mandatory.
Week 12 — personal best week. The final week of the plan is framed as a test week. The client attempts personal bests on their key compound lifts to measure progress over the full 12 weeks. This gives the plan a clear end point and a motivating finish. Results from week 12 are compared against week 1 baselines in the progress tracker.
On stalling — if a client cannot add weight for two consecutive weeks the following protocol applies: first check form, sleep and nutrition before assuming the weight needs adjusting. If all three are in order and the stall continues, reduce the weight by 10% and rebuild for two weeks before attempting the previous weight again. For clients on a fat loss programme stalling on strength is expected and normal — the priority during a cut is maintaining current weights not increasing them. Strength gains during a calorie deficit are a bonus not a requirement.
Sets and rep ranges do not change across the 12 weeks. Volume does not increase across the 12 weeks. The sole driver of progression is weight. This keeps the plan measurable, manageable and focused on the one metric that matters most.
2.11 — Injury substitutions
Injuries and physical limitations are handled through a three tier system built into the Stage 2 input flow. The system responds to both the injury type selected and the free text description of how it limits training — the description is weighted more heavily than the category tick as clients frequently underestimate their limitations. If a description suggests greater severity than the category selected a warning is triggered advising the client to seek professional assessment before beginning.
The three tiers of exercise management are as follows:
Tier 1 — injury prompted substitution. When an injury is flagged the system does not automatically remove exercises. Instead it generates a secondary prompt presenting each potentially affected exercise to the client with a simple yes or no question — "Based on your injury, can you perform this exercise?" If yes the exercise stays in the plan. If no the system substitutes it with a pre approved safe alternative. This gives the client control and accountability over their own limitations while ensuring the plan remains safe and appropriate. The substitution hierarchy is: substitute first, modify second, remove entirely only if no safe alternative exists.
Tier 2 — exercise preference customisation. After the plan is generated the client sees their full exercise list and can swap any exercise for an alternative with one click. Each exercise has two to three pre approved alternatives available. This handles both injury related preferences and general exercise dislikes — increasing plan adherence by giving the client ownership over their programme.
Tier 3 — manual override. A free text box allows the client to flag anything not covered by the above. This feeds directly into the plan generation prompt and is used by the AI to make additional adjustments.
Injury specific protocols are as follows:
Knee injuries — remove high compression knee exercises including full depth squats, deep lunges and leg press with feet low on platform. Keep leg press with feet high on platform, Romanian deadlift, hamstring curl and leg extension at partial range. Add terminal knee extensions with a band to strengthen the VMO and stabilise the joint. Start with higher rep lighter weight and build strength around the joint before loading heavily. Recommend knee sleeves for compression and warmth. If pain is sharp, worsening or affects daily movement — refer to physiotherapist before continuing and offer to hold the plan.
Shoulder injuries — the rotator cuff warm up is non negotiable for any client with shoulder history, not optional. For rotator cuff issues remove overhead pressing and loaded internal rotation. Keep lateral raises at light weight, face pulls, external rotation work and horizontal pressing if pain free. For AC joint issues remove heavy bench press and dips. Keep incline pressing at moderate weight, cable flys and machine chest work. For general shoulder pain reduce pressing volume, increase pulling volume to balance the joint, remove all behind the neck movements entirely. For serious shoulder pain advise rest, refer to physiotherapist and offer to hold the plan.
Lower back injuries — remove conventional deadlifts, good mornings and any loaded spinal flexion. Keep Romanian deadlift if pain free, trap bar deadlift, leg press, and all machine based alternatives. Emphasise core strengthening as a priority within every session. Refer to physiotherapist for anything beyond general tightness.
For any injury where training is not advisable the following protocol applies: advise strongly against training and recommend professional assessment. The plan is held and not charged for until the client confirms they are ready to begin. This protects the client, protects the business and ensures the plan delivers results rather than causing harm. If the client chooses to proceed against advice this is documented and the plan includes clear warnings throughout.
Section 3 — Progressive Overload Rules
The AI needs to know exactly how the plan evolves across 12 weeks.
Cover:
How and when you increase weight — after how many sessions, by how much
How sets and reps change across the three 4-week phases
What phase 1, phase 2 and phase 3 look like structurally
How you handle someone who isn't progressing
When to deload and what that looks like
How you increase difficulty without increasing injury risk
Perfect — enough to write up 3.1:
3.1 — The core progression rule
Progressive overload is the single most important training principle in the programme. Without it adaptation stops. Every decision about exercise selection, split structure, rep ranges and session length exists to support this one rule — add weight when you're ready, never before.
The core progression rule is as follows:
Week 1 is the baseline week. The client finds their working weight for every exercise within the prescribed rep range. No progression is expected or required this week. Week 1 numbers are logged and become the reference point for all future progression.
From week 2 onwards the progression rule is simple. If the client hits the top of the prescribed rep range on the heavy working set, weight increases the following week. If the client hits the top of the rep range on the back off set, weight increases on that set the following week. These are tracked independently — if the heavy set hits the top of the range but the back off set does not, only the heavy set weight increases. The back off set weight stays the same until it also hits the top of its range. Both sets progress at their own pace.
Weight increases are small and deliberate — 1.25 to 2.5kg per side is the standard increment. Larger jumps are permitted if the client feels ready and confident — there is no cap on how much weight can be added in a single week. The client knows their body and their readiness better than any programme can prescribe. However the consequences of jumping too far are noted — if the new weight cannot be performed within the rep range with good form the previous weight is returned to the following week.
If a client hits beyond the top of their rep range — for example performing 10 reps on a 6-8 rep set — weight increases the following session without waiting to confirm consistency. One clear performance above the top of the range is sufficient to trigger progression.
Rep ranges stay fixed for all 12 weeks. Sets stay fixed for all 12 weeks. The only variable that changes week to week is the weight lifted. This keeps the programme measurable, simple and focused on the one metric that drives results.
All working sets are performed with 1-2 reps in reserve for beginner and intermediate clients. Advanced clients may push to 1 RIR on standard working sets. Rest pause sets are performed at 0-1 RIR for intermediate clients and 0 RIR — true failure — for advanced clients. Standard working sets never go to true failure — the risk to recovery and injury outweighs the benefit.
3.2 — Progression by experience level
Progressive overload operates differently across experience levels. The fundamental rule stays the same — hit the top of the rep range, add weight — but the frequency and expectation of progression varies significantly.
Beginner progression — beginners are capable of progressing faster than any other group. The nervous system adapts rapidly in the early weeks of training and weight can increase almost every session during weeks 2 to 4. This is encouraged and celebrated — newbie gains are real and should be ridden as hard as possible while they last. The only governor on beginner progression is form. If adding weight causes form to break down the weight stays the same until form is solid. There is no time based rule — a beginner who can add weight every session safely should do exactly that. From weeks 5 to 8 progression naturally slows to weekly. From weeks 9 to 12 progression may become fortnightly as the easy gains taper off. Beginner experience level is reassessed at week 6 — if a beginner is progressing consistently, executing movements confidently and performing like an intermediate they are upgraded to intermediate for the remainder of the programme. Their plan adjusts accordingly — slightly more volume, slightly more complex movements and a lower rep floor.
Intermediate progression — intermediates progress week to week as a general rule. Some weeks progression comes every session, some weeks every fortnight. Whatever works for the individual is the right answer. The sweet spot is roughly every 1 to 2 weeks. Intermediates should not expect the rapid progression of a beginner but should be adding weight consistently across the 12 weeks on most exercises.
Advanced progression — advanced lifters progress more slowly and this is normal and expected. Weight increases may take 2 to 3 weeks on some exercises. On a fat loss programme maintaining current weights is the goal for advanced lifters — strength maintenance during a calorie deficit is a genuine win and should be framed as such in the plan. Chasing weight increases during an aggressive cut is unnecessary and potentially counterproductive. On a muscle building programme if an advanced lifter has not progressed in 2 consecutive weeks intensity techniques are introduced before the stall protocol is triggered. Drop sets and rest pause sets on the back off set add training stimulus without requiring a weight increase — they act as a bridge between weight jumps. If intensity techniques are already in use and progression has still stalled for 2 weeks the full stall protocol from section 3.6 applies.
Across all experience levels the goal of progressive overload is the same — do more over time. For beginners more comes quickly and frequently. For advanced lifters more comes slowly and requires more sophisticated tools to achieve. The programme meets each client where they are and sets appropriate expectations accordingly.
3.3 — Progression by muscle group
Not all muscle groups progress at the same rate. Larger compound movements driven by multiple muscle groups progress faster than small isolation exercises driven by one muscle. Progression expectations are set accordingly in the plan so clients are not discouraged when their lateral raises haven't moved in three weeks while their bench press is going up every session.
Large compound movements — bench press, squat variations, deadlift variations, barbell and dumbbell rows, overhead press — progress fastest. On a muscle building programme weight increases every 1 to 2 weeks is realistic and expected. On a fat loss programme maintaining current weights is the goal — strength maintenance during a deficit is a win. These movements respond well to small weight jumps of 1.25 to 2.5kg per side and clients should chase progression on these lifts as a priority above all others.
Medium isolation movements — chest fly, leg extension, leg curl, cable row, lat pulldown — progress at a moderate rate. Weight increases every 2 to 3 weeks is realistic. When the top of the rep range is hit consistently weight increases as normal. Drop sets and rest pause sets on the back off set are used as a bridge between weight jumps when progression slows.
Small isolation movements — bicep curls, tricep pushdowns, lateral raises, face pulls, rear delt flies — progress slowest. Weight increases every 3 to 4 weeks is realistic. Rep progression is used alongside weight progression here — when the top of the rep range is hit the client adds reps beyond the range before increasing weight. Partials are used specifically for lateral raises — after completing full range reps the client adds 3 to 5 partial reps at the top of the movement. When partials feel strong weight increases and the client returns to full reps. This bridges the gap between weight jumps on an exercise notorious for plateauing.
Rear delts and calves are the slowest progressing muscle groups. Rep progression is more reliable than weight progression for both. Calves are programmed at the start of leg day for clients who prioritise them — 3 to 4 sets of slow controlled reps with a full stretch at the bottom and a full contraction at the top. For clients who flag calves as a lagging muscle group calf work is added to additional sessions beyond leg day. Progression for calves comes through increasing reps, slowing tempo and eventually adding weight once the higher rep ranges are consistently hit.
Core progression follows a simple hierarchy — more reps first, then harder variations, then weighted. A client starts with bodyweight leg raises, crunches and sit ups. When the top of the rep range is hit consistently they progress to harder variations — hanging leg raises, decline sit ups, weighted crunches. Weighted core work is introduced once bodyweight variations are performed with full control and range of motion.
Progression expectations by muscle group are communicated clearly in the plan. Clients are told upfront that their bench press will progress faster than their lateral raises and that this is completely normal. Managing these expectations prevents frustration and keeps clients focused on the metrics that matter most — the big compound lifts where progression has the greatest impact on overall development.
3.4 — Progression for bodyweight movements
Bodyweight movements are legitimate training tools and not a compromise on proper programming. Pull ups and dips in particular are excellent compound movements that can be loaded with additional weight making them as progressive as any barbell exercise. They are programmed as main exercises in their own right — not just warm up or accessory movements.
Progression for bodyweight movements follows a clear hierarchy depending on the movement and the client's starting point:
Pull up progression — the pull up is one of the best upper body pulling movements available and is programmed accordingly on pull days and upper days where appropriate. For clients who cannot yet perform a full pull up the progression route is as follows: assisted machine pull up first to build the movement pattern and strength, band assisted pull ups next as assistance decreases over time, negative pull ups — jumping to the top position and lowering slowly — to build eccentric strength, then full bodyweight pull ups once strength allows, then weighted pull ups using a belt or holding a dumbbell between the feet. Rep range and weight progression follow the same rules as all other exercises — hit the top of the rep range, add resistance. For clients already performing 15 to 20 bodyweight pull ups easily progression moves immediately to weighted. Full range of motion is enforced — dead hang at the bottom, chin over bar at the top. Pause reps at the top position are used as an intermediate step between bodyweight and weighted.
Dip progression follows the same structure. Assisted machine dips for beginners, bodyweight dips once strength allows, weighted dips for intermediate and advanced clients. Full range of motion — shoulders below elbows at the bottom — is non negotiable. Partial range dips are not programmed.
Push up progression for home training clients — push ups are programmed as a primary chest movement for home based clients only, not for gym based clients where pressing equipment is available. Progression follows this hierarchy: standard push ups, tempo push ups with a slow lowering phase of 3 to 4 seconds, paused push ups with a 2 second pause at the bottom, decline push ups to increase difficulty and upper chest involvement, archer push ups for unilateral loading, weighted push ups with a plate on the back, and eventually one arm push up progressions for advanced home trainers. Rep progression is used alongside variation progression — when the top of the rep range is hit consistently the client either adds reps beyond the range or moves to the next harder variation.
For home training clients with no equipment progressive overload is achieved through four methods in order: rep progression — add reps until a new ceiling is reached, tempo manipulation — slower lowering phase increases time under tension without adding weight, harder variations — move to a more difficult version of the movement, unilateral progressions — one arm or one leg variations dramatically increase difficulty without any equipment. These four tools applied consistently produce genuine progressive overload for home trainers across a full 12 weeks.
Bodyweight movements for gym based clients are used primarily for beginners and occasionally as finishers or accessory work. Advanced gym based clients rarely need bodyweight movements as primary exercises — loaded alternatives exist for every bodyweight movement and are more easily progressive. The exception is pull ups and dips which remain excellent exercises for all experience levels when loaded appropriately.
3.5 — Progression for cardio
Cardio progression is goal dependent and responds to results rather than following a fixed weekly schedule. The primary driver of cardio progression is body weight data — if the client is losing weight at the target rate cardio stays the same. If weight loss stalls cardio increases. If weight loss is too fast cardio decreases or calories increase. This makes cardio a dynamic tool rather than a rigid prescription.
For fat loss clients cardio progresses across the 12 weeks in two ways — duration and step goal. Post session cardio starts at 10 minutes in weeks 1 to 4 while the client establishes the habit and their body adapts to the training load. It increases to 15 minutes in weeks 5 to 8 as adaptation occurs and the calorie burn needs to increase to maintain progress. In weeks 9 to 12 post session cardio increases to 20 minutes as the client approaches the end of the programme and fat loss momentum needs to be maintained. The step goal follows the same pattern — starting at 8,000 steps per day in weeks 1 to 4, increasing to 9,000 in weeks 5 to 8 and 10,000 in weeks 9 to 12. These are guidelines not rigid rules — if a client is losing weight consistently at the lower cardio levels there is no need to increase. If weight loss stalls at any point cardio duration and step goal increase immediately rather than waiting for the next phase.
Incline and resistance levels on cardio equipment can increase across the 12 weeks as fitness improves. This is treated as a secondary progression tool — duration and frequency increase first, intensity increases second. A client who has been walking on a flat treadmill for 10 minutes progresses to incline before increasing duration.
For muscle building clients cardio stays low and fixed for the majority of the 12 weeks to preserve the calorie surplus needed for growth. If a client is struggling to eat enough to maintain their surplus post session cardio can be reduced or removed temporarily — surplus calories are more important than cardio for muscle building clients. Cardio only increases for muscle building clients if cardiovascular health markers suggest it is necessary or if the client specifically requests it.
HIIT is introduced only when weight loss has stalled despite consistent post session cardio and a high step goal, or when a client specifically requests it. HIIT progression follows this structure — start with a work to rest ratio of 1 to 2, for example 20 seconds work and 40 seconds rest, for 6 to 8 rounds. As fitness improves the rest period decreases — 1 to 1.5 ratio then 1 to 1. Once the ratio reaches 1 to 1 rounds are added rather than further reducing rest. HIIT sessions replace one post session cardio slot — they do not add an extra session on top of the existing programme.
Cardio is tracked in the progress tracker with a simple log — date, type of cardio, duration and intensity level. This keeps the client accountable and provides data when weight loss stalls. If cardio is consistently not being completed this is addressed before increasing food or adjusting the training plan — compliance is always checked before variables are changed.
Good — let me fill the gaps:
On rebuilding after a 10% drop
Gradual is right and here's the structure I'd suggest. After dropping 10% the client spends 2 weeks building back through the rep range — not jumping straight back to the previous weight. The progression rule stays the same — hit the top of the rep range, add weight next week. So if they dropped from 100kg to 90kg they work back up naturally week by week rather than attempting 100kg again immediately. When they get back to 100kg they'll be stronger and more capable of pushing through it.
On multiple exercises stalling simultaneously
This is almost never a programme issue — it's systemic. When multiple lifts stall at the same time the cause is nearly always one of three things in order of likelihood:
Nutrition — not eating enough to support progression, protein too low, calories too low for the goal
Sleep — consistently poor sleep tanks recovery and progression across the board
Accumulated fatigue — the body needs a short deload across all exercises not just one
The protocol for multiple simultaneous stalls is: audit nutrition and sleep first — if both are in order take a full week of reduced volume and weight across all exercises then come back fresh. This is the closest thing to a deload in the programme without formally programming one.
On when strength loss becomes a problem during a cut
Here's a useful rule. Losing 1-2 reps on a set over 4 weeks of cutting is normal and acceptable. Losing more than that or losing weight on the bar consistently across multiple exercises over 2 weeks is significant and needs addressing. At that point calories need adjusting upward slightly — the deficit is too aggressive and muscle is being lost not just fat.
On the psychology of stalling
This is actually one of the most important coaching points in the whole plan. Here's how I'd frame it:
A stall is not failure — it's your body telling you it needs something. Either more food, more sleep, more recovery or a slightly different stimulus. Every advanced lifter in history has stalled. The ones who break through are the ones who treat it as a problem to solve not a reason to quit. A stall handled correctly almost always leads to a personal best shortly after.
3.6 — Stalling protocol
A stall is defined as failing to progress on an exercise for two consecutive weeks despite hitting the rep range consistently in previous weeks. A single week without progression is not a stall — it is normal variation. Two consecutive weeks of no progression triggers the stalling protocol.
Before applying any protocol the cause of the stall is identified. A single exercise stalling is usually a local issue — form, exercise specific fatigue or a weight jump that was too large. Multiple exercises stalling simultaneously is almost always a systemic issue — nutrition, sleep or accumulated fatigue. The cause determines the response.
For a single exercise stall the protocol is as follows. Week 1 of stall — audit form, sleep and nutrition before changing anything. Poor form wastes energy and reduces stimulus. Poor sleep kills recovery. Insufficient protein or calories prevents adaptation. If any of these are off address them before touching the programme. Week 2 of stall — if form, sleep and nutrition are in order drop the weight by 10% and rebuild gradually using the standard progression rule — hit the top of the rep range, add weight next week. The client works back up to the previous weight naturally over 2 to 3 weeks. When they return to the previous weight they will be stronger and more capable of pushing through it. Week 3 of stall — if the stall persists after rebuilding, swap the exercise for a variation that targets the same muscle group from a slightly different angle. For example flat bench press becomes incline bench press. This provides a new stimulus to the muscle and almost always breaks the plateau. The new exercise follows the same progression rules from week 1.
For multiple simultaneous stalls the protocol is as follows. First audit nutrition and sleep — when multiple lifts stall at the same time this is nearly always the cause. If nutrition and sleep are in order take one full week of reduced volume and weight across all exercises — approximately 60% of normal working weights for 2 sets only. This clears accumulated fatigue without losing fitness and almost always results in a personal best the following week.
For cutting clients losing strength the following rule applies. Losing 1 to 2 reps on a set over 4 weeks of cutting is normal and acceptable — this is an expected side effect of a calorie deficit and is not treated as a stall. Losing more than 2 reps or losing weight on the bar consistently across multiple exercises over 2 weeks is significant. At this point the deficit is too aggressive and calories are adjusted upward slightly to protect muscle mass. The goal of a cut is to lose fat not muscle — if muscle is being lost the deficit needs reducing.
For advanced lifters intensity techniques — drop sets and rest pause sets — are introduced on the back off set before the stall protocol is triggered. If intensity techniques are already in place and progression has still stalled for 2 consecutive weeks the full stall protocol above applies.
The psychology of stalling is addressed directly in the plan. A stall is not failure — it is the body communicating that it needs something. More food, more sleep, more recovery or a new stimulus. Every serious lifter stalls. The ones who break through treat it as a problem to solve not a reason to quit. A stall handled correctly with the protocol above almost always leads to a personal best within 2 to 3 weeks. Push through, trust the process and follow the steps.
3.7 — Long term progression — what happens after 12 weeks
The 12 week plan is a foundation — not a destination. The real transformation for most clients happens in cycles 2, 3 and beyond as progressive overload compounds over time and habits become automatic. Long term retention and results are built into the programme structure from day one.
Plan continuation is handled through the subscription model. Subscribers have their plan automatically updated every 12 weeks based on logged progress data. In week 12 the client completes their personal best week and logs their final weights and measurements in the progress tracker. The system reads this data and generates plan 2 automatically — starting weights in plan 2 are based on where the client finished in plan 1, not from zero. No decision is required from the client — the continuation happens seamlessly as part of their subscription.
A deload week between cycles is advised but not mandatory. After 12 weeks of progressive training the body benefits from a full week of reduced volume and intensity before beginning a new cycle. Clients are advised to take this week — lighter weights, fewer sets, more sleep, more food — and are told that this investment in recovery almost always produces a stronger start to cycle 2. The deload week is not programmed formally — it is recommended in the week 12 communication and the client decides whether to take it.
Split selection for plan 2 is guided by the system based on progression data. If the client has been progressing consistently on their current split the recommendation is to continue with the same split for another cycle — consistency and measurability outweigh novelty when gains are still coming. If progression has slowed significantly across multiple exercises in the final weeks of the plan a split change is recommended to provide a new stimulus. The client has final say — the system presents the recommendation and the reasoning, the client confirms or chooses an alternative.
Exercise selection in plan 2 builds on plan 1 with some variation. The core compound movements stay consistent across cycles — these are the exercises that drive the most progress and should not be rotated frequently. Isolation exercises can be varied between cycles to provide new stimulus and prevent boredom. Approximately 70% of exercises carry over from plan 1, 30% are new variations targeting the same muscle groups from slightly different angles.
For clients whose goal changes between cycles — for example finishing a cut and starting a bulk — the new plan is built entirely around the new goal. Calories, cardio and training intensity all adjust accordingly. The transition is handled in the week 12 check in where the client confirms their goal for the next cycle. The system builds plan 2 from their current stats and new goal as if it were a first plan — but with the advantage of 12 weeks of logged data informing the starting point.
For clients on multiple cycles the experience level assessment is updated at the start of each new plan. A client who started as a beginner in cycle 1 may be intermediate by cycle 2 and intermediate to advanced by cycle 3. The plan evolves accordingly — more complex splits, higher volume, lower rep floors and more intensity techniques as the client develops. The system tracks cycle number and adjusts the programme complexity automatically.
The motivational framing for plan 2 is built into the week 10 and week 12 communications: 12 weeks was the foundation. The habits are built, the movement patterns are solid and the progress is real. Plan 2 is where the transformation accelerates — not starting over, starting further ahead with more knowledge, more strength and more belief in the process.
3.8 — Communicating progressive overload to the client
Progressive overload is communicated across three separate touchpoints — the plan, the info doc and the tracker. Each has a specific purpose and none of them overlap. The plan stays clean and session focused. The info doc handles all education. The tracker handles all data and accountability.
Within the plan progressive overload is communicated in one simple line at the top of every session:
"Hit the top of your rep range? Add weight next week. No exceptions."
That is the only progressive overload instruction in the plan document itself. Everything else lives in the info doc.
The info doc contains a dedicated progressive overload section covering the following in short sharp bullet points:
What progressive overload is and why it is the most important principle in training
How to know when to add weight — hit the top of the rep range on the heavy set
How much to add — 1.25 to 2.5kg per side as a starting point, larger jumps permitted if ready
What to do if you can't add weight — the stalling protocol in simple language
Why your body needs progressive overload to grow — force it to adapt or it won't
The difference between the heavy set and the back off set and how each progresses independently
What a baseline week is and why week 1 is not about pushing limits
How beginners progress differently to advanced lifters
What to do when cutting — maintaining weight is winning
The psychology of stalling — it is a problem to solve not a reason to quit
For clients who are nervous about adding weight — common particularly among beginners and those who lack gym confidence — the info doc includes a specific section addressing this directly:
"Adding weight is not optional — it is the entire point. Your body grows when it is forced to. Staying at the same weight week after week tells your body it already has everything it needs to handle the demand. It doesn't need to change. The moment you add weight you send a signal — adapt or fall behind. Your body will always choose to adapt. Trust the process, add the weight, watch what happens."
Weight logging is handled in the tracker. Every session the client logs the weight used for each exercise. The tracker displays their progression visually over time — seeing the numbers go up week by week is one of the most powerful motivation tools available. The tracker is tied to the monthly subscription and forms the backbone of the accountability system. Clients who log consistently progress faster than those who don't — this is communicated clearly in the info doc.
Motivational messaging at key milestones is delivered through the email automation sequence — not in the plan document. The week 6 halfway point email acknowledges progress, reviews what has been achieved and refocuses the client on the second half. The week 10 email prepares them for the final push and introduces the concept of plan 2. The week 12 email celebrates completion, presents personal best week as the culmination of 12 weeks of work and introduces the continuation plan. These touchpoints keep the client emotionally connected to the programme without cluttering the plan itself.
The info doc is delivered alongside the plan at the point of purchase and is positioned as essential reading before session 1. It is framed not as a terms and conditions document but as a coaching briefing — the things your coach would tell you on day one if you were working together in person. Short, practical, honest and motivating.
SECTION 4 — EXERCISE LIBRARY: PROPOSED STRUCTURE
4.1 — How the exercise library works A short intro explaining what the library is, how the AI uses it and what the priority system means. Sets the context before anything else.
4.2 — Exercise selection rules The rules the AI follows when picking exercises. This is where session type, experience level, equipment, gender and goal all influence what gets selected. Covers all session types cleanly.
4.3 — Push day exercise list and priorities Clean table — exercise, primary muscle, priority, notes.
4.4 — Pull day exercise list and priorities Same format.
4.5 — Leg day exercise list and priorities Same format.
4.6 — Upper day exercise list and priorities Same format.
4.7 — Lower day exercise list and priorities Same format.
4.8 — Full body day exercise list and priorities Same format.
4.9 — Specialist session exercise lists Chest and Back day, Shoulders and Arms day, Posterior chain day, Arnold split days.
4.10 — Individual exercise entries The full database. Every exercise with coaching cues, common mistakes, equipment, experience level, rep ranges, injury contraindications, substitutions and YouTube links.
Section 4 — Exercise Library
Every exercise you want included in your plans. For each one cover:
Exercise name
Primary muscle group targeted
Secondary muscles
Equipment needed
Coaching cues — exactly how to perform it correctly
Common mistakes to avoid
Beginner, intermediate or advanced classification
YouTube link to the best demonstration you know of
Any injury contraindications — who should avoid it and why
What exercise to substitute if someone can't do it
Start with your 50–60 most used exercises. You can add more over time.
4.1 — How the Exercise Library Works
The exercise library is the database the AI draws from when building every plan. It is not a client facing document — clients never see it directly. They see the outputs: a clean training plan, a video library, injury substitutions and exercise alternatives. The library operates as a backend reference system that different parts of the platform query independently.
The library has three functions:
Plan generation. When a client completes their Stage 1 and Stage 2 inputs the plan generator queries the exercise library and filters by session type, experience level, equipment availability, goal, gender and priority rating. It selects the appropriate exercises and builds the session structure according to the rules in Section 2. The full library is never read in one go — only the relevant filtered entries are called.
Video library. The coaching cues and common mistakes from each exercise entry power the video library on the website. When a client clicks an exercise name in their plan they are taken to the corresponding video — filmed by the coach — with the coaching cues displayed as text overlays. The exercise library is therefore also the script for every coaching video.
Injury substitution. When a client flags an injury the system queries the injury contraindications and substitutions fields of the affected exercises and presents alternatives through the prompted substitution system established in Section 2.11.
New exercises can be added to the library at any time without rebuilding the plan generator — the system automatically incorporates new entries when they are added to the database.
4.2 — Universal Exercise Selection Rules
The exercise library operates on a universal priority system that applies across all session types. The AI uses the same priority ratings throughout and filters intelligently based on the following rules.
Priority Ratings
Primary: Always selected first. Core exercises for the muscle group and session type.
Secondary: Fill remaining slots after primaries. Add variety and volume.
Tertiary: Used occasionally for variety or specific situations.
Specialist: Only used when specifically triggered by experience level, goal, equipment, gender or direct client request.
Rule 1 — Priority Order
Primary exercises are always selected first. Secondary exercises fill remaining slots. Tertiary exercises are used occasionally for variety. Specialist exercises are only used when specifically triggered. This applies across every session type without exception.
Rule 2 — Muscle Group Allocation
The AI only selects exercises from the correct muscle groups for each session type. It never programmes a chest exercise on a pull day or a bicep exercise on a push day. Session type determines which muscle group lists are active. This is defined in section 4.3.
Rule 3 — No Repeated Movement Patterns
The same movement pattern is never programmed twice in one session regardless of priority rating. Flat dumbbell press and flat barbell press are the same pattern — only one appears per session. Incline dumbbell press and flat dumbbell press are different patterns — both can appear. Rope pushdown and V bar pushdown are the same pattern — only one per session. The AI varies angle, equipment and stimulus throughout every session.
Rule 4 — Balance on Upper Day
Upper day always maintains a balance between push and pull volume. If three chest exercises are selected, at least two back exercises must be included. The leading muscle group — chest on Upper 1, back on Upper 2 — gets one additional exercise over the secondary muscle group. Shoulders and triceps fill remaining slots after chest and back are allocated.
Rule 5 — Time Filtering
When time is limited Primary exercises are protected and Secondary, Tertiary and Specialist exercises are dropped first. The AI reduces volume by removing lower priority exercises before reducing sets on higher priority exercises.
45 minute session — Primary exercises only
60 minute session — Primary and Secondary exercises
90 minute and 2 hour session — all priority levels available
Rule 6 — Experience Level Filtering
Certain exercises are restricted by experience level regardless of priority rating:
Barbell squat — intermediate and advanced only
Barbell bent over row — intermediate and advanced only
Nordic curl — advanced only
Bulgarian split squat — intermediate and advanced only
Pull ups — assisted for beginners, bodyweight for intermediate, weighted for advanced
Beginners always receive simpler movement variations before complex ones
Rule 7 — Equipment Filtering
Equipment dependent exercises are only selected when the client has confirmed access to that equipment. MagGrip lat pulldown and Cybex row only appear when the client confirms their gym has these machines. Pendulum squat only appears when the client confirms access. If equipment is unavailable the AI selects the next highest priority alternative from the same muscle group.
Rule 8 — Gender Filtering
Hip thrust, glute bridge and cable kickback are Primary for female clients and Tertiary for male clients unless glutes are flagged as a lagging muscle group. The AI always includes at least one glute focused movement for female clients on leg day. For male clients these movements are only included when specifically requested.
Rule 9 — Session Type Exclusions
Certain exercises are excluded from specific session types regardless of priority rating:
Arnold press — excluded from upper day, too many better options competing for slots
Decline press — excluded from upper day, time is too tight
Traps and shrugs — excluded from upper day, reserved for dedicated pull day
Press ups — excluded from all gym based sessions, beginners and home training only
Barbell squat — excluded from full body sessions, too much recovery demand
Nordic curl — excluded from full body and upper lower sessions, too much recovery demand
Rule 10 — Variety Across 12 Weeks
The AI does not programme the same exercise combination every session across 12 weeks. Within the Primary exercise pool it rotates selections to provide variety while maintaining the core movement patterns. For example chest sessions rotate between dumbbell press, plate loaded press and barbell bench press across different weeks rather than always defaulting to the same exercise. Secondary and Tertiary exercises provide additional variety when slots are available.
Rule 11 — Injury and Preference Filtering
Exercises flagged through the injury and preference system in Section 2.11 are removed from the available pool entirely for that client. The AI selects the next highest priority alternative from the substitution list for each removed exercise. This filtering happens before any other selection rule is applied.
Rule 12 — Core Selection
Core is programmed 2 to 3 times per week across all session types. The AI selects 3 exercises from the core Primary list for 2 to 3 sets each at the end of the session. Core exercises rotate across sessions — the same 3 exercises are not repeated in consecutive sessions. Weighted crunches and hanging leg raises are reserved for intermediate and advanced clients.
4.3 — Session Type Muscle Group Allocation
Every session type draws from the exercise entries in Doc 2 and Doc 3. No new entries are required for upper, lower, full body or specialist sessions — they use the same exercises, filtered by the muscle groups active in that session type.
Push day: Chest, Shoulders, Triceps. Pulls from push day exercise list.
Pull day: Back, Rear Delts, Traps, Biceps. Pulls from pull day exercise list.
Legs day: Quads, Hamstrings, Glutes, Adductors, Abductors, Calves, Core. Pulls from leg day exercise list.
Upper day: Chest, Shoulders, Back, Triceps. Pulls from push day chest, shoulder and tricep list plus pull day back and rear delt list. Biceps excluded — they go on lower day.
Lower day: Quads, Hamstrings, Glutes, Calves, Core, Biceps. Pulls from leg day exercise list plus pull day bicep list. Triceps excluded — they go on upper day.
Full body day: Chest, Back, Shoulders, Legs, Arms. Pulls from all lists — one to two exercises per muscle group maximum. Primary compound movements only. Used for beginners and 3 day splits.
Chest and Back day: Chest and Back trained together. Pulls from push day chest list plus pull day back list. Used exclusively in the 5 day PPL plus Chest and Back plus Shoulders and Arms split.
Shoulders and Arms day: Shoulders, Triceps, Biceps. Pulls from push day shoulder and tricep list plus pull day bicep list. Used in the 5 day PPL plus Chest and Back plus Shoulders and Arms split and the Arnold split.
Posterior chain day: Hamstrings, Glutes, lower back friendly movements. Pulls from leg day hamstring and glute list. Used in the PPL plus Push plus Posterior split. Focus on RDL, lying and seated hamstring curl, hip thrust and glute focused movements.
Arnold split days: Chest and Back day and Shoulders and Arms day as above, each repeated twice per week alongside a Legs day.
4.4 — Push Day Exercise List and Priorities
Chest
Dumbbell Press — Primary
Incline Dumbbell Press — Primary
Plate Loaded Chest Press — Primary
Incline Plate Loaded Chest Press — Primary
Pec Fly Machine — Primary
Chest Dip — Primary
Incline Smith Bench Press — Primary
Barbell Bench Press — Primary (intermediate and advanced only)
Cable Fly — Secondary
Incline Barbell Bench Press — Secondary
Decline Press — Tertiary
Press Ups — Specialist (beginners and home training only)
Shoulders
Dumbbell Shoulder Press — Primary
Lateral Raise Dumbbell — Primary
Lateral Raise Machine — Primary
Cable Lateral Raise — Primary
Face Pull — Secondary
Overhead Barbell Press — Secondary (intermediate and advanced only)
Front Raise — Secondary
Upright Row — Secondary (intermediate and advanced only)
Arnold Press — Tertiary
Triceps
Skull Crushers — Primary
Tricep Pushdown Rope — Primary
Overhead Tricep Extension Cable — Primary
Cable Tricep Kickback — Primary
Dips Weighted — Primary
JM Press — Secondary
Close Grip Bench Press — Secondary
Tricep Pushdown V Bar — Secondary
Tricep Pushdown EZ Bar — Secondary
Overhead Tricep Extension Dumbbell — Specialist
Dumbbell Tricep Kickback — Specialist
Tricep Dips Between Benches — Specialist (beginners only)
Push Day Selection Rules
Always lead chest with a Primary pressing movement — dumbbell press, barbell bench or plate loaded press
Always include at least one lateral raise variation — dumbbell, machine or cable
Always include at least one Primary tricep pushdown — rope as default
Overhead barbell press used as variation for intermediate and advanced clients only
Press ups only for beginners and home training clients
Arnold press used occasionally for variety — never as the primary shoulder movement
Tricep dips between benches, dumbbell kickback and overhead dumbbell extension reserved for specific requests or lagging tricep development
Decline press used occasionally for clients who want lower chest emphasis
Cable fly and incline barbell bench used to add variety in secondary slots

Back

MagGrip Lat Pulldown — Primary (equipment dependent)
Cybex Row — Primary (equipment dependent)

Pull Day Selection Rules
Always lead with a Primary rowing movement — T bar, cable row, seated cable row or chest supported row
Always include at least one lat pulldown variation — standard, MagGrip or wide grip
Pull ups included for intermediate and advanced clients — assisted for beginners
MagGrip and Cybex only selected when client confirms gym has this equipment
Always include rear delt fly as a Primary rear delt movement
Traps — dumbbell shrugs with straps as default, barbell shrugs as variation, behind the back shrug rarely
Biceps — EZ bar curl as default Primary, other primaries rotated for variety
Never programme more than 2 bicep exercises per session regardless of session length
Straight arm pulldown used as a lat isolation finisher occasionally
4.6 — Leg Day Exercise List and Priorities
Quads
Hack Squat — Primary
Pendulum Squat — Primary (equipment dependent)
Leg Press — Primary
Leg Extension — Primary
Barbell Squat — Secondary (intermediate and advanced only)
Bulgarian Split Squat — Specialist
Single Leg Press — Tertiary
Step Ups — Tertiary (beginners and cardio finisher only)
Hamstrings and Glutes
Romanian Deadlift — Primary
Lying Hamstring Curl — Primary
Seated Hamstring Curl — Primary
Hip Thrust Barbell — Primary female / Tertiary male
Hip Thrust Machine — Primary female / Tertiary male (equipment dependent)

Cable Kickback — Primary female / Tertiary male

Core
Leg Raises — Primary
Hanging Leg Raises — Primary
Crunches — Primary
Decline Sit Ups — Primary
Weighted Crunches — Primary (intermediate and advanced only)
Leg Day Selection Rules
Always start with lying hamstring curl before any quad compound work — pre activation rule
Always lead quad session with a Primary machine squat movement — hack squat or pendulum squat as default, leg press as alternative
Barbell squat only programmed for intermediate and advanced clients — never for beginners
Always include at least one hamstring curl variation — lying or seated
Always include RDL on hamstring focused days
Bulgarian split squat used only for specific requests or lagging leg development
For female clients — always include at least one glute focused movement — hip thrust, glute bridge or cable kickback
For male clients — glute movements only included if specifically requested or glutes flagged as lagging
Adductor machine always included — abductor machine rotated in occasionally
All three calf variations rotated — standing as default primary
Core always included at end of session — 3 exercises, 2 to 3 sets, chosen from primary list
Single leg press and step ups used sparingly — beginners and specific situations only
4.7 — Upper Day Exercise List and Priorities
Upper day pulls from the push day chest, shoulder and tricep lists and the pull day back and rear delt lists. Biceps are excluded from upper day — they are programmed on lower day.
Chest
Dumbbell Press — Primary
Incline Dumbbell Press — Primary
Plate Loaded Chest Press — Primary
Incline Plate Loaded Chest Press — Primary
Barbell Bench Press — Primary (intermediate and advanced only)
Pec Fly Machine — Primary
Chest Dip — Primary
Cable Fly — Secondary
Incline Smith Bench Press — Secondary
Excluded from upper day: Arnold press, decline press.
Shoulders
Dumbbell Shoulder Press — Primary
Lateral Raise Dumbbell — Primary
Lateral Raise Machine — Primary
Cable Lateral Raise — Primary
Face Pull — Secondary
Overhead Barbell Press — Secondary (intermediate and advanced only)
Front Raise — Secondary
Upright Row — Secondary (intermediate and advanced only)

Back
T Bar Row — Primary
Lat Pulldown — Primary

Cable Row — Primary

Pull Ups — Primary

Cable Tricep Kickback — Primary
Dips Weighted — Primary
JM Press — Secondary
Close Grip Bench Press — Secondary
Tricep Pushdown V Bar — Secondary
Tricep Pushdown EZ Bar — Secondary
Upper Day Selection Rules
Upper 1 leads with chest — chest is programmed first, back second
Upper 2 leads with back — back is programmed first, chest second
This alternating lead ensures neither push nor pull is consistently deprioritised
Balance rule — if three chest exercises are selected, at least two back exercises must be included
The leading muscle group gets one additional exercise over the secondary muscle group
Shoulders and triceps fill remaining slots after chest and back are allocated
Traps and shrugs never appear on upper day — reserved for dedicated pull day
Arnold press excluded — too many better options competing for limited slots
Decline press excluded — session time is too limited
4.8 — Lower Day Exercise List and Priorities
Lower day pulls from the leg day exercise list plus the pull day bicep list. Triceps are excluded from lower day — they are programmed on upper day.
Quads
Hack Squat — Primary
Pendulum Squat — Primary (equipment dependent)
Leg Press — Primary
Leg Extension — Primary
Barbell Squat — Secondary (intermediate and advanced only)
Bulgarian Split Squat — Specialist
Single Leg Press — Tertiary
Hamstrings and Glutes
Romanian Deadlift — Primary
Lying Hamstring Curl — Primary
Seated Hamstring Curl — Primary
Hip Thrust Barbell — Primary female / Tertiary male
Hip Thrust Machine — Primary female / Tertiary male (equipment dependent)
Glute Bridge — Primary female / Tertiary male
Cable Kickback — Primary female / Tertiary male
Calves and Core

Seated Calf Raise — Primary

Leg Raises — Primary
Hanging Leg Raises — Primary
Crunches — Primary

Biceps

Hammer Curl — Primary
Cable Curl — Primary
Preacher Curl — Primary
Barbell Curl — Secondary
Lower Day Selection Rules
Hamstring curl always first — pre activation rule as established in Section 2.8
Quad compound work follows the hamstring curl
Biceps are the finisher — programmed after all leg and core work is complete
Never programme more than 2 bicep exercises per lower day session
Female clients always receive at least one glute focused movement
Core always included — 3 exercises, 2 to 3 sets, from the Primary core list
Adductor machine included when session length allows — minimum 60 minutes
4.9 — Full Body Day Exercise List and Priorities
Full body day pulls from all session type lists. Only Primary compound movements are used. One to two exercises per muscle group maximum. Used exclusively for beginners and 3 day splits.
Chest
Dumbbell Press — Primary
Plate Loaded Chest Press — Primary
Incline Dumbbell Press — Primary
No isolation chest work on full body day. One chest exercise per session maximum.
Back
Seated Cable Row — Primary
Lat Pulldown — Primary
Chest Supported Row — Primary
T Bar Row — Primary
No isolation back work on full body day. One back exercise per session maximum.
Shoulders
Dumbbell Shoulder Press — Primary
Lateral Raise Dumbbell — Primary
One pressing movement plus one lateral raise per session maximum.
Legs
Leg Press — Primary
Hack Squat — Primary
Romanian Deadlift — Primary
Lying Hamstring Curl — Primary
One quad dominant movement plus one hamstring movement per session. Barbell squat excluded from full body sessions.
Arms
Tricep Pushdown Rope — Primary
EZ Bar Curl — Primary
Dumbbell Curl — Primary
Hammer Curl — Primary
One tricep exercise and one bicep exercise per session maximum. Arms are the finisher on full body day.
Full Body Selection Rules
Compound movements only — no isolation exercises except lateral raises and arm work at the end
Every major muscle group hit every session — chest, back, shoulders, legs, arms
Session length drives volume — on a 45 minute session some muscle groups may only get one exercise
Barbell squat excluded — too much recovery demand alongside other compound work
Nordic curl excluded — too much recovery demand
Core included — 2 exercises, 2 sets, from the Primary core list
Beginners and 3 day splits only — not appropriate for intermediate or advanced clients at higher training frequencies

Legs day: Quads, Hamstrings, Glutes, Adductors, Abductors, Calves, Core. Pulls from leg day exercise list.
Upper day: Chest, Shoulders, Back, Triceps. Pulls from push day chest, shoulder and tricep list plus pull day back and rear delt list. Biceps excluded — they go on lower day.
Lower day: Quads, Hamstrings, Glutes, Calves, Core, Biceps. Pulls from leg day exercise list plus pull day bicep list. Triceps excluded — they go on upper day.
Full body day: Chest, Back, Shoulders, Legs, Arms. Pulls from all lists — one to two exercises per muscle group maximum. Primary compound movements only. Used for beginners and 3 day splits.
Chest and Back day: Chest and Back trained together. Pulls from push day chest list plus pull day back list. Used exclusively in the 5 day PPL plus Chest and Back plus Shoulders and Arms split.
Shoulders and Arms day: Shoulders, Triceps, Biceps. Pulls from push day shoulder and tricep list plus pull day bicep list. Used in the 5 day PPL plus Chest and Back plus Shoulders and Arms split and the Arnold split.
Posterior chain day: Hamstrings, Glutes, lower back friendly movements. Pulls from leg day hamstring and glute list. Used in the PPL plus Push plus Posterior split. Focus on RDL, lying and seated hamstring curl, hip thrust and glute focused movements.
Arnold split days: Chest and Back day and Shoulders and Arms day as above, each repeated twice per week alongside a Legs day.

Upper Day Selection Rules
Upper 1 leads with chest — chest is programmed first, back second
Upper 2 leads with back — back is programmed first, chest second
This alternating lead ensures neither push nor pull is consistently deprioritised
Balance rule — if three chest exercises are selected, at least two back exercises must be included
The leading muscle group gets one additional exercise over the secondary muscle group
Shoulders and triceps fill remaining slots after chest and back are allocated
Traps and shrugs never appear on upper day — reserved for dedicated pull day
Arnold press excluded — too many better options competing for limited slots
Decline press excluded — session time is too limited

4.9 — Full Body Day Exercise List and Priorities
Full body day pulls from all session type lists. Only Primary compound movements are used. One to two exercises per muscle group maximum. Used exclusively for beginners and 3 day splits.

Plate Loaded Chest Press — Primary
Incline Dumbbell Press — Primary
No isolation chest work on full body day. One chest exercise per session maximum.
Back
Seated Cable Row — Primary
Lat Pulldown — Primary
Chest Supported Row — Primary
T Bar Row — Primary
No isolation back work on full body day. One back exercise per session maximum.
Shoulders
Dumbbell Shoulder Press — Primary
Lateral Raise Dumbbell — Primary
One pressing movement plus one lateral raise per session maximum.
Legs
Leg Press — Primary
Hack Squat — Primary
Romanian Deadlift — Primary
Lying Hamstring Curl — Primary
One quad dominant movement plus one hamstring movement per session. Barbell squat excluded from full body sessions.
Arms
Tricep Pushdown Rope — Primary
EZ Bar Curl — Primary
Dumbbell Curl — Primary
Hammer Curl — Primary
One tricep exercise and one bicep exercise per session maximum. Arms are the finisher on full body day.
Full Body Selection Rules
Compound movements only — no isolation exercises except lateral raises and arm work at the end
Every major muscle group hit every session — chest, back, shoulders, legs, arms
Session length drives volume — on a 45 minute session some muscle groups may only get one exercise
Barbell squat excluded — too much recovery demand alongside other compound work
Nordic curl excluded — too much recovery demand
Core included — 2 exercises, 2 sets, from the Primary core list
Beginners and 3 day splits only — not appropriate for intermediate or advanced clients at higher training frequencies

4.12 — Posterior Chain Day Exercise List and Priorities
Used in the PPL plus Push plus Posterior split. Focuses exclusively on hamstrings, glutes and posterior chain movements. Quad dominant exercises are excluded. This is a dedicated hamstring and glute session with no competing muscle groups.

Posterior Chain Day Selection Rules

For female clients — at least one hip thrust or glute bridge variation always included
For male clients — hip thrust included when glutes are flagged as lagging or specifically requested
No barbell squat, hack squat, leg press or leg extension — this is a posterior chain day not a quad day
Adductor machine included when session length allows
Core and calves at the end of the session
Session volume is higher on posterior chain day than on a standard leg day for hamstrings and glutes — this is the dedicated session for these muscle groups
4.10 — Individual Exercise Entries
The complete exercise database. 82 exercises covering every session type. Each entry includes primary muscle, secondary muscles, equipment, experience level, priority rating, rep ranges, coaching cues, common mistakes, injury contraindications, substitutions and coach notes. YouTube links to be populated as coaching videos are filmed.
PUSH DAY — CHEST
Exercise 1: Dumbbell Press
Primary muscle: Chest — pectoralis major mid and lower fibres
Secondary muscles: Front deltoid, triceps
Equipment: Dumbbells, flat bench
Experience level: All
Priority: Primary
Rep ranges: Heavy set 6–8 reps. Back off set 10–12 reps.
Coaching cues:
Set up with dumbbells resting on knees, sit back onto the bench and drive them up with your knees as you lie back.
Plant feet flat on the floor, slight arch in the lower back, shoulder blades retracted and pressed into the bench.
Lower dumbbells slowly with elbows at 45 degrees from the body — not flared to 90.
At the bottom feel a stretch across the chest before driving back up — no bouncing.
At the top squeeze the chest — don’t fully lock out the elbows, keep tension on the muscle throughout.
Common mistakes:
Elbows flared to 90 degrees — excessive shoulder joint stress.
Bouncing at the bottom — removes tension and increases injury risk.
Not retracting shoulder blades — reduces chest activation and destabilises the shoulder.
Dumbbells touching at the top — loses tension on the chest.
Injury contraindications:
Shoulder impingement — reduce range of motion, keep elbows slightly closer to the body.
AC joint issues — switch to neutral grip dumbbell press.
Wrist pain — adjust wrist angle, consider wrist wraps.
Substitutions: Plate loaded chest press / Barbell bench press / Press ups (home training and beginners only)
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/dumbbell-press
Notes: Default opening chest exercise for most sessions. Sets the tone — if setup is wrong everything else suffers. Prioritise the setup cues above everything else especially for beginners.
Exercise 2: Incline Dumbbell Press
Primary muscle: Chest — pectoralis major upper fibres
Secondary muscles: Front deltoid, triceps
Equipment: Dumbbells, incline bench set to 30 or 45 degrees
Experience level: All
Priority: Primary
Rep ranges: Heavy set 6–8 reps. Back off set 10–12 reps.
Coaching cues:
Set bench to 30–45 degrees — higher than 45 shifts too much load onto the front deltoid and away from the chest.
Same setup as flat dumbbell press — feet flat, shoulder blades retracted, slight arch.
Lower dumbbells to upper chest level — not the middle of the chest.
Elbows at 45 degrees throughout — not flared.
Drive up and slightly inward at the top to maximise upper chest contraction.
Common mistakes:
Bench angle too high — turns it into a shoulder press, chest activation drops significantly.
Lowering to mid chest rather than upper chest — misses the upper chest entirely.
Elbows flaring — same shoulder risk as flat press.
Rushing the lowering phase — removes the stretch and reduces stimulus.
Injury contraindications:
Shoulder impingement — avoid, switch to flat pressing.
AC joint issues — neutral grip variation, reduce range of motion.
Wrist pain — adjust wrist angle, consider wrist wraps.
Substitutions: Incline plate loaded chest press / Incline barbell bench press / Incline cable press
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/incline-dumbbell-press
Notes: Upper chest is underdeveloped in most clients. Prioritise this movement early in chest sessions when energy is high. Bench angle is critical — check it before every set.
Exercise 3: Incline Smith Bench Press
Primary muscle: Chest — pectoralis major upper fibres
Secondary muscles: Front deltoid, triceps
Equipment: Smith machine, incline bench set to 30–45 degrees
Experience level: All
Priority: Primary
Rep ranges: Heavy set 6–8 reps. Back off set 10–12 reps.
Coaching cues:
Position the incline bench inside the smith machine so the bar path aligns with the upper chest.
Set bench to 30–45 degrees — same angle rules as incline dumbbell press apply.
Grip slightly wider than shoulder width — hands directly above elbows at the bottom.
Retract shoulder blades and press into the bench before unracking.
Lower the bar to the upper chest with control — slight touch, no bounce.
The fixed bar path removes the need for a spotter — safer than free barbell incline for clients training alone.
Common mistakes:
Bench position wrong — bar path must align with upper chest, check before starting.
Bench angle too high — shifts load to shoulders.
Not retracting shoulder blades — reduces chest activation.
Bouncing the bar — removes tension and increases injury risk.
Injury contraindications:
Shoulder impingement — avoid incline pressing entirely.
Wrist issues — the fixed bar path can be uncomfortable for some wrist positions, adjust grip width.
Substitutions: Incline dumbbell press / Incline barbell bench press / Incline plate loaded chest press
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/incline-smith-bench-press
Notes: Safer alternative to free barbell incline press for clients training without a spotter. Accessible for all experience levels. Good progressive overload tool.
Exercise 4: Barbell Bench Press
Primary muscle: Chest — pectoralis major mid and lower fibres
Secondary muscles: Front deltoid, triceps
Equipment: Barbell, flat bench, squat rack
Experience level: Intermediate and advanced only
Priority: Primary
Rep ranges: Heavy set 6–8 reps. Back off set 10–12 reps.
Coaching cues:
Set up in the rack with the bar at roughly eye level when lying down.
Grip slightly wider than shoulder width — pinky fingers on the ring marks as a starting point.
Plant feet flat on the floor, slight arch in lower back, shoulder blades retracted and squeezed together hard.
Unrack the bar and position directly over mid chest before lowering.
Lower with control to mid chest — slight touch, no bounce, elbows at 45–75 degrees.
Drive the bar up explosively — think about pushing yourself into the bench rather than pushing the bar up.
Re-rack safely — never dump the bar.
Common mistakes:
Feet off the floor — removes leg drive and base of support.
Shoulder blades not retracted — major injury risk under heavy load.
Elbows flared to 90 degrees — puts shoulder in a vulnerable position.
Bouncing the bar off the chest — reduces stimulus and risks injury.
Bar path drifting toward the face — keep it over the chest throughout.
Injury contraindications:
Any shoulder injury — switch to dumbbell variation for greater joint freedom.
Wrist issues — wrist wraps recommended, consider dumbbell variation.
Beginners — not programmed until dumbbell pressing pattern is established.
Substitutions: Dumbbell press / Plate loaded chest press / Smith machine bench press
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/barbell-bench-press
Notes: The benchmark chest exercise for most clients. Strong progressive overload tool. Always requires a rack. Intermediate and advanced only.
Exercise 5: Plate Loaded Chest Press
Primary muscle: Chest — pectoralis major mid fibres
Secondary muscles: Front deltoid, triceps
Equipment: Plate loaded chest press machine
Experience level: All
Priority: Primary
Rep ranges: Heavy set 6–8 reps. Back off set 10–12 reps.
Coaching cues:
Adjust the seat so handles are at mid chest height when seated — not too high or too low.
Sit tall, feet flat on the floor, shoulder blades retracted and pressed into the pad.
Grip handles and press forward in a smooth arc — don’t let the elbows flare.
At full extension squeeze the chest — don’t lock out completely, keep tension.
Return slowly under control — feel the stretch at the bottom before the next rep.
Keep the back pressed into the pad throughout — don’t lean forward to assist the press.
Common mistakes:
Seat height wrong — too high shifts load to shoulders, too low reduces chest activation.
Leaning forward off the pad — cheating the movement, reduces chest stimulus.
Rushing the return — loses the eccentric stimulus which drives muscle growth.
Locking out completely — removes tension from the chest at the top.
Injury contraindications:
Shoulder impingement — adjust seat height and reduce range of motion.
AC joint issues — machine variation is generally more comfortable, monitor carefully.
Wrist issues — machine handles are fixed, usually comfortable.
Substitutions: Dumbbell press / Barbell bench press / Cable chest press
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/plate-loaded-chest-press
Notes: Excellent primary chest movement for all experience levels. Safer than barbell pressing for beginners as the movement path is controlled. Default machine chest exercise.
Exercise 6: Incline Plate Loaded Chest Press
Primary muscle: Chest — pectoralis major upper fibres
Secondary muscles: Front deltoid, triceps
Equipment: Incline plate loaded chest press machine
Experience level: All
Priority: Primary
Rep ranges: Heavy set 6–8 reps. Back off set 10–12 reps.
Coaching cues:
Adjust seat so handles align with upper chest when seated.
Shoulder blades retracted and pressed into the pad throughout.
Press upward and slightly forward following the natural arc of the machine.
Squeeze the upper chest at the top — don’t lock out completely.
Return slowly — feel the stretch across the upper chest at the bottom.
Keep head pressed into the headrest — don’t crane the neck forward.
Common mistakes:
Seat too low — turns it into a mid chest exercise, loses upper chest focus.
Head coming off the headrest — indicates too much weight or poor setup.
Rushing the lowering phase — misses the upper chest stretch.
Shrugging the shoulders during the press — traps taking over, reduce weight.
Injury contraindications:
Shoulder impingement — avoid incline pressing, stick to flat variations.
Neck issues — ensure head is supported throughout.
AC joint issues — monitor carefully, machine variation is more controlled.
Substitutions: Incline dumbbell press / Incline barbell bench press / Smith machine incline press
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/incline-plate-loaded-chest-press
Notes: Paired with the flat plate loaded press this covers both upper and mid chest in a machine based session. Ideal for beginners who want to develop upper chest without the complexity of incline barbell pressing.
Exercise 7: Decline Press
Primary muscle: Chest — pectoralis major lower fibres
Secondary muscles: Front deltoid, triceps
Equipment: Decline bench, barbell or dumbbells
Experience level: Intermediate and advanced
Priority: Tertiary
Rep ranges: Heavy set 6–8 reps. Back off set 10–12 reps.
Coaching cues:
Secure feet under the foot pads before lying back — essential for safety on a decline bench.
Shoulder blades retracted and pressed into the bench.
Lower the bar or dumbbells to the lower chest — below the nipple line.
Elbows at 45–75 degrees — same as flat press.
Drive up explosively from the lower chest.
Never unrack or re-rack without a spotter on the barbell variation.
Common mistakes:
Feet not secured — serious injury risk on a decline bench.
Lowering to mid chest — misses the lower chest entirely.
No spotter on barbell variation — re-racking is awkward on a decline, always use a spotter.
Overusing decline — lower chest is already stimulated by flat pressing, decline is supplementary.
Injury contraindications:
Lower back issues — the decline position can aggravate lower back problems, avoid.
Shoulder issues — switch to flat pressing.
High blood pressure — inverted position is not recommended, avoid.
Substitutions: Dips with forward lean / Cable fly with cables set high / Flat dumbbell press
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/decline-press
Notes: Used sparingly — only when a client specifically wants lower chest emphasis. Most clients get sufficient lower chest stimulus from flat pressing and dips. Never the primary chest exercise in a session.
Exercise 8: Pec Fly Machine
Primary muscle: Chest — pectoralis major, full stretch and contraction
Secondary muscles: Front deltoid minimal
Equipment: Pec fly machine (pec deck)
Experience level: All
Priority: Primary
Rep ranges: Heavy set 8–10 reps. Back off set 12–15 reps.
Coaching cues:
Adjust seat so arms are parallel to the floor when holding the handles — elbows at shoulder height.
Sit tall, back pressed firmly into the pad, feet flat on the floor.
Keep a slight bend in the elbows throughout — never lock out straight.
Bring the handles together in a smooth arc — squeeze the chest hard at the point of contraction.
Return slowly and under full control — feel the stretch across the chest at the full open position.
Don’t let the weight stack touch between reps — keep tension on the muscle throughout.
Common mistakes:
Seat too high or too low — shifts load away from chest.
Arms too straight — puts excessive stress on the elbow joint.
Rushing the return — loses the eccentric and the stretch.
Letting the weight stack touch between reps — removes tension at the most valuable point.
Shrugging — traps taking over, reduce weight.
Injury contraindications:
Shoulder impingement — reduce range of motion, don’t go to full stretch.
AC joint issues — machine fly is generally well tolerated but monitor carefully.
Pectoral muscle tear history — avoid full range fly movements, consult physiotherapist first.
Substitutions: Cable fly / Dumbbell fly / Cable crossover
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/pec-fly-machine
Notes: Excellent isolation exercise for the chest. The full stretch at the bottom and hard contraction at the top make this one of the best chest finishing movements available. Higher rep ranges work well here. Default chest isolation movement.
Exercise 9: Cable Fly
Primary muscle: Chest — pectoralis major, emphasis depends on cable height
Secondary muscles: Front deltoid minimal
Equipment: Cable machine, two adjustable cable pulleys
Experience level: All
Priority: Secondary
Rep ranges: Heavy set 8–10 reps. Back off set 12–15 reps.
Coaching cues:
Set cable height based on chest area being targeted — high cables target lower chest, low cables target upper chest, mid cables target mid chest.
Stand in the centre of the cable machine with a slight forward lean and staggered stance for stability.
Keep a slight bend in the elbows throughout — arms move in an arc not a straight line.
Bring hands together in front of the chest — squeeze hard at the point of contraction.
Return slowly under control — feel the stretch across the chest at the full open position.
Keep the core braced throughout — don’t let the body rotate or lean excessively.
Common mistakes:
Standing too upright — reduces chest activation and puts more load on the shoulders.
Arms straight — elbow joint stress.
Not controlling the return — loses the eccentric stimulus.
Using too much weight — turns it into a pushing movement rather than a fly.
Not adjusting cable height — missing the target area of the chest.
Injury contraindications:
Shoulder impingement — reduce range of motion.
AC joint issues — monitor carefully, adjust cable height to find a comfortable position.
Wrist issues — adjust grip angle.
Substitutions: Pec fly machine / Dumbbell fly / Resistance band fly (home training)
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/cable-fly
