Notes: Versatile exercise — cable height determines which part of the chest is targeted. High cables for lower chest emphasis, low cables for upper chest emphasis. Good variety movement when the pec fly machine is occupied.
Exercise 10: Chest Dip
Primary muscle: Chest — pectoralis major lower fibres with forward lean
Secondary muscles: Triceps, front deltoid
Equipment: Dip bars or assisted dip machine
Experience level: All — assisted for beginners
Priority: Primary
Rep ranges: Heavy set 6–10 reps bodyweight or weighted. Back off set 10–15 reps.
Coaching cues:
Lean forward throughout — the forward lean shifts emphasis from triceps to chest. Upright position equals tricep dip, forward lean equals chest dip.
Grip the bars and lower yourself slowly — chest moving toward the bars, elbows flaring slightly outward.
Lower until shoulders are below elbows — full range of motion.
Drive back up through the chest — squeeze at the top without fully locking out.
For weighted dips — use a dip belt or hold a dumbbell between the feet.
For beginners — use the assisted dip machine with appropriate counterweight.
Common mistakes:
Not leaning forward — trains triceps not chest.
Insufficient depth — not getting shoulders below elbows reduces chest stimulus significantly.
Rushing the movement — loss of control under bodyweight or added weight is a serious injury risk.
Too much weight too soon — master bodyweight first before adding load.
Injury contraindications:
Shoulder impingement — avoid, the end range position is uncomfortable and potentially damaging.
AC joint issues — avoid, significant stress on the AC joint at the bottom of the movement.
Elbow issues — monitor carefully, reduce range of motion if pain occurs.
Substitutions: Decline press / Cable fly with high cables / Pec fly machine
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/chest-dip
Notes: One of the best chest exercises available when performed correctly with a forward lean. Beginners use the assisted machine. Intermediate clients use bodyweight. Advanced clients add weight via a dip belt.
Exercise 11: Press Ups
Primary muscle: Chest — pectoralis major
Secondary muscles: Triceps, front deltoid, core
Equipment: None — bodyweight
Experience level: Beginners and home training only
Priority: Specialist
Rep ranges: Standard 10–20 reps. As a finisher — to failure.
Coaching cues:
Hands slightly wider than shoulder width, fingers pointing forward or slightly outward.
Body in a straight line from head to heels — no sagging hips or raised backside.
Lower chest toward the floor — full range of motion, chest touches or nearly touches the floor.
Elbows at 45 degrees — not flared to 90.
Drive back up explosively — squeeze the chest at the top.
For progressions — elevate feet for upper chest emphasis, use rings for instability.
Common mistakes:
Hips sagging — core not engaged, back injury risk.
Partial range of motion — not getting chest to the floor significantly reduces stimulus.
Elbows flared to 90 degrees — shoulder stress.
Head drooping — keep neutral spine throughout including the neck.
Injury contraindications:
Wrist issues — perform on fists or use push up handles to keep wrists neutral.
Shoulder issues — reduce range of motion, monitor carefully.
Substitutions: Dumbbell press when equipment becomes available / Plate loaded chest press
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/press-ups
Notes: Specialist exercise — only programmed for beginners with no gym access or home training clients. Not used in gym based plans where pressing equipment is available.
PUSH DAY — SHOULDERS
Exercise 12: Dumbbell Shoulder Press
Primary muscle: Shoulders — anterior and lateral deltoid
Secondary muscles: Triceps, upper traps
Equipment: Dumbbells, adjustable bench set to 90 degrees
Experience level: All
Priority: Primary
Rep ranges: Heavy set 6–8 reps. Back off set 10–12 reps.
Coaching cues:
Sit upright with back fully supported — 90 degree back rest prevents lower back strain under heavy load.
Start with dumbbells at shoulder height, elbows slightly in front of the body — not directly out to the sides.
Press directly overhead in a slight arc — dumbbells come together slightly at the top without touching.
At the top do not lock out completely — keep tension on the deltoid.
Lower slowly back to shoulder height — full range of motion, elbows below shoulder level at the bottom.
Keep core braced throughout — no excessive arching of the lower back.
Common mistakes:
Arching the lower back excessively — using momentum rather than shoulder strength, reduce weight.
Elbows flared directly out to the sides — increases shoulder impingement risk.
Partial range of motion — not lowering dumbbells to shoulder height reduces stimulus.
Dumbbells touching at the top — loses tension on the deltoid.
Using momentum to initiate the press — strict controlled movement throughout.
Injury contraindications:
Shoulder impingement — avoid overhead pressing, switch to lateral raises and face pulls only.
Rotator cuff issues — avoid, switch to machine shoulder press with reduced range of motion.
AC joint issues — monitor carefully, reduce range of motion if pain occurs.
Substitutions: Overhead barbell press / Arnold press / Machine shoulder press / Smith machine shoulder press
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/dumbbell-shoulder-press
Notes: Default primary shoulder movement. Dumbbell variation preferred over barbell for greater range of motion and independent arm movement. Seated with back support recommended for heavier loads.
Exercise 13: Overhead Barbell Press
Primary muscle: Shoulders — anterior and lateral deltoid
Secondary muscles: Triceps, upper traps, core
Equipment: Barbell, squat rack
Experience level: Intermediate and advanced
Priority: Secondary
Rep ranges: Heavy set 6–8 reps. Back off set 10–12 reps.
Coaching cues:
Set up with barbell at upper chest height in a rack — grip slightly wider than shoulder width.
Brace the core hard before unracking — the standing overhead press demands significant core stability.
Press the bar directly overhead — the bar travels in a slight arc moving slightly back as it passes the face.
At the top the bar should be directly over the mid foot — not in front of the body.
Lower under control to upper chest height — full range of motion.
Keep the glutes squeezed and core braced throughout — no excessive lower back arch.
Common mistakes:
Bar path drifting forward — bar should end up over the mid foot not in front of it.
Excessive lower back arch — lumbar spine under significant stress, reduce weight.
Grip too wide — reduces power output and increases shoulder stress.
Not bracing the core — serious lower back injury risk under heavy load.
Injury contraindications:
Any shoulder injury — switch to dumbbell variation for greater joint freedom.
Lower back issues — seated variation only or avoid entirely.
Beginners — not programmed until dumbbell pressing pattern is well established.
Substitutions: Dumbbell shoulder press / Smith machine shoulder press / Seated barbell press
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/overhead-barbell-press
Notes: Intermediate and advanced clients only. A strong compound movement that builds significant overhead strength and core stability. Dumbbell press leads — this is used as a variation to add variety and drive strength progression.
Exercise 14: Arnold Press
Primary muscle: Shoulders — full deltoid, all three heads
Secondary muscles: Triceps, upper traps
Equipment: Dumbbells, adjustable bench set to 90 degrees
Experience level: Intermediate and advanced
Priority: Tertiary
Rep ranges: Heavy set 8–10 reps. Back off set 12–15 reps.
Coaching cues:
Start with dumbbells at chin height, palms facing toward you — as if at the top of a bicep curl.
As you press overhead rotate the palms outward so they face away from you at the top.
The rotation should be smooth and continuous throughout the press — not a two stage movement.
At the top dumbbells are in the standard shoulder press position — palms facing forward.
Reverse the rotation on the way down — palms return to facing you at chin height.
Keep the movement controlled throughout — the rotation requires lighter weight than a standard press.
Common mistakes:
Rushing the rotation — the value of the Arnold press is in the controlled rotation, rushing it defeats the purpose.
Going too heavy — the rotating movement requires more control than a standard press.
Incomplete rotation — not rotating fully reduces the stimulus on all three deltoid heads.
Partial range of motion — must go through the full arc from chin height to overhead.
Injury contraindications:
Any shoulder injury — avoid, the rotation adds stress to an already compromised joint.
Rotator cuff issues — avoid entirely.
Beginners — not programmed, too complex before basic pressing patterns are established.
Substitutions: Dumbbell shoulder press / Overhead barbell press
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/arnold-press
Notes: Used occasionally for variety — never as the primary shoulder movement. Hits all three deltoid heads through the rotating movement. Tertiary rating reflects its occasional use rather than any lack of quality.
Exercise 15: Lateral Raise — Dumbbell
Primary muscle: Shoulders — lateral deltoid
Secondary muscles: Upper traps minimal
Equipment: Dumbbells
Experience level: All
Priority: Primary
Rep ranges: Heavy set 10–12 reps. Back off set 15–20 reps. Partials — 3–5 additional partial reps at top of range.
Coaching cues:
Stand with dumbbells at the sides, slight bend in the elbows throughout — never straight arms.
Raise dumbbells out to the sides in an arc — lead with the elbows not the hands.
At the top the dumbbells should be at shoulder height — no higher, no lower.
Tilt the front of the dumbbell very slightly downward at the top — as if pouring water from a jug. Keeps tension on the lateral deltoid and reduces upper trap involvement.
Lower slowly under full control — 3–4 second lowering phase maximises stimulus.
Partials — after completing full reps, perform 3–5 partial reps at the top half of the range to fully exhaust the lateral deltoid.
Common mistakes:
Swinging the body to initiate the movement — momentum rather than muscle, reduce weight.
Raising dumbbells too high — above shoulder height shifts load to upper traps.
Arms straight — elbow joint stress and reduced lateral deltoid activation.
Rushing the lowering phase — the eccentric is where a significant portion of the stimulus comes from.
Tilting the dumbbell upward at the top — activates upper traps instead of lateral deltoid.
Injury contraindications:
Shoulder impingement — reduce range of motion, stop before shoulder height.
Rotator cuff issues — use very light weight and monitor carefully.
AC joint issues — pain at the top of the range indicates AC joint involvement, reduce range of motion.
Substitutions: Lateral raise machine / Cable lateral raise / Resistance band lateral raise (home training)
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/lateral-raise-dumbbell
Notes: One of the most important exercises for shoulder width development. The lateral deltoid creates the wide shoulder look — chronically underdeveloped in most clients because lateral raises are performed incorrectly. The pouring water cue and the slow lowering phase are the two most important coaching points. Partials are a standard progression tool here.
Exercise 16: Lateral Raise — Machine
Primary muscle: Shoulders — lateral deltoid
Secondary muscles: Upper traps minimal
Equipment: Lateral raise machine
Experience level: All
Priority: Primary
Rep ranges: Heavy set 10–12 reps. Back off set 15–20 reps.
Coaching cues:
Adjust the seat so the pivot point of the machine aligns with the shoulder joint — critical for correct muscle activation.
Sit upright, back pressed into the pad, feet flat on the floor.
Push the pads outward and upward in a smooth arc — lead with the elbows.
At the top pause briefly and squeeze the lateral deltoid.
Lower slowly under full control — 3–4 second lowering phase.
Keep the traps relaxed throughout — shrugging indicates the weight is too heavy or the seat is set incorrectly.
Common mistakes:
Seat height wrong — pivot point misalignment significantly reduces lateral deltoid activation.
Shrugging — upper traps taking over, adjust seat or reduce weight.
Rushing the lowering phase — loses the eccentric stimulus.
Leaning forward off the pad — cheating the movement.
Injury contraindications:
Shoulder impingement — reduce range of motion using the machine’s range limiter if available.
AC joint issues — monitor carefully, machine variation is more controlled than dumbbell.
Rotator cuff issues — light weight only, monitor carefully.
Substitutions: Lateral raise dumbbell / Cable lateral raise
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/lateral-raise-machine
Notes: Machine variation of the lateral raise. Excellent for maintaining tension throughout the movement. The seat adjustment is the most important setup step — always check it before starting.
Exercise 17: Cable Lateral Raise
Primary muscle: Shoulders — lateral deltoid
Secondary muscles: Upper traps minimal
Equipment: Cable machine, single handle attachment
Experience level: All
Priority: Primary
Rep ranges: Heavy set 12–15 reps. Back off set 15–20 reps.
Coaching cues:
Set the cable to the lowest position — creates constant tension throughout the full range of motion unlike dumbbells which have no tension at the bottom.
Stand side on to the cable machine — the cable crosses in front of or behind the body.
Slight bend in the elbow throughout.
Raise the arm out to the side in an arc — elbow leads, not the hand.
At shoulder height pause and squeeze the lateral deltoid.
Lower slowly under full control — the cable provides resistance throughout the lowering phase.
Common mistakes:
Cable set too high — reduces the range of motion and the benefit of constant tension.
Swinging the body — momentum rather than muscle.
Rushing the lowering phase — cable resistance on the way down is valuable, use it.
Standing too close to the machine — limits range of motion.
Injury contraindications:
Shoulder impingement — reduce range of motion.
Rotator cuff issues — light weight, monitor carefully.
Substitutions: Lateral raise dumbbell / Lateral raise machine
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/cable-lateral-raise
Notes: The cable lateral raise provides constant tension throughout the full range of motion — unlike dumbbells which have no tension at the bottom. This makes it an excellent variation for maximising lateral deltoid stimulus.
Exercise 18: Front Raise
Primary muscle: Shoulders — anterior deltoid
Secondary muscles: Upper chest, upper traps
Equipment: Dumbbells, barbell or plate
Experience level: All
Priority: Secondary
Rep ranges: Heavy set 10–12 reps. Back off set 12–15 reps.
Coaching cues:
Stand with dumbbells or a plate at the front of the thighs, slight bend in the elbows.
Raise the weight directly in front of the body to shoulder height — no higher.
Keep the movement controlled — no swinging or body momentum.
Lower slowly under full control.
Alternate arms if using dumbbells for greater time under tension or perform both arms simultaneously.
Common mistakes:
Raising above shoulder height — increases shoulder impingement risk.
Swinging the body — momentum rather than muscle.
Using too much weight — the anterior deltoid is already heavily stimulated by pressing movements.
Rushing the movement — slow and controlled maximises stimulus.
Injury contraindications:
Shoulder impingement — avoid, the anterior deltoid is already stressed in this condition.
Rotator cuff issues — avoid or use very light weight only.
Substitutions: Cable front raise — constant tension variation / Plate front raise
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/front-raise
Notes: Secondary movement — the anterior deltoid receives significant stimulus from all pressing movements. Front raises are used as an accessory when the anterior deltoid is identified as lagging. Not used in every session.
Exercise 19: Upright Row
Primary muscle: Shoulders — lateral deltoid and upper traps
Secondary muscles: Biceps, rear deltoid
Equipment: Barbell, dumbbells, cable or EZ bar
Experience level: Intermediate and advanced
Priority: Secondary
Rep ranges: Heavy set 8–10 reps. Back off set 12–15 reps.
Coaching cues:
Grip the bar slightly narrower than shoulder width — too wide reduces range of motion, too narrow increases impingement risk.
Pull the bar upward along the body — elbows drive up and outward, higher than the hands throughout.
At the top elbows should be at or slightly above shoulder height — the bar at mid chest to chin level.
Lower slowly under full control.
Keep the bar close to the body throughout the movement.
EZ bar or dumbbell variation recommended over straight barbell — more comfortable on the wrists and shoulders.
Common mistakes:
Grip too wide — reduces range of motion significantly.
Grip too narrow — increases shoulder impingement risk.
Elbows not higher than hands — defeats the purpose of the movement.
Pulling the bar too high — chin level is sufficient, going higher increases impingement risk.
Using straight barbell — EZ bar or dumbbells are more joint friendly.
Injury contraindications:
Shoulder impingement — avoid, one of the highest risk exercises for shoulder impingement.
Rotator cuff issues — avoid entirely.
Wrist issues — EZ bar variation only or avoid.
Substitutions: Lateral raise — safer lateral deltoid alternative / Face pull — safer upper trap and rear deltoid alternative
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/upright-row
Notes: Intermediate and advanced clients only. EZ bar or dumbbell variation always preferred over straight barbell. Monitor carefully for any shoulder discomfort.
Exercise 20: Face Pull
Primary muscle: Shoulders — rear deltoid and rotator cuff
Secondary muscles: Upper traps, rhomboids
Equipment: Cable machine, rope attachment set at face height
Experience level: All
Priority: Secondary on push day — included in warm up; working exercise on pull day
Rep ranges: Warm up — 15–20 reps light weight, no rest. Working sets — 12–15 reps. Back off set 15–20 reps.
Coaching cues:
Set the cable at face height or slightly above — the rope pulls toward the face not downward.
Grip the rope with both hands, thumbs pointing toward you.
Pull the rope toward the face — elbows drive back and outward, higher than the hands.
At the end position the hands should be at ear level — elbows wide and high.
Squeeze the rear deltoid and rotator cuff hard at the end position — pause for one second.
Return slowly under control — the eccentric is important for shoulder health.
Common mistakes:
Cable set too low — turns it into a row, loses the rear deltoid and rotator cuff emphasis.
Elbows dropping below hands — same issue, becomes a row.
Rushing the movement — face pulls are a shoulder health exercise as much as a strength exercise, control is essential.
Too much weight — reduces range of motion and control, defeats the purpose.
Injury contraindications:
Face pulls are generally safe for most shoulder conditions and are often used in rehabilitation.
Severe rotator cuff tears — consult physiotherapist before performing.
Substitutions: Rear delt fly / Band pull apart (warm up alternative) / Reverse cable fly
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/face-pull
Notes: Double function — warm up tool and working exercise. Always included in the push day warm up for shoulder health. Also programmed as a working exercise on pull day. One of the most important exercises for long term shoulder health. Light weight, high reps, full control always.
PUSH DAY — TRICEPS
Exercise 21: Skull Crushers
Primary muscle: Triceps — long head emphasis
Secondary muscles: None significant
Equipment: EZ bar, straight barbell or dumbbells, flat bench
Experience level: All
Priority: Primary
Rep ranges: Heavy set 8–10 reps. Back off set 12–15 reps.
Coaching cues:
Lie flat on the bench, arms extended directly above the shoulders — not above the chest.
Lower the bar toward the forehead or just behind the head.
Keep the upper arms completely still throughout — only the forearms move.
Lower slowly under full control — rushing skull crushers is a genuine injury risk.
Drive back up to the start position — squeeze the tricep at the top.
EZ bar is preferred over straight barbell — more comfortable on the wrists and elbows.
Common mistakes:
Upper arms moving — elbows flaring out or drifting back removes the tricep isolation.
Rushing the lowering phase — the bar moving toward the skull requires absolute control.
Straight barbell over EZ bar — unnecessary wrist and elbow stress.
Locking out at the top — keep slight tension on the tricep throughout.
Injury contraindications:
Elbow issues — reduce range of motion or switch to cable overhead extension.
Wrist issues — EZ bar only or switch to dumbbell variation.
Shoulder issues — the overhead position can aggravate some shoulder conditions, monitor carefully.
Substitutions: Cable tricep pushdown / Overhead cable extension / JM press
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/skull-crushers
Notes: One of the best long head tricep exercises available. The long head of the tricep is stretched at the bottom making it particularly effective for overall tricep mass. EZ bar always preferred. Control is non negotiable.
Exercise 22: JM Press
Primary muscle: Triceps — all heads
Secondary muscles: Chest minimal
Equipment: EZ bar or straight barbell, flat bench
Experience level: Intermediate and advanced
Priority: Secondary
Rep ranges: Heavy set 6–8 reps. Back off set 10–12 reps.
Coaching cues:
Lie flat on the bench, bar positioned above the chest as in a bench press.
Lower the bar toward the throat — halfway between a bench press and a skull crusher.
As the bar lowers the elbows tuck in and forward — this is the key difference from a skull crusher.
The upper arms are not vertical — they angle slightly forward toward the face.
Drive back up explosively — the bar path returns to above the chest.
Keep the movement controlled — the bar is moving toward the throat which demands respect.
Common mistakes:
Going too heavy — technically demanding and requires lighter weight than expected.
Elbows not tucking — turns it into a close grip bench press rather than a JM press.
Rushing — bar moving toward the throat requires full control at all times.
Not understanding the movement before attempting — watch a demonstration first.
Injury contraindications:
Elbow issues — avoid, significant stress on the elbow joint.
Shoulder issues — monitor carefully.
Beginners — not programmed, too technically demanding.
Substitutions: Skull crushers / Close grip bench press / Cable tricep pushdown
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/jm-press
Notes: A hybrid movement between a skull crusher and a close grip bench press. Allows heavier loading than skull crushers while maintaining significant tricep isolation. Intermediate and advanced clients only.
Exercise 23: Close Grip Bench Press
Primary muscle: Triceps — all heads
Secondary muscles: Chest, front deltoid
Equipment: Barbell, flat bench, squat rack
Experience level: Intermediate and advanced
Priority: Secondary
Rep ranges: Heavy set 6–8 reps. Back off set 10–12 reps.
Coaching cues:
Grip the bar with hands shoulder width apart or slightly narrower — not too narrow as this increases wrist stress.
Same setup as regular bench press — feet flat, shoulder blades retracted, slight arch.
Lower the bar to the lower chest — slightly lower than regular bench press.
Keep elbows tucked close to the body throughout — this shifts emphasis from chest to triceps.
Drive back up explosively — squeeze the triceps at the top.
Never grip too narrow — a grip width that causes wrist pain defeats the purpose.
Common mistakes:
Grip too narrow — wrist stress without additional tricep benefit.
Elbows flaring — becomes a regular bench press, loses the tricep emphasis.
Lowering to mid chest rather than lower chest — reduces tricep range of motion.
Too much weight — ego lifting on close grip removes the tricep isolation.
Injury contraindications:
Elbow issues — monitor carefully, reduce range of motion if pain occurs.
Wrist issues — adjust grip width, consider wrist wraps.
Beginners — not programmed until regular bench press pattern is established.
Substitutions: Skull crushers / Tricep pushdown / JM press
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/close-grip-bench-press
Notes: A compound tricep movement that allows heavier loading than isolation exercises. Used as a secondary tricep movement — skull crushers lead the tricep work in most sessions.
Exercise 24: Tricep Pushdown — Rope
Primary muscle: Triceps — all heads, lateral head emphasis at full extension
Secondary muscles: None significant
Equipment: Cable machine, rope attachment
Experience level: All
Priority: Primary — default pushdown variation
Rep ranges: Heavy set 10–12 reps. Back off set 15–20 reps.
Coaching cues:
Set cable to the highest position.
Grip the rope with both hands, thumbs pointing upward.
Keep the upper arms completely still and close to the body throughout — only the forearms move.
Push the rope downward and slightly outward at the bottom — splitting the rope maximises lateral head activation and full tricep contraction.
At the bottom squeeze the triceps hard — hold for one second.
Return slowly under control — don’t let the weight pull the elbows up.
Common mistakes:
Upper arms moving — elbows drifting forward or upward removes the tricep isolation.
Not splitting the rope at the bottom — misses the full contraction.
Leaning excessively forward — using bodyweight rather than tricep strength.
Rushing — the tricep pushdown responds well to slow controlled reps.
Injury contraindications:
Elbow issues — reduce weight and range of motion, monitor carefully.
Wrist issues — rope attachment is generally comfortable as it allows wrist rotation.
Substitutions: Tricep pushdown V bar / Tricep pushdown EZ bar / Overhead cable extension
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/tricep-pushdown-rope
Notes: Default tricep pushdown variation. The rope allows the hands to split at the bottom for a full tricep contraction that straight bar variations cannot achieve. Used in the majority of tricep pushdown slots.
Exercise 25: Tricep Pushdown — V Bar
Primary muscle: Triceps — all heads
Secondary muscles: None significant
Equipment: Cable machine, V bar attachment
Experience level: All
Priority: Secondary
Rep ranges: Heavy set 10–12 reps. Back off set 15–20 reps.
Coaching cues:
Set cable to the highest position.
Grip the V bar with a neutral grip — palms facing each other.
Upper arms fixed close to the body — only forearms move.
Push straight down to full extension — squeeze the triceps hard at the bottom.
Return slowly under control.
The fixed bar path allows slightly heavier loading than the rope variation.
Common mistakes:
Upper arms drifting — same error as all pushdown variations.
Partial range of motion — must achieve full elbow extension at the bottom.
Leaning too far forward — momentum rather than tricep strength.
Injury contraindications:
Elbow issues — monitor carefully.
Wrist issues — neutral grip is generally comfortable but monitor.
Substitutions: Tricep pushdown rope — default alternative / Tricep pushdown EZ bar
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/tricep-pushdown-v-bar
Notes: Secondary pushdown variation — rotated in for variety. The fixed V bar allows slightly heavier loading than the rope and some clients find the neutral grip more comfortable.
Exercise 26: Tricep Pushdown — EZ Bar
Primary muscle: Triceps — all heads, medial head emphasis
Secondary muscles: None significant
Equipment: Cable machine, EZ bar attachment
Experience level: All
Priority: Secondary
Rep ranges: Heavy set 10–12 reps. Back off set 15–20 reps.
Coaching cues:
Set cable to the highest position.
Grip the EZ bar at the angled sections — more comfortable on the wrists than a straight bar.
Upper arms fixed close to the body — only forearms move.
Push straight down to full extension — squeeze the triceps at the bottom.
Return slowly under control.
The EZ bar allows a pronated grip which emphasises the medial head of the tricep.
Common mistakes:
Using a straight bar instead of EZ bar — unnecessary wrist stress.
Upper arms drifting.
Partial range of motion.
Injury contraindications:
Elbow issues — monitor carefully.
Wrist issues — EZ bar is the most wrist friendly bar variation but monitor.
Substitutions: Tricep pushdown rope / Tricep pushdown V bar
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/tricep-pushdown-ez-bar
Notes: Third pushdown variation — rotated in for variety. Emphasises the medial head of the tricep through the pronated grip.
Exercise 27: Overhead Tricep Extension — Cable
Primary muscle: Triceps — long head emphasis
Secondary muscles: None significant
Equipment: Cable machine, rope attachment, cable set to lowest position
Experience level: All
Priority: Primary
Rep ranges: Heavy set 10–12 reps. Back off set 15–20 reps.
Coaching cues:
Set the cable to the lowest position — the resistance pulls downward and backward.
Face away from the cable machine, grip the rope with both hands behind the head.
Upper arms pointing straight up — elbows close to the head throughout.
Extend the forearms upward and forward to full extension — squeeze the triceps hard at the top.
Lower slowly under control — feel the stretch in the long head of the tricep at the bottom.
Keep the core braced — the overhead position challenges stability.
Common mistakes:
Elbows flaring outward — reduces long head isolation.
Upper arms not vertical — changes the angle of resistance and reduces the stretch.
Rushing the lowering phase — the stretch in the long head at the bottom is the most valuable part.
Too much weight — elbows will flare and upper arms will drop, control is lost.
Injury contraindications:
Elbow issues — reduce range of motion, monitor carefully.
Shoulder issues — the overhead position may aggravate some shoulder conditions, monitor carefully.
Neck issues — keep the head neutral, don’t crane forward.
Substitutions: Overhead tricep extension dumbbell / Skull crushers / Tricep pushdown if overhead position is uncomfortable
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/overhead-tricep-extension-cable
Notes: The overhead position fully stretches the long head of the tricep — the largest of the three tricep heads. Cable variation preferred over dumbbell as the resistance is constant throughout. Primary rating reflects its importance for long head development.
Exercise 28: Overhead Tricep Extension — Dumbbell
Primary muscle: Triceps — long head emphasis
Secondary muscles: None significant
Equipment: Single dumbbell or two dumbbells
Experience level: Intermediate and advanced
Priority: Specialist
Rep ranges: Heavy set 10–12 reps. Back off set 15–20 reps.
Coaching cues:
Sit or stand holding one dumbbell with both hands — grip the inner plate with thumbs wrapped around the handle for safety.
Raise the dumbbell overhead — upper arms vertical, elbows close to the head.
Lower the dumbbell behind the head slowly — feel the stretch in the long head of the tricep.
Extend back to overhead — squeeze the triceps hard at the top.
Elbows stay close to the head throughout — no flaring.
Alternative — one dumbbell in each hand, same movement pattern.
Common mistakes:
Grip insecure — always grip the inner plate firmly with both hands, a dropped dumbbell overhead is dangerous.
Elbows flaring — reduces long head isolation.
Upper arms not vertical — same issue as cable variation.
Too much weight — control is essential with a dumbbell overhead.
Injury contraindications:
Elbow issues — monitor carefully.
Shoulder issues — overhead position may aggravate, monitor carefully.
Beginners — not programmed, the overhead dumbbell position requires body awareness and control.
Substitutions: Overhead cable extension — preferred primary variation / Skull crushers / Tricep pushdown
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/overhead-tricep-extension-dumbbell
Notes: Specialist rating — used when cable machine is unavailable or for variety. Cable variation is preferred as the resistance is constant. Intermediate and advanced clients only.
Exercise 29: Cable Tricep Kickback
Primary muscle: Triceps — lateral head emphasis
Secondary muscles: None significant
Equipment: Cable machine, single handle attachment, cable set low
Experience level: All
Priority: Primary
Rep ranges: Heavy set 12–15 reps. Back off set 15–20 reps.
Coaching cues:
Set cable to the lowest position, grip the single handle.
Hinge forward at the hips — torso roughly parallel to the floor.
Upper arm close to the body and parallel to the floor — this position is held throughout.
Extend the forearm backward to full extension — squeeze the tricep hard at full extension.
Lower slowly under control — the cable provides resistance throughout the return.
Perform one arm at a time for maximum isolation and focus.
Common mistakes:
Upper arm not parallel to the floor — the most common error, significantly reduces tricep activation.
Swinging the arm — momentum rather than muscle.
Not achieving full extension — the contraction at full extension is the entire point of the movement.
Standing upright — the hinge position is essential for correct muscle activation.
Injury contraindications:
Elbow issues — monitor carefully.
Lower back issues — the hinge position places demand on the lower back, reduce range of motion or avoid.
Substitutions: Dumbbell tricep kickback / Tricep pushdown / Overhead cable extension
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/cable-tricep-kickback
Notes: Excellent lateral head tricep isolation exercise. The cable provides constant tension throughout unlike the dumbbell variation. Performed one arm at a time. The upper arm position parallel to the floor is the most important coaching point.
Exercise 30: Dumbbell Tricep Kickback
Primary muscle: Triceps — lateral head emphasis
Secondary muscles: None significant
Equipment: Dumbbell, flat bench for support
Experience level: All
Priority: Specialist
Rep ranges: Heavy set 12–15 reps. Back off set 15–20 reps.
Coaching cues:
Place one hand and same side knee on a flat bench for support — opposite foot on the floor.
Upper arm close to the body and parallel to the floor — hold this position throughout.
Extend the dumbbell backward to full extension — squeeze the tricep hard.
Lower slowly — control the dumbbell back to 90 degrees at the elbow.
Perform all reps on one side before switching.
Common mistakes:
Upper arm not parallel to the floor — same critical error as cable variation.
Swinging — momentum removes the isolation.
Not achieving full extension.
Using too heavy a dumbbell — control is lost, upper arm drops.
Injury contraindications:
Elbow issues — monitor carefully.
Lower back issues — the hinge position places demand on the lower back.
Substitutions: Cable tricep kickback — preferred variation / Tricep pushdown
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/dumbbell-tricep-kickback
Notes: Specialist rating — cable kickback is preferred as it provides constant tension. Dumbbell variation used when cable machine is unavailable or for variety.
Exercise 31: Tricep Dips Between Benches
Primary muscle: Triceps — all heads
Secondary muscles: Front deltoid, chest minimal
Equipment: Two flat benches
Experience level: Beginners
Priority: Specialist
Rep ranges: Standard 10–15 reps. Progression — add a plate on the lap for resistance.
Coaching cues:
Place hands on the edge of one bench behind the body, feet on the edge of the second bench in front.
Keep the back close to the bench — not drifting forward away from it.
Lower the body by bending the elbows — straight down, not forward.
At the bottom elbows should be at 90 degrees — no deeper.
Drive back up to full extension — squeeze the triceps at the top.
Keep the shoulders down — don’t shrug throughout the movement.
Common mistakes:
Back drifting away from the bench — turns it into a shoulder exercise.
Going too deep — excessive shoulder stress at the bottom.
Shrugging — upper traps taking over.
Feet too far away — increases the load before the client is ready.
Injury contraindications:
Shoulder impingement — avoid, significant stress on the shoulder at the bottom.
AC joint issues — avoid.
Wrist issues — the wrist position can be uncomfortable, monitor carefully.
Substitutions: Weighted dips on dip bars / Tricep pushdown / Close grip bench press
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/tricep-dips-between-benches
Notes: Specialist rating — used for beginners who cannot yet perform full dips on dip bars. A stepping stone exercise toward full dips.
Exercise 32: Dips — Weighted
Primary muscle: Triceps — all heads with upright torso / Chest with forward lean
Secondary muscles: Front deltoid, chest or triceps depending on torso angle
Equipment: Dip bars, dip belt for weighted variation, assisted dip machine for beginners
Experience level: All — assisted for beginners, bodyweight for intermediate, weighted for advanced
Priority: Primary
Rep ranges: Beginners assisted — 10–15 reps. Intermediate bodyweight — 8–12 reps. Advanced weighted — 6–10 reps.
Coaching cues:
For tricep emphasis — keep torso upright throughout, elbows close to the body.
For chest emphasis — lean forward, elbows flare slightly outward.
Lower until shoulders are below elbows — full range of motion.
Drive back up to full extension — squeeze at the top.
For weighted — use a dip belt with plates hanging between the legs, or hold a dumbbell between the feet.
For assisted — set the counterweight appropriately, reduce it progressively as strength improves.
Common mistakes:
Insufficient depth — not getting shoulders below elbows significantly reduces stimulus.
Too much weight too soon — master bodyweight before adding load.
Shrugging at the top — traps taking over, reduce weight.
Not controlling the descent — losing control under bodyweight or added weight is a serious injury risk.
Injury contraindications:
Shoulder impingement — avoid, the bottom position is particularly stressful on an impinged shoulder.
AC joint issues — avoid.
Elbow issues — monitor carefully, reduce range of motion if pain occurs.
Substitutions: Close grip bench press / Tricep pushdown / Tricep dips between benches (beginner stepping stone)
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/dips-weighted
Notes: Primary rating across all experience levels. The progression from assisted to bodyweight to weighted makes this suitable for everyone. On push day the forward lean chest emphasis version is the default.
PULL DAY — BACK
Exercise 33: T Bar Row
Primary muscle: Back — mid back, rhomboids, lats
Secondary muscles: Biceps, rear deltoid, lower back
Equipment: T bar row machine or landmine attachment with V grip handle
Experience level: All
Priority: Primary
Rep ranges: Heavy set 6–8 reps. Back off set 10–12 reps.
Coaching cues:
Set up with chest against the pad if using a chest supported T bar — this removes lower back demand.
Grip the handles and retract the shoulder blades before the first rep — initiate every rep with the back not the arms.
Pull the handles toward the lower chest — elbows drive back and slightly outward.
At the top squeeze the mid back hard — hold for one second.
Lower slowly under full control — feel the stretch across the back at full arm extension.
Keep the chest pressed against the pad throughout — don’t let the torso rise off it.
Common mistakes:
Initiating with the arms rather than the back — biceps fatigue before the back is properly stimulated.
Not retracting the shoulder blades — reduces mid back activation significantly.
Rushing the lowering phase — loses the eccentric stimulus.
Too much weight — torso rises off the pad, lower back takes over.
Injury contraindications:
Lower back issues — chest supported variation only, never the standing variation.
Shoulder issues — monitor range of motion, reduce if pain occurs.
Bicep tendon issues — monitor carefully, reduce weight.
Substitutions: Seated cable row / Chest supported row / Barbell bent over row
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/t-bar-row
Notes: One of the best mid back exercises available. The chest supported variation removes lower back involvement entirely. Default primary rowing movement. Always initiate with the back — cue is to think about driving the elbows back rather than pulling with the hands.
Exercise 34: Lat Pulldown
Primary muscle: Back — latissimus dorsi
Secondary muscles: Biceps, rear deltoid, rhomboids
Equipment: Lat pulldown machine, wide grip bar attachment
Experience level: All
Priority: Primary
Rep ranges: Heavy set 8–10 reps. Back off set 12–15 reps.
Coaching cues:
Adjust the knee pad so the thighs are locked in securely — prevents the body from rising during heavy sets.
Grip the bar slightly wider than shoulder width — hands just outside the angled sections of a standard lat bar.
Lean back very slightly — 10–15 degrees — this puts the lats in a better pulling angle.
Initiate the pull by depressing the shoulder blades downward — think about pulling the elbows down to the hips not pulling the bar to the chest.
Pull the bar to the upper chest — not behind the neck, never behind the neck.
Lower slowly under full control — full arm extension at the top to maximise lat stretch.
Common mistakes:
Pulling behind the neck — serious cervical spine and shoulder injury risk, always pull to the front.
Not achieving full arm extension at the top — misses the lat stretch which is critical for lat development.
Initiating with the arms — biceps fatigue before the lats are properly stimulated.
Excessive backward lean — turns it into a row rather than a pulldown.
Kipping or using momentum — reduces lat stimulus significantly.
Injury contraindications:
Shoulder impingement — reduce range of motion, pull to chin height rather than upper chest.
Elbow issues — monitor carefully, wide grip places less stress on the elbow than close grip.
Never pull behind the neck regardless of experience level — excluded for all clients.
Substitutions: MagGrip lat pulldown / Wide grip row / Pull ups
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/lat-pulldown
Notes: The fundamental lat building exercise. Full arm extension at the top is the most important coaching point. The cue to pull the elbows down to the hips rather than pulling the bar to the chest dramatically improves lat activation for most clients.
Exercise 35: MagGrip Lat Pulldown
Primary muscle: Back — latissimus dorsi, varied emphasis based on attachment used
Secondary muscles: Biceps, rear deltoid, rhomboids
Equipment: MagGrip attachment system, lat pulldown machine
Experience level: All
Priority: Primary — equipment dependent
Rep ranges: Heavy set 8–10 reps. Back off set 12–15 reps.
Coaching cues:
Select the MagGrip attachment that suits the target — wide grip for overall lat width, neutral grip for lat thickness, close grip for lower lat emphasis.
Same setup as standard lat pulldown — knee pad secured, slight backward lean.
The rotating MagGrip handles allow the wrists to move naturally through the pull — use this freedom rather than fighting it.
Initiate with the shoulder blades depressing — elbows drive down to the hips.
Pull to upper chest — full range of motion.
Lower slowly to full arm extension.
Common mistakes:
Not using the rotating handles — defeats the purpose of the MagGrip system.
Same errors as standard lat pulldown apply — momentum, partial range, pulling behind neck.
Injury contraindications:
Same as standard lat pulldown.
Wrist issues — the rotating handles are generally more comfortable than fixed bars.
Substitutions: Standard lat pulldown / Wide grip row / Pull ups
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/maggrip-lat-pulldown
Notes: Equipment dependent — only programmed when the client confirms their gym has MagGrip attachments. The rotating handles allow a more natural wrist and elbow path reducing joint stress.
Exercise 36: Cybex Row
Primary muscle: Back — mid back, rhomboids, lats
Secondary muscles: Biceps, rear deltoid
Equipment: Cybex row machine
Experience level: All
Priority: Primary — equipment dependent
Rep ranges: Heavy set 8–10 reps. Back off set 12–15 reps.
Coaching cues:
Adjust the chest pad and seat so the handles are at mid chest height when seated.
Press the chest firmly into the pad before starting — keep it there throughout.
Initiate with the shoulder blades retracting — think about squeezing the shoulder blades together.
Pull the handles toward the body — elbows drive back and slightly outward.
Squeeze the mid back hard at the end position — hold for one second.
Return slowly to full arm extension — feel the stretch across the back.
Common mistakes:
Chest coming off the pad — lower back taking over, reduce weight.
Initiating with arms — biceps fatigue before the back is stimulated.
Partial range of motion — must achieve full arm extension and full contraction.
Rushing — the Cybex row responds well to slow controlled reps.
Injury contraindications:
Lower back issues — chest supported machine, generally very safe.
Shoulder issues — monitor range of motion.
Bicep tendon issues — monitor carefully.
Substitutions: T bar row / Seated cable row / Chest supported row
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/cybex-row
Notes: Equipment dependent — only programmed when the client confirms their gym has a Cybex row machine. An excellent chest supported rowing machine. The fixed movement path makes it beginner friendly while still providing enough resistance for advanced clients.
Exercise 37: Cable Pullover
Primary muscle: Back — latissimus dorsi
Secondary muscles: Chest, triceps, core
Equipment: Cable machine, rope or straight bar attachment, cable set high
Experience level: Intermediate and advanced
Priority: Primary
Rep ranges: Heavy set 10–12 reps. Back off set 12–15 reps.
Coaching cues:
Set the cable to the highest position, attach a rope or straight bar.
Stand facing the cable machine, step back to create tension, hinge slightly at the hips.
Keep the arms straight throughout — this is not a row, the elbows stay extended.
Pull the rope or bar downward and toward the hips in a sweeping arc.
At the bottom squeeze the lats hard — the hands should be at hip level.
Return slowly along the same arc — feel the stretch in the lats at the top.
Common mistakes:
Bending the elbows — turns it into a pushdown, loses the lat isolation.
Standing too close to the machine — reduces the range of motion.
Rushing — the arc movement requires control throughout.
Not hinging at the hips — reduces the stretch at the top of the movement.
Injury contraindications:
Shoulder issues — the overhead stretch position can aggravate some shoulder conditions, monitor carefully.
Elbow issues — keeping the elbows straight places demand on the elbow joint, monitor.
Substitutions: Straight arm pulldown / Lat pulldown / Pull ups
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/cable-pullover
Notes: An excellent lat isolation exercise that keeps the arms straight throughout — this removes bicep involvement and forces the lats to do all the work. Intermediate and advanced clients — beginners often struggle to feel the lat activation with straight arms.
Exercise 38: Low Row
Primary muscle: Back — lower lats, mid back
Secondary muscles: Biceps, rear deltoid
Equipment: Low row machine or cable machine set low with V grip or wide grip
Experience level: All
Priority: Primary
Rep ranges: Heavy set 8–10 reps. Back off set 12–15 reps.
Coaching cues:
Sit upright with feet on the footplate, slight bend in the knees.
Grip the handle and sit tall — don’t lean back excessively before pulling.
Initiate by retracting the shoulder blades — pull the elbows back toward the hips.
At the end position squeeze the lower lats — elbows should be past the torso.
Return slowly to full arm extension — sit tall throughout, don’t lean forward excessively on the return.
Keep the core braced — the lower row position challenges spinal stability.
Common mistakes:
Excessive backward lean at the start — uses momentum and lower back rather than lats.
Excessive forward lean on the return — adds unnecessary lower back stress.
Not achieving full arm extension — misses the lat stretch.
Pulling to the upper chest rather than the lower chest and hips — reduces lower lat activation.
Injury contraindications:
Lower back issues — monitor carefully, reduce range of motion of the torso lean.
Bicep tendon issues — monitor.
Substitutions: Seated cable row / T bar row / Chest supported row
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/low-row
Notes: Targets the lower lats specifically — an area that is often underdeveloped. The pulling angle toward the hips rather than the upper chest is the key difference from a standard seated row. Keep the torso movement minimal.
Exercise 39: Wide Grip Row
Primary muscle: Back — upper lats, rhomboids, rear deltoid
Secondary muscles: Biceps, upper traps
Equipment: Cable machine with wide grip bar
Experience level: All
Priority: Primary
Rep ranges: Heavy set 8–10 reps. Back off set 12–15 reps.
Coaching cues:
Grip the bar wider than shoulder width — the wide grip shifts emphasis to the upper lats and rear deltoid.
Sit upright, feet on the footplate, slight knee bend.
Retract the shoulder blades before pulling — initiate with the back.
Pull the bar toward the upper chest — the wide grip naturally pulls the bar higher than a close grip row.
At the end position squeeze the upper back hard — elbows flare slightly outward.
Return slowly to full arm extension.
Common mistakes:
Grip not wide enough — becomes a standard row, loses the upper lat and rear deltoid emphasis.
Pulling to the lower chest or stomach — changes the muscle emphasis.
Not retracting shoulder blades — reduces upper back activation.
Excessive torso movement — keep the back stable.
Injury contraindications:
Shoulder issues — wide grip rows place the shoulder in a wider position, reduce range of motion if discomfort occurs.
Elbow issues — monitor carefully, wide grip places less elbow stress than close grip.
Substitutions: Seated cable row — close grip alternative / T bar row / Lat pulldown
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/wide-grip-row
Notes: The wide grip changes the muscle emphasis significantly — more upper lat, more rear deltoid, less lower lat and bicep. Good for clients who want more upper back and rear deltoid development.
Exercise 40: Cable Row
Primary muscle: Back — mid lats, rhomboids
Secondary muscles: Biceps, rear deltoid, lower back
Equipment: Cable machine, various attachments
Experience level: All
Priority: Primary
Rep ranges: Heavy set 8–10 reps. Back off set 12–15 reps.
Coaching cues:
Sit upright on the cable row station, feet on the footplate.
Select attachment based on target — V grip for mid back thickness, wide grip for upper back, rope for rear deltoid emphasis.
Retract shoulder blades before pulling — back initiates the movement.
Pull toward the lower chest or stomach — elbows drive back close to the body.
Squeeze the mid back at full contraction — hold for one second.
Return slowly to full arm extension — feel the stretch.
Common mistakes:
Same errors as all rowing movements — momentum, partial range, not initiating with the back.
Not varying the attachment — using the same grip every session reduces the variety of stimulus across the back.
Injury contraindications:
Lower back issues — keep torso movement minimal, brace the core.
Bicep tendon issues — monitor.
Substitutions: Seated cable row / T bar row / Chest supported row
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/cable-row
Notes: Versatile exercise — the attachment determines the emphasis. V grip for mid back thickness, wide grip for upper back width, rope for rear deltoid. Rotating attachments across sessions provides variety without changing the exercise.
Exercise 41: Seated Cable Row
Primary muscle: Back — mid lats, rhomboids
Secondary muscles: Biceps, rear deltoid
Equipment: Seated cable row machine, V grip attachment
Experience level: All
Priority: Primary
Rep ranges: Heavy set 8–10 reps. Back off set 12–15 reps.
Coaching cues:
Sit upright with feet on the footplate, slight bend in the knees.
Grip the V handle and sit tall before pulling.
Retract the shoulder blades — initiate with the back not the arms.
Pull the handle toward the lower chest — elbows drive straight back close to the body.
At full contraction squeeze the mid back hard.
Return slowly — sit tall on the return, minimal torso movement throughout.
Common mistakes:
Leaning back excessively — momentum and lower back rather than lat strength.
Leaning forward excessively on return — unnecessary lower back stress.
Arms initiating — biceps fatigue too early.
Not sitting tall — slouching reduces the range of motion and the mid back activation.
Injury contraindications:
Lower back issues — keep torso movement to a minimum, brace the core throughout.
Bicep tendon issues — monitor.
Substitutions: Chest supported row — removes lower back demand entirely / T bar row / Cable row
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/seated-cable-row
Notes: A fundamental mid back exercise. The seated position and V grip make this accessible for all experience levels. Default close grip rowing movement.
Exercise 42: Chest Supported Row
Primary muscle: Back — mid back, rhomboids, lats
Secondary muscles: Biceps, rear deltoid
Equipment: Chest supported row machine or incline bench with dumbbells
Experience level: All
Priority: Primary
Rep ranges: Heavy set 8–10 reps. Back off set 12–15 reps.
Coaching cues:
Set the chest pad so the chest is fully supported — the pad removes all lower back demand.
Grip the handles and press the chest firmly into the pad before starting.
Retract the shoulder blades — initiate with the back.
Pull the handles toward the lower chest — elbows drive back and slightly outward.
Squeeze the mid back hard at full contraction.
Lower slowly to full arm extension — feel the stretch across the back.
Common mistakes:
Chest coming off the pad — lower back compensating, reduce weight.
Initiating with arms.
Partial range of motion — must achieve full extension and full contraction.
Shrugging — upper traps taking over, keep the shoulders down throughout.
Injury contraindications:
Lower back issues — this is the safest rowing variation for lower back problems, highly recommended.
Shoulder issues — monitor range of motion.
Substitutions: T bar row chest supported variation / Seated cable row / Dumbbell row on incline bench
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/chest-supported-row
Notes: The chest supported position eliminates lower back involvement entirely — the safest rowing variation for clients with lower back issues. Allows heavier loading than standing or seated rows because the lower back is removed from the equation.
Exercise 43: Barbell Bent Over Row
Primary muscle: Back — mid back, lats, rhomboids
Secondary muscles: Biceps, rear deltoid, lower back, hamstrings
Equipment: Barbell
Experience level: Intermediate and advanced
Priority: Secondary
Rep ranges: Heavy set 6–8 reps. Back off set 10–12 reps.
Coaching cues:
Hinge at the hips with a slight knee bend — torso at roughly 45 degrees to the floor.
Grip the bar slightly wider than shoulder width — overhand grip for upper back emphasis, underhand for lower lat emphasis.
Brace the core hard before every rep — the bent over position places significant demand on the lower back.
Retract the shoulder blades before pulling — initiate with the back.
Pull the bar toward the lower chest or stomach — elbows drive back close to the body.
Lower slowly under control — maintain the hip hinge throughout.
Common mistakes:
Torso rising during the pull — using momentum and lower back rather than back strength, reduce weight.
Not bracing the core — serious lower back injury risk under load.
Jerking the bar — momentum removes the back stimulus and increases injury risk.
Grip too narrow — reduces upper back activation.
Standing upright — the hip hinge is essential for the correct muscle activation.
Injury contraindications:
Lower back issues — avoid, switch to chest supported variations.
Hamstring tightness — limited hip hinge range of motion increases lower back stress, avoid.
Beginners — not programmed until hip hinge pattern is established.
Substitutions: Chest supported row / T bar row / Seated cable row
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/barbell-bent-over-row
Notes: Secondary rating — powerful back builder but the lower back demand makes it unsuitable for beginners and clients with lower back issues. Intermediate and advanced clients only. Overhand vs underhand grip changes the emphasis — overhand hits upper back and rhomboids more, underhand hits lower lats and biceps more.
Exercise 44: Dumbbell Single Arm Row
Primary muscle: Back — lats, mid back
Secondary muscles: Biceps, rear deltoid
Equipment: Dumbbell, flat bench for support
Experience level: All
Priority: Secondary
Rep ranges: Heavy set 8–10 reps each side. Back off set 12–15 reps each side.
Coaching cues:
Place one hand and same side knee on a flat bench — opposite foot on the floor for stability.
Back parallel to the floor — neutral spine throughout.
Let the dumbbell hang at full arm extension before pulling.
Initiate by retracting the shoulder blade — pull the elbow up and back toward the hip.
At the top the elbow should be above the torso — squeeze the lat hard.
Lower slowly to full arm extension — feel the stretch in the lat at the bottom.
Perform all reps on one side before switching.
Common mistakes:
Rotating the torso to assist the pull — reduces lat isolation, keep the torso square.
Not achieving full arm extension at the bottom — misses the lat stretch.
Pulling the elbow up and out rather than up and back — shifts emphasis from lats to rear deltoid.
Using too much weight — torso rotates, lower back compensates.
Injury contraindications:
Lower back issues — monitor carefully.
Shoulder issues — monitor range of motion.
Wrist issues — adjust grip, consider straps for heavier weights.
Substitutions: Seated cable row — bilateral alternative / Chest supported row / T bar row
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/dumbbell-single-arm-row
Notes: Excellent unilateral back exercise. Training one side at a time allows heavier loading per lat and eliminates the dominant side compensating for the weaker side. Straps recommended for heavier sets. The lat stretch at the bottom of each rep is critical.
Exercise 45: Straight Arm Pulldown
Primary muscle: Back — latissimus dorsi
Secondary muscles: Triceps, core
Equipment: Cable machine, rope or straight bar, cable set high
Experience level: All
Priority: Secondary
Rep ranges: Heavy set 12–15 reps. Back off set 15–20 reps.
Coaching cues:
Set cable to the highest position, attach a rope or straight bar.
Stand facing the cable machine, slight forward lean, arms extended overhead gripping the attachment.
Keep the arms straight throughout — elbows stay extended, this is not a pulldown.
Pull the rope or bar downward in an arc toward the hips.
At the bottom squeeze the lats hard — hands at hip level.
Return slowly along the same arc — feel the full lat stretch at the top with arms overhead.
Common mistakes:
Bending the elbows — turns it into a pulldown, loses the lat isolation.
Standing too upright — reduces the range of motion and the stretch.
Rushing — the arc movement requires control.
Not feeling the lats — if the triceps are fatiguing instead, reduce weight and focus on initiating with the lats.
Injury contraindications:
Shoulder issues — the overhead stretch position may aggravate some shoulder conditions.
Elbow issues — keeping the elbows straight places demand on the joint.
Substitutions: Cable pullover / Lat pulldown / Pull ups
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/straight-arm-pulldown
Notes: A pure lat isolation exercise — the straight arms remove all bicep involvement forcing the lats to work in isolation. Excellent as a lat finisher at the end of a pull session. Higher rep ranges work well here.
Exercise 46: Pull Ups
Primary muscle: Back — latissimus dorsi
Secondary muscles: Biceps, rear deltoid, rhomboids, core
Equipment: Pull up bar, assisted pull up machine for beginners
Experience level: All — assisted for beginners, bodyweight for intermediate, weighted for advanced
Priority: Primary
Rep ranges: Beginners assisted — 8–12 reps. Intermediate bodyweight — 6–10 reps. Advanced weighted — 5–8 reps.
Coaching cues:
Grip the bar slightly wider than shoulder width — overhand grip for standard pull up.
Hang at full arm extension before pulling — dead hang start.
Initiate by depressing the shoulder blades — think about pulling the elbows down to the hips.
Pull until the chin is above the bar — full range of motion.
Lower slowly to a full dead hang — complete arm extension between every rep.
Keep the core braced and legs still — no kipping.
Common mistakes:
Not achieving full dead hang at the bottom — misses the lat stretch.
Kipping — momentum rather than strength, reduces the stimulus significantly.
Chin not clearing the bar — partial range of motion.
Not initiating with the shoulder blades — biceps fatigue before the lats are stimulated.
Head craning forward to try to get chin over bar — reduce the weight or assistance level.
Injury contraindications:
Shoulder impingement — reduce range of motion or switch to lat pulldown.
Elbow issues — monitor carefully, the dead hang position places demand on the elbow joint.
Wrist issues — use straps or switch to lat pulldown.
Substitutions: Lat pulldown / Assisted pull up machine / Band assisted pull ups
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/pull-ups
Notes: The gold standard upper body pulling exercise. Full dead hang at the bottom is non negotiable. Progression follows the hierarchy — assisted machine, band assisted, bodyweight, weighted. Chin up variation — underhand grip — hits the biceps more, can be rotated in for variety.
PULL DAY — REAR DELT AND UPPER BACK
Exercise 47: Rear Delt Fly
Primary muscle: Shoulders — rear deltoid
Secondary muscles: Rhomboids, upper traps, external rotators
Equipment: Dumbbells, pec fly machine in reverse, cable machine or rear delt machine
Experience level: All
Priority: Primary
Rep ranges: Heavy set 12–15 reps. Back off set 15–20 reps.
Coaching cues:
Hinge forward at the hips — torso roughly parallel to the floor for dumbbell variation.
Let the dumbbells hang directly below the shoulders — slight bend in the elbows.
Raise the dumbbells outward and upward in an arc — lead with the elbows.
At the top the arms should be parallel to the floor — squeeze the rear deltoid hard.
Lower slowly under full control — 3–4 second lowering phase.
Keep the neck neutral — don’t crane the head up to see the mirror.
Common mistakes:
Torso too upright — shifts emphasis away from the rear deltoid.
Using momentum — the rear deltoid is a small muscle and responds poorly to heavy swinging weight.
Not achieving full range — arms must reach parallel to the floor for full rear deltoid activation.
Rushing the lowering phase — the eccentric is critical for rear deltoid development.
Too much weight — form breaks down entirely, rear deltoid receives minimal stimulus.
Injury contraindications:
Lower back issues — seated variation on the pec fly machine or cable variation recommended.
Shoulder impingement — rear delt flies are generally safe and often beneficial for impingement as they strengthen the external rotators.
Substitutions: Face pull / Reverse cable fly / Reverse pec deck
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/rear-delt-fly
Notes: The rear deltoid is chronically underdeveloped in most clients. Light weight and strict form always. The pec fly machine in reverse is an excellent alternative — keeps the movement path consistent and removes the lower back demand.
PULL DAY — TRAPS
Exercise 48: Dumbbell Shrugs with Straps
Primary muscle: Traps — upper trapezius
Secondary muscles: Levator scapulae, rhomboids
Equipment: Dumbbells, lifting straps
Experience level: All
Priority: Primary — default trap movement
Rep ranges: Heavy set 8–10 reps. Back off set 12–15 reps.
Coaching cues:
Use lifting straps — grip should never be the limiting factor on shrugs.
Stand with dumbbells at the sides, arms straight.
Shrug the shoulders straight up toward the ears — no rolling of the shoulders forward or backward.
At the top hold for one to two seconds — squeeze the traps hard.
Lower slowly under full control — full depression of the shoulders at the bottom.
Keep the head neutral — don’t crane the neck.
Common mistakes:
Rolling the shoulders — forward or backward rolling adds no additional trap stimulus and increases injury risk.
Not holding at the top — the isometric contraction at the top is where the trap stimulus comes from.
Rushing — shrugs respond well to slow controlled reps with a hold at the top.
Not using straps — grip gives out before traps are fully stimulated.
Partial range — must achieve full shoulder depression at the bottom and full elevation at the top.
Injury contraindications:
Neck issues — heavy shrugging can compress the cervical spine, reduce weight and monitor carefully.
AC joint issues — monitor carefully, shoulder elevation can aggravate AC joint problems.
Shoulder impingement — monitor carefully.
Substitutions: Barbell shrugs / Behind the back shrug / Cable shrug
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/dumbbell-shrugs-with-straps
Notes: Default trap movement. Straps always used. The hold at the top is the most important part of the movement — clients who rush through without holding at the top are wasting the exercise.
Exercise 49: Barbell Shrugs
Primary muscle: Traps — upper trapezius
Secondary muscles: Levator scapulae
Equipment: Barbell
Experience level: All
Priority: Secondary
Rep ranges: Heavy set 8–10 reps. Back off set 12–15 reps.
Coaching cues:
Same technique as dumbbell shrugs — straight up, hold at the top, slow lowering.
Grip the bar slightly wider than shoulder width.
Straps recommended for heavy sets.
The barbell allows heavier loading than dumbbells — useful for advanced clients.
No rolling of the shoulders — straight up and straight down only.
Common mistakes:
Same errors as dumbbell shrugs — rolling, rushing, no hold at the top.
Gripping too narrow — wrist stress under heavy load.
Injury contraindications:
Same as dumbbell shrugs.
Lower back issues — barbell hangs in front of the body which can place stress on the lower back under very heavy loads, monitor.
Substitutions: Dumbbell shrugs with straps / Behind the back shrug / Trap bar shrug
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/barbell-shrugs
Notes: Secondary variation — barbell allows heavier loading than dumbbells which can be useful for advanced clients. Same technique principles apply.
Exercise 50: Behind the Back Shrug
Primary muscle: Traps — upper and mid trapezius
Secondary muscles: Rhomboids
Equipment: Barbell or smith machine behind the body
Experience level: Intermediate and advanced
Priority: Tertiary
Rep ranges: Heavy set 10–12 reps. Back off set 12–15 reps.
Coaching cues:
Hold the barbell behind the body at arm’s length — smith machine makes this easier and safer.
Shrug straight up — the behind the body position naturally changes the angle of pull on the traps.
Hold at the top for one to two seconds.
Lower slowly under control.
Keep the head neutral and facing forward throughout.
Common mistakes:
Bar drifting away from the body — keep it close to the glutes throughout.
Rolling the shoulders — straight up only.
Not holding at the top.
Injury contraindications:
Shoulder issues — the behind the body position places the shoulder in an unusual position, monitor carefully.
Wrist issues — the behind the body grip can be uncomfortable, use smith machine variation.
Substitutions: Dumbbell shrugs with straps / Barbell shrugs
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/behind-the-back-shrug
Notes: Tertiary rating — used occasionally to hit the mid trap fibres from a slightly different angle. Smith machine variation recommended for ease of setup and safety.
PULL DAY — BICEPS
Exercise 51: EZ Bar Curl
Primary muscle: Biceps — biceps brachii
Secondary muscles: Brachialis, brachioradialis
Equipment: EZ bar
Experience level: All
Priority: Primary — default barbell bicep movement
Rep ranges: Heavy set 8–10 reps. Back off set 12–15 reps.
Coaching cues:
Grip the EZ bar at the angled sections — the semi supinated grip reduces wrist stress while maintaining strong bicep activation.
Upper arms pinned to the sides — completely still throughout.
Curl upward in a smooth arc — squeeze the bicep at the top.
Lower slowly to full arm extension — feel the stretch at the bottom.
No body swinging — strict throughout.
Common mistakes:
Upper arms moving — elbows drifting forward or upward removes the bicep isolation.
Swinging the body — momentum rather than bicep strength.
Gripping the straight sections rather than the angled sections — defeats the wrist friendly purpose of the EZ bar.
Not achieving full arm extension at the bottom — misses the bicep stretch.
Injury contraindications:
Wrist issues — EZ bar is the most wrist friendly barbell curl variation, monitor if still uncomfortable.
Elbow issues — monitor carefully.
Bicep tendon issues — reduce weight and monitor.
Substitutions: Dumbbell curl / Cable curl / Barbell curl if EZ bar unavailable
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/ez-bar-curl
Notes: Default barbell bicep movement — preferred over straight barbell due to the more comfortable wrist position. The semi supinated grip reduces wrist and elbow stress while maintaining excellent bicep activation.
Exercise 52: Dumbbell Curl
Primary muscle: Biceps — biceps brachii
Secondary muscles: Brachialis, brachioradialis
Equipment: Dumbbells
Experience level: All
Priority: Primary
Rep ranges: Heavy set 8–10 reps. Back off set 12–15 reps.
Coaching cues:
Stand or sit with dumbbells at arm’s length, palms facing each other to start — neutral grip.
As the curl begins supinate the wrist — rotate the palm to face upward as the dumbbell rises.
The supination should be complete by the midpoint of the curl — palm fully facing up.
Squeeze the bicep hard at the top — the full supination maximises bicep contraction.
Lower slowly — reverse the supination on the way down.
Upper arms completely still — pinned to the sides.
Common mistakes:
Not supinating — curling with a neutral grip throughout reduces bicep activation significantly.
Upper arms moving.
Rushing the lowering phase.
Swinging the body.
Injury contraindications:
Wrist issues — reduce supination range, monitor.
Elbow issues — monitor carefully.
Bicep tendon issues — reduce weight and monitor.
Substitutions: EZ bar curl / Cable curl / Hammer curl — neutral grip alternative
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/dumbbell-curl
Notes: The supination during the curl is the key coaching point — it maximises bicep activation. Many clients curl with a neutral grip throughout and wonder why their biceps aren’t developing. Teach the supination from day one.
Exercise 53: Incline Dumbbell Curl
Primary muscle: Biceps — long head emphasis, maximum stretch
Secondary muscles: Brachialis
Equipment: Dumbbells, incline bench set to 45–60 degrees
Experience level: All
Priority: Primary
Rep ranges: Heavy set 8–10 reps. Back off set 12–15 reps.
Coaching cues:
Set the bench to 45–60 degrees — the incline position allows the arms to hang behind the body, creating a maximum stretch on the long head of the bicep.
Sit back against the bench, arms hanging straight down behind the body at full extension.
Curl the dumbbells upward — supinating the wrist as with a standard dumbbell curl.
At the top squeeze the bicep hard.
Lower slowly to the full hanging position — the stretch at the bottom is the most valuable part of this exercise.
Keep the upper arms still — they may drift slightly forward but should not swing.
Common mistakes:
Bench angle too upright — reduces the stretch, becomes a standard seated curl.
Not allowing full hang at the bottom — misses the long head stretch entirely.
Rushing — the stretch position is where the value comes from, hold it briefly between reps.
Upper arms swinging forward aggressively.
Injury contraindications:
Bicep tendon issues — the maximum stretch position places significant stress on the bicep tendon, avoid or use very light weight.
Shoulder issues — the behind the body position may aggravate some shoulder conditions, monitor.
Substitutions: Dumbbell curl — standard variation without the stretch / Cable curl with cable set low / EZ bar curl
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/incline-dumbbell-curl
Notes: One of the best bicep exercises for long head development. The incline position creates a stretch no other curl variation can match. The long head creates the bicep peak — clients wanting more peak development should prioritise this exercise.
Exercise 54: Hammer Curl
Primary muscle: Biceps — brachialis and brachioradialis emphasis
Secondary muscles: Biceps brachii
Equipment: Dumbbells
Experience level: All
Priority: Primary
Rep ranges: Heavy set 8–10 reps. Back off set 12–15 reps.
Coaching cues:
Stand with dumbbells at the sides, palms facing each other — neutral grip maintained throughout.
Curl the dumbbells upward keeping the palms facing each other — no supination.
At the top squeeze hard — the neutral grip hits the brachialis and brachioradialis more than the bicep brachii.
Lower slowly to full arm extension.
Upper arms pinned to the sides — completely still.
Can be performed alternating or both arms simultaneously.
Common mistakes:
Supinating during the curl — turns it into a standard dumbbell curl, loses the brachialis emphasis.
Upper arms moving.
Rushing.
Injury contraindications:
Elbow issues — the hammer curl is generally well tolerated and is often recommended for clients with elbow discomfort as the neutral grip reduces stress.
Wrist issues — neutral grip is comfortable for most wrist conditions.
Substitutions: Cable hammer curl with rope attachment / Cross body hammer curl / EZ bar curl
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/hammer-curl
Notes: The hammer curl targets the brachialis — a muscle that sits underneath the bicep and when developed pushes the bicep up making it appear larger and more peaked. Should be included regularly alongside supinating curl variations for complete arm development.
Exercise 55: Cable Curl
Primary muscle: Biceps — biceps brachii
Secondary muscles: Brachialis, brachioradialis
Equipment: Cable machine, straight bar or EZ bar attachment, cable set low
Experience level: All
Priority: Primary
Rep ranges: Heavy set 10–12 reps. Back off set 15–20 reps.
Coaching cues:
Set the cable to the lowest position — provides constant tension throughout the full range of motion.
Grip the attachment with an underhand grip.
Upper arms pinned to the sides — completely still.
Curl upward in a smooth arc — squeeze the bicep hard at the top.
Lower slowly to full arm extension — the cable maintains tension at the bottom unlike a dumbbell.
Keep the wrists neutral.
Common mistakes:
Upper arms moving.
Rushing the lowering phase — the cable provides resistance throughout, use it.
Standing too close to the machine — reduces range of motion.
Partial range of motion.
Injury contraindications:
Elbow issues — monitor carefully.
Wrist issues — EZ bar attachment is more wrist friendly than straight bar.
Bicep tendon issues — reduce weight and monitor.
Substitutions: EZ bar curl / Dumbbell curl / Preacher curl
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/cable-curl
Notes: The cable provides constant tension throughout the full range of motion — unlike free weight curls which have no tension at the bottom. Higher rep ranges work particularly well here. Good as a finisher at the end of a bicep session.
Exercise 56: Preacher Curl
Primary muscle: Biceps — short head emphasis, peak contraction
Secondary muscles: Brachialis
Equipment: Preacher curl bench, EZ bar or dumbbells
Experience level: All
Priority: Primary
Rep ranges: Heavy set 8–10 reps. Back off set 12–15 reps.
Coaching cues:
Adjust the preacher bench so the top of the pad is at armpit height when seated.
Place the upper arms flat against the pad — they stay in contact with the pad throughout.
Grip the EZ bar at the angled sections — underhand grip.
Lower the bar slowly to near full arm extension — do not lock out completely, keep slight tension.
Curl upward to full contraction — squeeze the bicep hard at the top.
The pad prevents the upper arms from moving — this forces strict bicep isolation.
Common mistakes:
Arms coming off the pad — defeats the purpose of the preacher bench.
Locking out at the bottom — significant stress on the bicep tendon at full extension, always keep a slight bend.
Rushing — strict slow reps maximise the stimulus.
Seat height wrong — upper arms should be flat against the pad, not angled up or down.
Injury contraindications:
Bicep tendon issues — avoid locking out, keep a bend in the elbow at the bottom always. If pain persists avoid entirely.
Elbow issues — monitor carefully, the preacher position places unique stress on the elbow joint.
Substitutions: EZ bar curl / Dumbbell curl / Cable curl
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/preacher-curl
Notes: The preacher bench locks the upper arms in place making cheating impossible. Never lock out at the bottom on a preacher curl — the bicep tendon is under significant stress at that position and locking out fully is a common cause of bicep tendon injury. Always keep a slight bend.
Exercise 57: Barbell Curl
Primary muscle: Biceps — biceps brachii
Secondary muscles: Brachialis, brachioradialis, forearms
Equipment: Barbell or EZ bar
Experience level: All
Priority: Secondary
Rep ranges: Heavy set 8–10 reps. Back off set 12–15 reps.
Coaching cues:
Stand with barbell at arm’s length, underhand grip slightly wider than shoulder width.
Keep the upper arms completely still throughout — pinned to the sides of the torso.
Curl the bar upward in an arc — squeeze the bicep hard at the top.
Lower slowly under full control — full arm extension at the bottom.
Keep the wrists neutral — don’t let them curl under the bar.
No swinging of the body — strict form throughout.
Common mistakes:
Upper arms moving — elbows drifting forward or upward removes the bicep isolation.
Swinging the body — momentum rather than bicep strength.
Not achieving full arm extension at the bottom — misses the bicep stretch.
Wrists curling — places unnecessary stress on the wrist joint.
Too much weight — form breaks down entirely.
Injury contraindications:
Wrist issues — EZ bar variation is more wrist friendly.
Elbow issues — monitor carefully, reduce range of motion if pain occurs.
Bicep tendon issues — monitor carefully, reduce weight significantly.
