Substitutions: EZ bar curl — more wrist friendly alternative / Dumbbell curl / Cable curl
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/barbell-curl
Notes: Secondary rating — EZ bar curl leads as the default barbell bicep exercise due to the more comfortable wrist position. Straight barbell curl rotated in for variety.
LEG DAY — QUADS
Exercise 58: Barbell Squat
Primary muscle: Quads — quadriceps, full activation
Secondary muscles: Glutes, hamstrings, lower back, core
Equipment: Barbell, squat rack
Experience level: Intermediate and advanced
Priority: Secondary
Rep ranges: Heavy set 6–8 reps. Back off set 10–12 reps.
Coaching cues:
Set the bar on the rack at upper chest height — step under and position the bar across the upper traps, not the neck.
Grip the bar just outside shoulder width — create tension in the upper back by trying to bend the bar.
Step back from the rack — feet shoulder width or slightly wider, toes pointed out 15–30 degrees.
Brace the core hard — take a deep breath into the belly before each rep.
Descend by pushing the knees out in the direction of the toes — sit back and down simultaneously.
At the bottom the crease of the hip should be below the top of the knee — full depth.
Drive back up through the heels — knees stay pushed out throughout.
Lock out fully at the top — squeeze the glutes.
Common mistakes:
Knees caving inward — the most common and dangerous error, always push knees out.
Heels rising — ankle mobility issue, elevate heels slightly or work on ankle mobility.
Excessive forward lean — upper back not tight enough or bar position too high.
Shallow depth — hip crease must reach below the knee.
Bar on the neck — always on the upper traps.
Injury contraindications:
Knee issues — avoid, switch to hack squat or leg press.
Lower back issues — avoid, the spinal loading under a barbell is significant.
Hip issues — monitor carefully, adjust stance width.
Beginners — not programmed, movement pattern must be established on goblet squat and leg press first.
Substitutions: Hack squat / Leg press / Goblet squat — beginner alternative
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/barbell-squat
Notes: Intermediate and advanced clients only. The barbell squat is the king of lower body exercises but requires significant technique, mobility and body awareness before loading heavily. Never programmed for beginners. Full depth is non negotiable.
Exercise 59: Hack Squat
Primary muscle: Quads — quadriceps, full activation
Secondary muscles: Glutes, hamstrings
Equipment: Hack squat machine
Experience level: All
Priority: Primary
Rep ranges: Heavy set 6–8 reps. Back off set 10–12 reps.
Coaching cues:
Position the back flat against the pad, shoulders under the shoulder pads.
Feet shoulder width apart on the platform, toes pointed slightly outward.
Unhook the safety handles before descending.
Descend slowly — knees tracking over the toes, pushing outward.
At the bottom the knees should be at 90 degrees or deeper — full range of motion.
Drive back up through the heels — squeeze the quads at the top.
Do not lock out completely — keep tension on the quads throughout.
Re-hook the safety handles before stepping off.
Common mistakes:
Feet too low on the platform — excessive knee travel over the toes, increases knee stress.
Feet too high — reduces quad activation, becomes more glute dominant.
Knees caving inward — always push outward.
Not reaching full depth — partial reps significantly reduce quad stimulus.
Forgetting to re-hook safety handles — serious injury risk.
Injury contraindications:
Knee issues — reduce depth, keep above 90 degrees if pain occurs below.
Lower back issues — the machine supports the back, generally safe. Monitor carefully.
Hip issues — adjust foot width and toe angle.
Substitutions: Leg press / Pendulum squat / Barbell squat — advanced alternative
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/hack-squat
Notes: Default primary quad exercise for most clients. The machine supports the back removing lower back as a limiting factor. Full depth is important — the hack squat machine makes it tempting to use excessive weight with partial reps. Always prioritise depth over load.
Exercise 60: Pendulum Squat
Primary muscle: Quads — quadriceps, strong quad isolation
Secondary muscles: Glutes, hamstrings minimal
Equipment: Pendulum squat machine
Experience level: All
Priority: Primary — equipment dependent
Rep ranges: Heavy set 8–10 reps. Back off set 12–15 reps.
Coaching cues:
Position the back against the pad, shoulders under the shoulder pads.
Feet relatively close together and low on the platform — the pendulum arc naturally keeps the shins vertical making this very knee friendly.
The machine follows a pendulum arc — do not fight the natural movement path.
Descend slowly following the arc — full depth, knees tracking over toes.
Drive back up through the pendulum arc — squeeze the quads hard at the top.
The upright torso position places maximum tension on the quads.
Common mistakes:
Fighting the pendulum arc — let the machine guide the movement.
Partial depth — the pendulum squat is most effective at full depth.
Feet too high — reduces the quad dominant nature of the exercise.
Too much weight — the natural arc limits how much can be loaded compared to a hack squat.
Injury contraindications:
Knee issues — one of the most knee friendly squat variations due to the vertical shin angle, generally well tolerated.
Lower back issues — machine supported, generally safe.
Substitutions: Hack squat / Leg press / Leg extension — isolation alternative
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/pendulum-squat
Notes: The pendulum squat produces an exceptionally strong quad contraction due to the upright torso and natural arc. One of the best quad isolation exercises on a machine. Equipment dependent — only programmed when the client confirms access to a pendulum squat machine.
Exercise 61: Leg Press
Primary muscle: Quads — quadriceps
Secondary muscles: Glutes, hamstrings, calves
Equipment: Leg press machine
Experience level: All
Priority: Primary
Rep ranges: Heavy set 8–10 reps. Back off set 12–15 reps.
Coaching cues:
Feet shoulder width apart in the middle of the platform — toes pointed slightly outward.
Back and glutes pressed firmly into the seat throughout — never let the lower back round off the seat.
Unhook the safety handles before pressing.
Lower the platform slowly — knees tracking over the toes, pushing outward.
At the bottom the knees should be at 90 degrees — do not let the knees travel past the toes excessively.
Drive through the heels back to near full extension — do not lock out the knees completely.
Re-hook the safety handles before removing feet from the platform.
Common mistakes:
Lower back rounding off the seat at the bottom — serious spinal injury risk, reduce the range of motion.
Locking out the knees — removes tension and risks hyperextension.
Feet too low — excessive knee travel, increases knee stress.
Feet too high — reduces quad activation, becomes glute dominant.
Knees caving inward — always push outward.
Injury contraindications:
Knee issues — feet higher on the platform reduces knee stress, monitor carefully.
Lower back issues — do not allow the lower back to round off the seat, reduce range of motion if necessary.
Hip issues — adjust foot width and position.
Substitutions: Hack squat / Pendulum squat / Barbell squat — advanced alternative
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/leg-press
Notes: The most accessible quad exercise for all experience levels. Foot position determines the emphasis — low and narrow for quad focus, high and wide for glute and hamstring focus. Never lock out the knees at the top.
Exercise 62: Leg Extension
Primary muscle: Quads — quadriceps, strong isolation
Secondary muscles: None significant
Equipment: Leg extension machine
Experience level: All
Priority: Primary
Rep ranges: Heavy set 10–12 reps. Back off set 15–20 reps.
Coaching cues:
Adjust the seat so the back of the knees sit at the edge of the seat — the knee joint should align with the pivot point of the machine.
Adjust the foot pad so it sits just above the ankle — not on the shin.
Sit upright, back against the pad, grip the handles.
Extend the legs to full extension — squeeze the quads hard at the top, hold for one second.
Lower slowly under full control — 3–4 second lowering phase.
Do not let the weight stack touch between reps — keep tension on the quads throughout.
Common mistakes:
Seat position wrong — knee joint must align with the machine pivot, incorrect setup causes knee stress.
Foot pad too high — on the shin rather than above the ankle, changes the lever arm and increases knee stress.
Not achieving full extension — misses the peak quad contraction.
Rushing the lowering phase — the eccentric is critical for quad development.
Letting the weight stack touch — removes tension.
Injury contraindications:
Knee issues — places significant stress on the patella tendon and ACL. Use with caution. Partial range starting from 90 degrees significantly reduces knee stress.
Patella tendon issues — avoid or use very light weight with partial range only.
Substitutions: Hack squat / Leg press / Terminal knee extension with band — knee rehabilitation alternative
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/leg-extension
Notes: The only true quad isolation exercise. The peak contraction hold at the top is the most important part. Clients with knee issues should use partial range of motion — starting from 90 degrees reduces patella tendon stress significantly.
Exercise 63: Bulgarian Split Squat
Primary muscle: Quads — quadriceps, strong unilateral activation
Secondary muscles: Glutes, hamstrings, core
Equipment: Dumbbells or barbell, flat bench or box
Experience level: Intermediate and advanced
Priority: Specialist
Rep ranges: Heavy set 8–10 reps each leg. Back off set 12–15 reps each leg.
Coaching cues:
Place the rear foot on a bench or box — laces down, not the top of the foot.
Front foot far enough forward that the shin stays roughly vertical when at the bottom — too close and the knee drifts excessively forward.
Hold dumbbells at the sides or barbell on the upper traps.
Descend straight down — the back knee drops toward the floor.
At the bottom the front thigh should be parallel to the floor or deeper.
Drive back up through the front heel — squeeze the quad and glute at the top.
Complete all reps on one leg before switching.
Common mistakes:
Front foot too close — excessive forward knee travel, increases knee stress significantly.
Rear foot instability — takes time to find the balance, start with bodyweight.
Not descending deep enough — partial range significantly reduces the stimulus.
Leaning forward excessively — shifts emphasis from quads to lower back.
Using too much weight before mastering balance — form breaks down completely.
Injury contraindications:
Knee issues — avoid, the forward knee stress is significant even with correct setup.
Lower back issues — monitor carefully, the unilateral position can create spinal instability.
Hip flexor tightness — the rear hip flexor is stretched significantly in this position, can be uncomfortable.
Substitutions: Single leg press / Hack squat / Barbell squat
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/bulgarian-split-squat
Notes: Specialist rating — one of the most effective leg exercises but requires significant balance, mobility and technique. Not programmed for beginners. Used for intermediate and advanced clients who want to address strength imbalances between legs. Start with bodyweight before adding load.
Exercise 64: Single Leg Press
Primary muscle: Quads — quadriceps, unilateral
Secondary muscles: Glutes, hamstrings
Equipment: Leg press machine
Experience level: All
Priority: Tertiary
Rep ranges: Heavy set 10–12 reps each leg. Back off set 15–20 reps each leg.
Coaching cues:
Same setup as bilateral leg press — back and glutes pressed into the seat.
One foot in the centre of the platform — the other foot rests off the platform.
Use significantly less weight than bilateral leg press — one leg handles half the load.
Descend slowly — knee tracking over the toe.
Drive back up through the heel to near full extension.
Complete all reps on one leg before switching.
Common mistakes:
Using too much weight — the most common error, single leg press requires significantly less weight than bilateral.
Lower back rounding — same risk as bilateral leg press.
Knee caving inward — push the knee outward throughout.
Injury contraindications:
Knee issues — monitor carefully, the single leg position increases the demand on the knee joint.
Lower back issues — monitor, keep the back flat against the seat.
Substitutions: Bilateral leg press / Bulgarian split squat — more challenging unilateral alternative / Hack squat
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/single-leg-press
Notes: Tertiary rating — used sparingly for specific situations. Good for addressing leg strength imbalances and for clients who want unilateral leg work without the balance demands of a Bulgarian split squat.
Exercise 65: Step Ups
Primary muscle: Quads — quadriceps, unilateral
Secondary muscles: Glutes, hamstrings, core
Equipment: Box, bench or step, dumbbells optional
Experience level: Beginners and all levels for cardio finisher
Priority: Tertiary
Rep ranges: Standard 10–15 reps each leg. Cardio finisher — 20+ reps or timed sets.
Coaching cues:
Place the full foot on the box — not just the toes.
Drive through the heel of the elevated foot to stand up — the trailing leg should not push off the floor.
Stand tall at the top — fully extend the hip and knee.
Step back down slowly and under control.
Keep the torso upright throughout — minimal forward lean.
Add dumbbells for progressive overload once bodyweight is comfortable.
Common mistakes:
Pushing off the floor with the trailing leg — removes the unilateral demand entirely.
Toes only on the box — unstable and reduces the quad drive.
Leaning forward excessively — lower back compensation.
Stepping down too quickly — losing control on the descent.
Injury contraindications:
Knee issues — monitor carefully, the step up places demand on the knee joint.
Balance issues — use a lower box and hold a wall for support initially.
Substitutions: Leg press / Hack squat / Bulgarian split squat — more advanced alternative
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/step-ups
Notes: Tertiary rating — used for beginners learning unilateral movement patterns and as a cardio finisher on leg day for all experience levels. Not a primary strength exercise for intermediate and advanced clients.
LEG DAY — HAMSTRINGS AND GLUTES
Exercise 66: Romanian Deadlift
Primary muscle: Hamstrings — biceps femoris, long head
Secondary muscles: Glutes, lower back, core
Equipment: Barbell or dumbbells
Experience level: All
Priority: Primary
Rep ranges: Heavy set 8–10 reps. Back off set 12–15 reps.
Coaching cues:
Stand with barbell or dumbbells at arm’s length in front of the body.
Slight bend in the knees — maintained throughout, the knees do not bend further during the movement.
Hinge at the hips — push the hips back as the weight lowers toward the floor.
Keep the back flat and neutral throughout — no rounding of the lower back.
Lower the weight along the legs — it should stay close to the body throughout.
Feel the stretch in the hamstrings — go as low as flexibility allows while keeping the back flat.
Drive the hips forward to return to standing — squeeze the glutes at the top.
Common mistakes:
Rounding the lower back — the most dangerous error, the hamstring stretch should come from the hip hinge not spinal flexion.
Bending the knees too much — turns it into a conventional deadlift, loses the hamstring isolation.
Bar drifting away from the legs — increases lower back stress significantly.
Going lower than flexibility allows — lower back rounds to compensate, reduce range of motion.
Not feeling the hamstrings — if the lower back fatigues before the hamstrings the hinge pattern is incorrect.
Injury contraindications:
Lower back issues — monitor carefully, keep range of motion limited to where the back stays flat. If lower back pain occurs switch to lying hamstring curl.
Hamstring strain — avoid during acute phase, return with very light weight once healed.
Hip issues — monitor the hinge pattern carefully.
Substitutions: Lying hamstring curl — removes lower back demand entirely / Seated hamstring curl
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/romanian-deadlift
Notes: One of the best hamstring exercises available. The hip hinge pattern is fundamental — if a client cannot hinge with a flat back the RDL should be taught with a light weight or a dowel rod along the spine before loading. The stretch in the hamstrings at the bottom drives hamstring development — never sacrifice range of motion for load. Dumbbell variation is more beginner friendly.
Exercise 67: Lying Hamstring Curl
Primary muscle: Hamstrings — biceps femoris, both heads
Secondary muscles: Calves, glutes minimal
Equipment: Lying hamstring curl machine
Experience level: All
Priority: Primary
Rep ranges: Heavy set 10–12 reps. Back off set 15–20 reps.
Coaching cues:
Lie face down on the machine — the knee joint should align with the pivot point of the machine.
The foot pad should sit just above the ankle — not on the calves.
Keep the hips pressed down into the pad throughout — do not let them rise.
Curl the weight toward the glutes in a smooth arc.
At the top squeeze the hamstrings hard — hold for one second.
Lower slowly under full control — 3–4 second lowering phase.
Do not let the weight stack touch between reps — keep tension on the hamstrings.
Common mistakes:
Hips rising off the pad — the most common error, reduces hamstring isolation and stresses the lower back.
Foot pad position wrong — knee joint must align with machine pivot.
Rushing the lowering phase — the eccentric drives hamstring development.
Partial range — must curl all the way to the glutes.
Letting the weight stack touch — removes tension.
Injury contraindications:
Hamstring strain — avoid during acute phase, return cautiously with light weight.
Knee issues — monitor carefully, the lying curl is generally well tolerated.
Lower back issues — the prone position is generally comfortable for lower back conditions.
Substitutions: Seated hamstring curl / Romanian deadlift / Nordic curl — advanced alternative
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/lying-hamstring-curl
Notes: The first exercise on every leg day — performed before any quad work as a pre activation and warm up for the hamstrings. Always performed first regardless of whether it is a quad focused or hamstring focused day. The hold at the top and the slow lowering phase are the two most important coaching points.
Exercise 68: Seated Hamstring Curl
Primary muscle: Hamstrings — biceps femoris, greater stretch than lying variation
Secondary muscles: Calves, glutes minimal
Equipment: Seated hamstring curl machine
Experience level: All
Priority: Primary
Rep ranges: Heavy set 10–12 reps. Back off set 15–20 reps.
Coaching cues:
Adjust the back pad so the knees sit just at the edge of the seat.
The foot pad should sit just above the ankle.
Sit tall, back pressed against the pad.
The seated position places the hip in flexion which stretches the hamstring further than the lying variation — this increased stretch drives more muscle growth.
Pull the foot pad downward and back — curl the weight toward the seat.
At the bottom squeeze the hamstrings hard.
Return slowly to full extension — feel the stretch at the top.
Common mistakes:
Back not pressed against the pad — torso leans forward, reduces hamstring isolation.
Seat position wrong — knees must be at the edge of the seat for full range of motion.
Rushing the return — the stretch at the top is the most valuable part of the seated variation.
Partial range — must achieve full extension at the top.
Injury contraindications:
Hamstring strain — avoid during acute phase.
Knee issues — generally well tolerated, monitor.
Lower back issues — the seated upright position is generally comfortable.
Substitutions: Lying hamstring curl / Romanian deadlift / Nordic curl
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/seated-hamstring-curl
Notes: The seated position creates a greater hamstring stretch than the lying variation because the hip is flexed — this stretched position under load drives more muscle growth. Both lying and seated hamstring curls should be included regularly to hit the hamstrings through different ranges.
Exercise 69: Hip Thrust — Barbell
Primary muscle: Glutes — gluteus maximus
Secondary muscles: Hamstrings, quads, core
Equipment: Barbell, flat bench, barbell pad
Experience level: All
Priority: Primary — female clients / Tertiary — male clients unless requested
Rep ranges: Heavy set 8–10 reps. Back off set 12–15 reps.
Coaching cues:
Sit on the floor with the upper back against a flat bench — the bench should be at roughly mid shoulder blade height.
Roll the barbell over the legs and position it across the hip crease — always use a barbell pad for comfort.
Feet flat on the floor, shoulder width apart, toes pointed slightly outward.
Drive through the heels — thrust the hips upward until the torso is parallel to the floor.
At the top squeeze the glutes as hard as possible — hold for one to two seconds.
Lower slowly under control — hips descend toward the floor without fully touching.
Keep the chin tucked — looking straight ahead or slightly down prevents hyperextension of the lower back.
Common mistakes:
Hyperextending the lower back at the top — the torso should be parallel to the floor not arched upward, squeeze the glutes not the lower back.
Chin up and head back — leads to lower back hyperextension.
Feet too close or too far — experiment with foot position to maximise glute activation.
Not squeezing at the top — the isometric hold at the top is where most of the glute stimulus comes from.
No barbell pad — barbell on the bare hip crease under heavy load is extremely uncomfortable and unnecessary.
Injury contraindications:
Lower back issues — monitor carefully, hyperextension at the top can aggravate lower back problems. Keep the movement strictly controlled.
Hip issues — the hip crease loading position of the barbell may be uncomfortable, try alternative positions or switch to machine variation.
Knee issues — generally well tolerated as the knee is not under significant stress.
Substitutions: Hip thrust machine / Glute bridge / Cable kickback
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/hip-thrust-barbell
Notes: Primary for female clients — the hip thrust is the single best glute exercise available and should feature in every female client’s leg session. Tertiary for male clients — included only when specifically requested or glutes are flagged as lagging. The barbell pad is non negotiable. The squeeze and hold at the top is the most important part of the movement.
Exercise 70: Hip Thrust — Machine
Primary muscle: Glutes — gluteus maximus
Secondary muscles: Hamstrings, quads
Equipment: Hip thrust machine
Experience level: All
Priority: Primary — female clients / Tertiary — male clients unless requested — equipment dependent
Rep ranges: Heavy set 8–10 reps. Back off set 12–15 reps.
Coaching cues:
Adjust the machine so the pad sits across the hip crease when seated.
Feet flat on the platform, shoulder width apart.
Drive through the heels — thrust the hips forward and upward.
At the top squeeze the glutes hard — hold for one to two seconds.
Return slowly under control.
The machine variation removes the setup complexity of the barbell variation and provides a more consistent resistance curve.
Common mistakes:
Pad position wrong — must be at the hip crease not the stomach or thighs.
Not squeezing at the top — same critical point as barbell variation.
Hyperextending the lower back — keep the movement controlled.
Injury contraindications:
Same as barbell hip thrust.
Generally more comfortable than the barbell variation for clients with hip discomfort.
Substitutions: Barbell hip thrust / Glute bridge / Cable kickback
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/hip-thrust-machine
Notes: Same priority rules as barbell hip thrust. The machine variation is more beginner friendly and removes the setup complexity of the barbell. Equipment dependent — only programmed when the client confirms their gym has a hip thrust machine.
Exercise 71: Glute Bridge
Primary muscle: Glutes — gluteus maximus
Secondary muscles: Hamstrings, core
Equipment: Bodyweight, plate or dumbbell for loaded variation
Experience level: All
Priority: Primary — female clients / Tertiary — male clients unless requested
Rep ranges: Bodyweight — 15–20 reps. Loaded — 10–15 reps.
Coaching cues:
Lie on the floor, knees bent, feet flat on the floor shoulder width apart.
For loaded variation — place a plate or dumbbell across the hip crease.
Drive through the heels — lift the hips toward the ceiling.
At the top squeeze the glutes hard — body forms a straight line from knees to shoulders.
Hold at the top for one to two seconds.
Lower slowly under control — hips descend toward the floor without fully touching.
Keep the core braced throughout — do not hyperextend the lower back.
Common mistakes:
Hyperextending the lower back at the top — squeeze glutes not lower back.
Feet too close — knees travel over the toes, reduces glute activation.
Not holding at the top — the isometric contraction drives glute development.
Rushing — slow controlled reps with a hold maximise stimulus.
Injury contraindications:
Lower back issues — generally well tolerated as the range of motion is limited. Monitor carefully.
Knee issues — generally safe, adjust foot position if knee discomfort occurs.
Substitutions: Hip thrust barbell or machine — more loaded progression / Cable kickback
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/glute-bridge
Notes: Same priority rules as hip thrust variations. The glute bridge is a simpler and more accessible version of the hip thrust — no bench required and the range of motion is smaller. Used for beginners learning the hip extension pattern before progressing to hip thrusts, and as a bodyweight warm up for the glutes before heavier work.
Exercise 72: Cable Kickback
Primary muscle: Glutes — gluteus maximus
Secondary muscles: Hamstrings
Equipment: Cable machine, ankle attachment
Experience level: All
Priority: Primary — female clients / Tertiary — male clients unless requested
Rep ranges: Heavy set 12–15 reps each leg. Back off set 15–20 reps each leg.
Coaching cues:
Attach the ankle cuff to the lowest cable pulley.
Stand facing the cable machine, slight forward lean, hands on the machine for support.
Keep the working leg straight or with a very slight knee bend.
Drive the leg backward and upward in an arc — squeeze the glute hard at the top.
At the top the leg should be at roughly 45 degrees behind the body — no higher.
Return slowly under control — the cable maintains tension throughout the lowering phase.
Complete all reps on one leg before switching.
Common mistakes:
Swinging the leg — momentum rather than glute activation, reduce weight.
Leg going too high — lower back hyperextension compensating, keep the movement controlled.
Not squeezing at the top — the contraction is the entire point of the movement.
Leaning too far forward — shifts emphasis away from the glutes.
Hip rotating — keep the hips square throughout.
Injury contraindications:
Lower back issues — the extension movement can aggravate lower back problems, monitor carefully and keep range of motion controlled.
Hip issues — monitor carefully.
Knee issues — generally safe as the knee is not under significant stress.
Substitutions: Hip thrust / Glute bridge
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/cable-kickback
Notes: Same priority rules as hip thrust and glute bridge. An excellent glute isolation exercise — the cable provides constant tension throughout the movement unlike bodyweight variations. Performed one leg at a time for maximum glute isolation. The squeeze at the top of each rep is the most important coaching point.
LEG DAY — ADDUCTORS AND ABDUCTORS
Exercise 73: Adductor Machine
Primary muscle: Inner thighs — adductors
Secondary muscles: Glutes minimal
Equipment: Adductor machine
Experience level: All
Priority: Primary
Rep ranges: Heavy set 12–15 reps. Back off set 15–20 reps.
Coaching cues:
Adjust the machine so the leg pads sit just above the knees on the inner thighs.
Set the range of motion — start with a comfortable range and increase as flexibility improves.
Sit tall, back against the pad, grip the handles.
Squeeze the legs together in a smooth controlled movement.
At the point of full adduction squeeze the inner thighs hard — hold briefly.
Return slowly under control — feel the stretch in the inner thighs at the full open position.
Do not let the weight stack touch between reps.
Common mistakes:
Range of motion too wide too soon — inner thigh strain is common when the range is increased too quickly.
Rushing — the adductor machine responds well to slow controlled reps.
Not holding at the point of full adduction — misses the peak contraction.
Too much weight — form breaks down and hip flexors compensate.
Injury contraindications:
Groin strain — avoid during acute phase, return very cautiously with light weight and reduced range of motion.
Hip issues — monitor carefully, adjust range of motion.
Substitutions: Cable adduction — standing cable alternative / Sumo squat — compound adductor movement
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/adductor-machine
Notes: Always included in leg sessions — the adductors are an important but frequently neglected muscle group. Strong adductors improve knee stability and reduce the risk of groin injuries. The range of motion should be increased gradually — do not force the legs to a wide position before the inner thighs are flexible enough.
Exercise 74: Abductor Machine
Primary muscle: Outer thighs and glutes — abductors, gluteus medius
Secondary muscles: TFL, glutes
Equipment: Abductor machine
Experience level: All
Priority: Secondary
Rep ranges: Heavy set 12–15 reps. Back off set 15–20 reps.
Coaching cues:
Adjust the machine so the leg pads sit just above the knees on the outer thighs.
Sit upright, back against the pad.
Push the legs outward against the resistance — smooth controlled movement.
At the point of full abduction squeeze the outer glutes and abductors.
Return slowly under control.
Do not let the weight stack touch between reps.
Common mistakes:
Rushing — same as adductor machine, slow controlled reps.
Too much weight — hip flexors and lower back compensate.
Not sitting upright — leaning forward reduces abductor activation.
Injury contraindications:
Hip issues — monitor carefully.
IT band issues — the abductor machine can aggravate IT band problems, monitor.
Substitutions: Cable abduction — standing alternative / Lateral band walk — bodyweight alternative
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/abductor-machine
Notes: Secondary rating — rotated in regularly but not in every session. The abductor machine targets the gluteus medius which is important for hip stability and knee alignment. Strong abductors reduce the risk of knee valgus during squatting movements. Particularly beneficial for female clients alongside the adductor machine.
LEG DAY — CALVES
Exercise 75: Standing Calf Raise
Primary muscle: Calves — gastrocnemius, the upper calf muscle
Secondary muscles: Soleus minimal
Equipment: Standing calf raise machine or step
Experience level: All
Priority: Primary — default calf movement
Rep ranges: Working sets 12–15 reps. Higher rep sets 15–20 reps. Slow tempo sets 8–10 reps with 3 second raise, 3 second lower, 2 second hold.
Coaching cues:
Position the balls of the feet on the platform — heels hanging off the edge for full range of motion.
Stand tall, slight bend in the knees — do not lock the knees out.
Lower the heels as far as possible toward the floor — full stretch at the bottom, hold for one second.
Drive up onto the toes as high as possible — full contraction at the top, hold for one second.
Lower slowly under control — the eccentric is critical for calf development.
The calf is a stubborn muscle — it responds best to slow controlled reps with a full stretch and full contraction on every rep.
Common mistakes:
Partial range of motion — the most common calf training error. Must achieve full stretch at the bottom and full contraction at the top on every single rep.
Bouncing — using the elastic energy of the Achilles tendon rather than the calf muscle.
Rushing — calves respond to slow controlled reps, not fast bouncy ones.
Not holding at the bottom — the stretch is where calf growth comes from.
Locking out the knees — shifts emphasis away from the gastrocnemius.
Injury contraindications:
Achilles tendon issues — avoid full stretch position, reduce range of motion significantly.
Plantar fasciitis — monitor carefully, the full stretch position may aggravate.
Calf strain — avoid during acute phase.
Substitutions: Seated calf raise — different muscle emphasis / Leg press calf raise
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/standing-calf-raise
Notes: Default calf movement. The gastrocnemius is the visible upper calf muscle that creates the rounded calf appearance. It is best targeted with the knee straight or slightly bent. Slow tempo and full range of motion are non negotiable — calves are a notoriously stubborn muscle group that responds poorly to the rushed partial reps most people perform.
Exercise 76: Seated Calf Raise
Primary muscle: Calves — soleus, the lower calf muscle
Secondary muscles: Gastrocnemius minimal
Equipment: Seated calf raise machine
Experience level: All
Priority: Primary
Rep ranges: Working sets 12–15 reps. Higher rep sets 15–20 reps.
Coaching cues:
Sit on the machine with the knee pad resting just above the knees.
Balls of the feet on the platform, heels hanging off.
Lower the heels as far as possible — full stretch at the bottom, hold for one second.
Drive up onto the toes as high as possible — full contraction at the top, hold for one second.
Lower slowly under control.
The seated position bends the knee — this relaxes the gastrocnemius and isolates the soleus.
Common mistakes:
Same errors as standing calf raise — partial range, bouncing, rushing.
Knee pad too far up the thigh — reduces the load on the calves.
Injury contraindications:
Same as standing calf raise.
Generally more comfortable for Achilles tendon issues than the standing variation.
Substitutions: Standing calf raise / Leg press calf raise
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/seated-calf-raise
Notes: The seated position bends the knee which relaxes the gastrocnemius and isolates the soleus — the deeper calf muscle that sits underneath the gastrocnemius. Both standing and seated variations should be included regularly to fully develop the calf. The soleus is often neglected because most people only do standing calf raises.
Exercise 77: Leg Press Calf Raise
Primary muscle: Calves — gastrocnemius
Secondary muscles: Soleus minimal
Equipment: Leg press machine
Experience level: All
Priority: Primary
Rep ranges: Working sets 15–20 reps. Higher rep sets 20–25 reps.
Coaching cues:
Position only the balls of the feet at the bottom edge of the leg press platform — heels hanging off.
Keep the legs straight or with a very slight knee bend — do not push the platform away.
Lower the toes toward the body — full stretch in the calves.
Push the platform away by pointing the toes — full contraction at the top.
Hold at the top for one second — squeeze the calves.
Return slowly — full stretch at the bottom.
Use the safety handles — do not trust the platform to stay in place without them engaged.
Common mistakes:
Bending the knees and leg pressing the platform — not a calf raise at all.
Partial range — same critical error as all calf exercises.
Feet too high on the platform — reduces the range of motion.
Not using safety handles — the platform could fall if the calves give out.
Injury contraindications:
Same as standing calf raise.
Generally comfortable for most calf and ankle conditions.
Substitutions: Standing calf raise / Seated calf raise
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/leg-press-calf-raise
Notes: A good alternative when the standing calf raise machine is occupied or for clients who find the standing machine uncomfortable. Higher rep ranges work well here as the leg press calf raise allows a large range of motion in a comfortable seated position. Always use the safety handles — non negotiable on the leg press calf raise.
LEG DAY — CORE
Exercise 78: Leg Raises
Primary muscle: Core — lower abs, hip flexors
Secondary muscles: Rectus abdominis
Equipment: Flat bench or floor
Experience level: All
Priority: Primary
Rep ranges: Standard 12–15 reps. Advanced 15–20 reps.
Coaching cues:
Lie flat on a bench or the floor, hands under the glutes or gripping the sides of the bench.
Keep the legs straight or with a very slight bend in the knees.
Lower back pressed into the bench or floor — do not allow it to arch.
Raise the legs from the floor to vertical — smooth controlled movement.
At the top tilt the pelvis slightly — this increases lower ab activation at the top of the movement.
Lower slowly under full control — stop just before the heels touch the floor to keep tension on the abs.
Do not let the lower back arch away from the bench as the legs lower — if this happens reduce the range of motion.
Common mistakes:
Lower back arching — the most common error and the most dangerous. The lower back must stay flat throughout.
Swinging the legs — momentum rather than ab strength.
Letting the heels touch the floor between reps — removes tension.
Bending the knees excessively — reduces the lever arm and makes the exercise too easy.
Injury contraindications:
Lower back issues — avoid, the leg raise places significant demand on the lower back. Switch to crunches or decline sit ups.
Hip flexor tightness — the leg raise heavily involves the hip flexors, may cause hip flexor strain if tight.
Substitutions: Hanging leg raises — more advanced variation / Crunches — lower back friendly alternative / Decline sit ups
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/leg-raises
Notes: Primary core exercise — one of the best lower ab movements available. The lower back staying flat is the most important coaching point. Clients who cannot keep the lower back flat should reduce the range of motion until core strength improves.
Exercise 79: Hanging Leg Raises
Primary muscle: Core — lower abs, hip flexors
Secondary muscles: Rectus abdominis, grip, shoulders
Equipment: Pull up bar or hanging straps
Experience level: Intermediate and advanced
Priority: Primary
Rep ranges: Standard 10–15 reps. Advanced 15–20 reps.
Coaching cues:
Hang from a pull up bar at full arm extension — use straps if grip is a limiting factor.
Keep the body still — no swinging before starting.
Raise the legs by flexing at the hips — legs straight for maximum difficulty, knees bent for reduced difficulty.
At the top the legs should be parallel to the floor or higher — toes toward the bar for maximum difficulty.
Lower slowly under full control — do not let the legs drop.
Keep the body as still as possible throughout — no swinging.
Common mistakes:
Swinging the body — momentum rather than ab strength.
Letting the legs drop — rushing the lowering phase removes the eccentric stimulus.
Bending the knees to make it easier without acknowledging the progression — use bent knees intentionally as a regression, not unconsciously.
Not controlling the movement — the hanging position requires significant body awareness.
Injury contraindications:
Shoulder issues — the hanging position places demand on the shoulder joint, monitor carefully.
Grip issues — use straps to remove grip as a limiting factor.
Lower back issues — monitor carefully, reduce range of motion if pain occurs.
Substitutions: Leg raises on a bench — easier alternative / Crunches / Ab wheel rollout — alternative advanced core exercise
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/hanging-leg-raises
Notes: Intermediate and advanced clients only. The hanging variation is significantly more challenging than the bench variation as the body must be stabilised throughout. Straps are recommended for clients whose grip gives out before the abs are fatigued.
Exercise 80: Crunches
Primary muscle: Core — rectus abdominis, upper abs
Secondary muscles: Obliques minimal
Equipment: None — bodyweight
Experience level: All
Priority: Primary
Rep ranges: Standard 15–20 reps. Weighted 12–15 reps.
Coaching cues:
Lie on the floor, knees bent, feet flat on the floor.
Hands lightly behind the head or crossed on the chest — never pull on the neck.
Curl the upper body upward — the lower back stays on the floor throughout.
The movement is small — the shoulder blades lift off the floor, the lower back does not.
At the top squeeze the abs hard — hold for one second.
Lower slowly under control — shoulder blades almost touch the floor before the next rep.
Exhale on the way up, inhale on the way down.
Common mistakes:
Pulling on the neck — serious cervical spine strain risk, hands only lightly touch the head.
Lower back leaving the floor — this is a crunch not a sit up, the movement is small and isolated.
Rushing — fast crunches use momentum rather than ab strength.
Not squeezing at the top — the hold at the top maximises ab activation.
Injury contraindications:
Lower back issues — crunches are generally safe as the lower back stays on the floor. Monitor carefully.
Neck issues — hands on chest rather than behind the head.
Substitutions: Decline sit ups — more advanced alternative / Cable crunch — weighted alternative / Leg raises
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/crunches
Notes: The most accessible core exercise — requires no equipment and can be performed anywhere. The distinction between a crunch and a sit up is important — a crunch is a small isolated movement of the upper body, the lower back never leaves the floor. For weighted progression a plate can be held on the chest.
Exercise 81: Decline Sit Ups
Primary muscle: Core — rectus abdominis, full activation
Secondary muscles: Hip flexors, obliques
Equipment: Decline bench
Experience level: All
Priority: Primary
Rep ranges: Standard 12–15 reps. Weighted 10–12 reps.
Coaching cues:
Secure the feet under the foot pads on the decline bench.
Hands crossed on the chest or lightly behind the head.
Lower the torso slowly toward the bench — full range of motion, shoulder blades touch the bench.
Drive back up by contracting the abs — do not use momentum.
At the top squeeze the abs — slight crunch at the top to maximise contraction.
Keep the movement controlled throughout — no swinging or jerking.
Common mistakes:
Using momentum to sit up — pull with the abs, not the hip flexors.
Not achieving full range at the bottom — shoulder blades must touch the bench.
Hands pulling on the neck — same risk as crunches.
Too steep a decline — reduces control and increases hip flexor involvement over ab involvement.
Injury contraindications:
Lower back issues — the full range sit up movement places more demand on the lower back than a crunch, monitor carefully.
Hip flexor issues — the decline sit up heavily involves the hip flexors, may cause discomfort.
High blood pressure — the inverted position may be uncomfortable, monitor.
Substitutions: Crunches — less range of motion alternative / Cable crunch — seated alternative / Leg raises
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/decline-sit-ups
Notes: A more challenging core exercise than standard crunches due to the full range of motion and the decline angle. For weighted progression a plate can be held on the chest. The decline sit up trains the rectus abdominis through a greater range of motion than a crunch — both should be included regularly for full ab development.
Exercise 82: Weighted Crunches
Primary muscle: Core — rectus abdominis
Secondary muscles: Obliques minimal
Equipment: Weight plate or dumbbell, flat floor or decline bench
Experience level: Intermediate and advanced
Priority: Primary
Rep ranges: Working sets 12–15 reps. Heavy sets 10–12 reps.
Coaching cues:
Same technique as standard crunches — lower back stays on the floor, movement is small and isolated.
Hold a weight plate on the chest — start light and increase progressively.
The added weight increases the resistance at the point of contraction — this drives ab development beyond what bodyweight crunches can achieve.
Squeeze hard at the top — the weight makes the hold more challenging and more effective.
Lower slowly — the weight increases the eccentric demand.
Common mistakes:
Too much weight too soon — form breaks down, neck pulls forward.
Holding the weight at arm’s length above the head — changes the lever arm and stresses the shoulder, always hold on the chest.
Same errors as standard crunches apply.
Injury contraindications:
Lower back issues — same as standard crunches, monitor.
Neck issues — start very light, ensure the neck is not strained.
Substitutions: Standard crunches — bodyweight progression / Cable crunch — alternative weighted ab exercise / Decline sit ups with weight
YouTube link: To be filmed by coach — plus4performance.com/videos/weighted-crunches
Notes: Intermediate and advanced clients only. Progressive overload applies to core training just as it does to all other muscle groups — adding weight to crunches drives ab development beyond what high rep bodyweight crunches can achieve. Start with a light plate — 5 or 10kg — and increase progressively. The abs respond to progressive overload just like any other muscle.
Section 5 — Cardio Guidelines
Cover:
How much cardio you prescribe for fat loss vs muscle building vs maintenance
Your preferred cardio types and why — LISS, HIIT, walking etc
How cardio fits around weight training sessions
Rest day cardio recommendations
How cardio changes across the 12 weeks
COACHING BIBLE — SECTION 5: CARDIO GUIDELINES
5.1 — Cardio philosophy and principles
Cardio is non-negotiable regardless of goal. Whether a client is building muscle, losing fat or maintaining weight, cardiovascular health matters and cardio supports all three. The heart is a muscle — it needs to be trained like one. Clients who neglect cardio entirely while lifting weights are building a body that looks strong on the outside but is cardiovascularly underdeveloped underneath. That is not the standard this programme holds.
Cardio in this programme serves three distinct purposes. First — calorie expenditure. Cardio burns calories and contributes to the energy deficit required for fat loss or helps maintain energy balance during maintenance phases. Second — cardiovascular health. Regular low intensity cardio improves heart health, reduces resting heart rate, improves blood pressure and increases overall fitness capacity. These benefits exist regardless of goal and are programmed accordingly. Third — active recovery. Low intensity cardio on training days and rest days increases blood flow to the muscles, reduces soreness and accelerates recovery between sessions.
The cardio approach in this programme is built around sustainability and simplicity. Clients do not need to run marathons, attend HIIT classes five times a week or spend hours on a treadmill. They need to move consistently, hit their step target daily and complete the post session cardio prescribed with their weight training. That is enough — and it is enough because it compounds over 12 weeks into a significant and measurable contribution to their results.
Cardio and weight training are never in competition. They coexist. The weight training builds the muscle. The cardio supports fat loss, heart health and recovery. Removing either undermines the whole.
5.2 — Cardio by goal
The cardio prescription changes based on the client's goal. The type of cardio — low intensity steady state — remains the same across all goals. What changes is the volume, the duration and the frequency.
Fat loss — cardio is a tool for increasing calorie expenditure beyond what diet alone achieves. The goal is to create a meaningful calorie deficit without compromising muscle mass or recovery capacity. Too little cardio and fat loss stalls. Too much cardio and muscle mass is sacrificed, hunger increases and recovery suffers. The sweet spot is structured post session cardio plus a meaningful daily step target. Cardio volume increases progressively across the 12 weeks as the body adapts — see section 5.6.
Muscle building — cardio is kept low to preserve the calorie surplus required for muscle growth. Excessive cardio on a muscle building programme burns calories that should be going toward building muscle and increases recovery demand that competes with the weight training. Post session cardio is short and low intensity — its purpose is cardiovascular health and recovery, not calorie burning. If a client is struggling to eat enough to maintain their surplus, post session cardio duration is reduced temporarily. The calorie surplus is always the priority on a muscle building programme.
Maintenance — cardio sits between the fat loss and muscle building prescriptions. Moderate post session cardio and a consistent daily step target support body recomposition — the simultaneous loss of fat and gain of muscle that is most achievable at maintenance calories. Cardio is consistent and moderate throughout the 12 weeks with no significant progression unless body composition goals shift.
5.3 — Post session cardio protocols
Post session cardio is performed immediately after every weight training session. It is not optional and not separate — it is built into the session as the final component. The client finishes their last working set, completes their core work and moves directly to post session cardio without leaving the gym.
Post session cardio is always low intensity steady state. The preferred methods in order of preference are:
Incline treadmill walk — the default post session cardio method. An incline of 8-12 percent at a walking pace of 5-6 kilometres per hour. Low impact, effective calorie burn, zero recovery demand. The incline increases the calorie burn significantly compared to flat walking without adding any additional stress to the joints or the cardiovascular system.
Stationary bike — second preference. Low impact, comfortable seated position, easy to maintain a consistent low intensity output. Good for clients with joint issues who find incline walking uncomfortable.
Stair climber — third preference. Slightly higher intensity than incline walking or the bike but still within the low intensity steady state range. Good for clients who want a higher calorie burn from their post session cardio without approaching HIIT intensity.
Post session cardio duration by goal and phase:
Fat loss clients:
Weeks 1-4 — 10 minutes post session
Weeks 5-8 — 15 minutes post session
Weeks 9-12 — 20 minutes post session
Muscle building clients:
All 12 weeks — 10 minutes post session
Reducible to 5 minutes or removed entirely if calorie surplus is difficult to maintain
Maintenance clients:
All 12 weeks — 10-15 minutes post session
Consistent throughout, no significant progression
Post session cardio is performed at a conversational pace — the client should be able to hold a conversation throughout. If breathing becomes laboured the intensity is too high. The purpose is active recovery and additional calorie burn, not cardiovascular training to exhaustion.
5.4 — Step goal guidelines
The daily step goal runs throughout the entire 12 weeks as a background habit separate from structured cardio sessions. Steps are not a workout — they are a daily activity target that compounds into a significant calorie burn over the course of a week, a month and 12 weeks.
The step goal is the most underrated fat loss tool available. Ten thousand steps per day at an average pace burns approximately 400-500 calories — that is a meaningful contribution to a weekly deficit without any recovery cost, any gym equipment or any structured effort. It is simply moving more throughout the day.
Step goals by goal:
Fat loss clients:
Weeks 1-4 — 8,000 steps per day
Weeks 5-8 — 9,000 steps per day
Weeks 9-12 — 10,000 steps per day
If weight loss stalls — step goal increases before cardio duration increases
Muscle building clients:
All 12 weeks — 7,000-8,000 steps per day
Consistent throughout — steps support cardiovascular health without burning excess calories
Maintenance clients:
All 12 weeks — 8,000-9,000 steps per day
Consistent throughout
Step tracking is done through the client's phone or a fitness tracker. The plan includes a recommendation to track steps daily and log them in the progress tracker weekly. Clients who consistently fall short of their step target are advised to build walking into their existing routine — walking to work, walking at lunch, walking after dinner — rather than treating it as a separate dedicated activity.
The step goal and the post session cardio are treated as two entirely separate things in the plan. Steps are a daily background habit. Post session cardio is a structured component of the training session. Neither replaces the other.
5.5 — HIIT protocol
HIIT is a last resort — not a default. It is introduced only in two circumstances: when fat loss has stalled despite consistent post session cardio and a high step goal, or when a client specifically requests it. For all other clients steady state cardio is prescribed throughout the full 12 weeks.
The reason HIIT is not the default is simple. HIIT is high intensity — it demands recovery just like a weights session. A client already training 4 days a week with post session cardio and a high step goal does not need additional recovery demand placed on their system. Adding HIIT on top of this increases injury risk, slows recovery and frequently leads to burnout and programme abandonment. Steady state cardio gives 80% of the benefit with a fraction of the recovery cost.
When HIIT is introduced it replaces one post session cardio slot — it does not add an additional session on top of the existing programme. The client performs HIIT instead of their standard post session cardio on one day per week maximum.
HIIT protocol and progression:
Phase 1 — introduction:
Work to rest ratio 1:2 — 20 seconds work, 40 seconds rest
6-8 rounds
Total session time approximately 6-8 minutes excluding warm up
Preferred methods — stationary bike sprints, treadmill sprints, rowing machine intervals
Phase 2 — progression:
Work to rest ratio 1:1.5 — 20 seconds work, 30 seconds rest
8-10 rounds
Total session time approximately 8-10 minutes
Phase 3 — advanced:
Work to rest ratio 1:1 — 20 seconds work, 20 seconds rest
10 rounds
Total session time approximately 10 minutes
Once 1:1 ratio is achieved comfortably, rounds are added rather than further reducing rest
HIIT intensity — the work periods must be genuinely high intensity. Not uncomfortable jog pace — maximum or near maximum effort. If the client can hold a conversation during the work period the intensity is not high enough. The rest periods are complete rest — step off the machine, recover, go again.
HIIT warm up — always perform 3-5 minutes of low intensity cardio before HIIT. Never go directly from rest to maximum effort sprints — the injury risk is significant.
HIIT is never programmed on the same day as a leg session — the combination of heavy lower body training and lower body HIIT creates excessive recovery demand and injury risk. HIIT is programmed on upper body training days only.
5.6 — Cardio progression across 12 weeks
Cardio progression is dynamic — it responds to results rather than following a rigid weekly schedule. The primary driver of cardio progression is body weight data from the progress tracker. The rule is simple:
If the client is losing weight at the target rate — cardio stays the same. If weight loss stalls for two consecutive weeks — cardio increases. If weight loss is too fast — cardio decreases or calories increase.
For fat loss clients the progression structure is built into the plan as a default — duration increases at weeks 5 and 9 as established in section 5.3. These are baseline progressions that apply regardless of results. If weight loss stalls before these checkpoints the progression is accelerated. If weight loss is progressing well at the scheduled checkpoint the progression still applies — maintaining momentum matters.
For muscle building clients cardio does not progress across 12 weeks. It stays consistent at 10 minutes post session throughout. If the client is struggling to maintain their calorie surplus cardio is reduced — the surplus is always the priority.
For maintenance clients cardio stays consistent throughout the 12 weeks. The body recomposition process at maintenance calories is slow and requires consistency rather than escalating intervention.
Incline and resistance levels on cardio equipment can be increased as a secondary progression tool once duration is established. A client who has been performing incline treadmill walks at 8% incline progressively increases to 10% and then 12% as fitness improves. Resistance on the stationary bike increases similarly. Duration always increases before intensity — building the habit of consistent cardio comes before making it harder.
For HIIT progressions — see section 5.5 for the full phase structure.
Cardio is logged in the progress tracker — date, type, duration and intensity level. This data is used at the monthly check-in to assess whether cardio volume is appropriate for the client's current rate of progress. If cardio is consistently not being completed this is addressed before adjusting food targets or training volume — compliance is always checked first.
Section 6 — Nutrition Philosophy
Your beliefs about food and diet. Not rules yet — your thinking.
Cover:
How you view the relationship between training and nutrition
Your stance on flexible dieting vs strict meal plans
How you think about cheat meals — frequency, approach, mindset
Your view on supplements — what you recommend and why
How important meal timing is in your opinion
Your approach to hydration
6.1 — How you view the relationship between training and nutrition
Nutrition is the harder discipline and the more important one. Most people can show up to the gym and train reasonably well — the environment, the equipment and the structure of a session make it relatively straightforward to execute. Nutrition happens outside the gym, across every meal, every day, in every situation — at work, at restaurants, at family events, when tired, when stressed, when motivation is low. It is harder to get right and easier to get wrong, which is why most people who train consistently but do not see results are failing on the nutrition side not the training side.
The relationship between training and nutrition is not one of competition — it is one of multiplication. Training provides the stimulus. Nutrition provides the materials. Without the stimulus the materials sit unused. Without the materials the stimulus produces nothing. A client who trains perfectly but eats poorly will make minimal progress. A client who eats perfectly but trains poorly will make more progress than most people expect — because calorie balance and protein intake drive the majority of body composition change. But a client who gets both right will achieve results that neither discipline could produce alone.
You cannot out-train a bad diet. This is not a motivational phrase — it is a mathematical reality. A one hour weight training session burns approximately 300-500 calories. A single poor food choice can replace those calories in minutes. The gym creates the demand. The kitchen determines whether that demand is met with the right materials or the wrong ones.
Meal timing is not a priority. The total calories consumed and the total protein consumed across the day matter far more than when those calories and that protein are eaten. Clients who stress about eating within a 30 minute post-workout window but routinely miss their daily protein target are focusing on the wrong thing entirely. Get the totals right first. Once the totals are consistently right, meal timing can be optimised as a secondary consideration.
Abs are revealed in the kitchen — not made there. The abdominal muscles are built through training — direct core work and compound movements that engage the core throughout every session. But they are hidden under body fat that only a sustained calorie deficit removes. No amount of ab training reveals abs on a client who is not in a calorie deficit. And no amount of dieting builds the ab musculature without direct training. Both are required — the kitchen reveals them, the training builds them.
6.2 — Your stance on flexible dieting vs strict meal plans
Both approaches have a place in this programme — the right choice depends on the client's experience level, nutritional knowledge and lifestyle. The goal of both approaches is the same: hit the daily calorie and macro targets consistently. The method used to get there is secondary to the outcome.
For beginners a full meal plan is the default recommendation. Beginners do not yet have the nutritional knowledge to make consistently good flexible choices. A full meal plan removes the decision making burden, builds an understanding of what a day of eating should look like and establishes habits before flexibility is introduced. The first four weeks of the programme are meal plan led for beginner clients — structured, specific and simple to follow. From week five onward the transition to flexible macro tracking begins as the client develops nutritional literacy.
For intermediate and advanced clients flexible macro tracking is the preferred approach. It is more sustainable than a strict meal plan, adapts to real life situations and builds genuine long term nutritional literacy. A strict meal plan breaks down the moment life gets in the way — a restaurant meal, a holiday, a work event. Flexible dieting adapts to these situations because the client understands their targets and knows how to hit them regardless of what they are eating. This is the nutritional skill that produces lifelong results rather than 12 week results.
Tracking apps are recommended for all clients — MyFitnessPal and Cronometer are the primary recommendations. Tracking creates awareness. Most clients significantly underestimate their calorie intake before they start tracking — seeing the numbers removes the guesswork and creates accountability. The long term goal is to build a proprietary tracking tool within the Plus 4 Performance platform that integrates directly with the plan and the progress tracker.
Food should be weighed wherever possible — to the gram. Estimating portion sizes produces significant inaccuracies that compound over time. A client who estimates their peanut butter portion is routinely eating 50-100 calories more than they think — across a week that is 350-700 additional calories that do not exist in their tracking. Weigh the food, log it accurately and trust the numbers. Once a client has weighed the same foods consistently for several weeks they develop an accurate eye for portion sizes — but weighing remains the gold standard throughout the programme.
Clients who refuse to track or weigh food are advised clearly and honestly — this approach will not maximise their progress. Eating clean without tracking is better than eating poorly but it leaves significant results on the table. The client who eats well but does not track their intake has no way of knowing whether they are in a deficit, at maintenance or in a surplus. They are navigating without a map. This is communicated without judgment — the choice is theirs — but the consequences are explained honestly.
The 80/20 principle applies to nutrition compliance. Hitting targets 80% of the time produces results for the majority of clients — the 20% flexibility allows for real life without guilt or derailment. For clients with more serious goals — competitors, advanced clients with aggressive timelines — compliance expectations increase accordingly. The plan sets the compliance expectation based on the client's stated goal and adjusts the language around strictness accordingly.
No food is labelled good or bad. This programme promotes whole foods and educates clients on the difference between high volume nutrient dense foods and low volume calorie dense foods — without banning anything. A client who understands that 500 calories of chicken, rice and vegetables fills them up for hours while 500 calories of processed food barely touches the sides makes better choices through knowledge rather than restriction. The phrase used throughout the plan is simple: no food is off limits — some foods just make hitting your targets easier than others. This framework removes guilt, prevents bingeing and builds a healthy long term relationship with food.
Clients who display signs of an obsessive or disordered relationship with food are handled with care and without judgment. Signs include extreme calorie restriction beyond the programmed deficit, fear of specific foods, guilt and distress after eating anything off plan, social isolation to avoid food situations and anxiety driven over-tracking. When these signs are present the coaching priority shifts. The client is acknowledged for their dedication, the concern is raised gently and directly, and professional support is recommended. The plan continues with less aggressive targets in the meantime. A coach's responsibility is to improve health in its fullest sense — physical and mental. An obsessive relationship with food is a health issue that a nutrition plan alone cannot and should not attempt to address.
6.3 — Your view on cheat meals
The term used in this programme is cheat meal. The language is intentional — it acknowledges that the meal sits outside the plan without dressing it up as something clinical or calculated. Clients understand what a cheat meal is, they look forward to it, and calling it something else adds unnecessary complexity.
Cheat meals are built into the programme for every client regardless of goal. Enjoying life is ultimately more important than perfect adherence — a programme that leaves no room for enjoyment is not sustainable and sustainability is the only metric that produces long term results. One reasonable cheat meal per week is not a setback. It is a feature.
Cheat meal frequency by goal:
Fat loss — one cheat meal per week maximum. The weekly calorie deficit provides a buffer that absorbs one reasonable cheat meal without derailing progress. More than one per week and the weekly deficit begins to erode to the point where fat loss stalls. One is the limit — non-negotiable on a fat loss programme.
Muscle building — one to two cheat meals per week is acceptable. The calorie surplus provides more buffer than a deficit and the body is in a growth state — one or two reasonable cheat meals have minimal impact on the overall weekly surplus and on muscle building progress.
Maintenance — one to two cheat meals per week. Maintenance calories already provide more dietary flexibility than a cut and the impact of a cheat meal on weekly totals is smaller. Recomposition progress at maintenance is slower than dedicated cutting or bulking — the additional flexibility reflects this.
The cheat meal has one rule — keep it reasonable. One meal, not one day. A burger and chips is a cheat meal. Three pizzas followed by dessert and a takeaway the next morning is not. The difference is not about being perfect — it is about not undoing a week of good work in a single sitting. Clients who consistently turn one cheat meal into a full cheat day or a full weekend are told honestly and directly — this is why progress has stalled. The cheat meal is built into the programme to support adherence, not to provide a weekly opportunity to start again from zero.
Cheat meals are off the books. Clients do not track their cheat meal. The psychological value of a cheat meal comes from the freedom it provides — removing the tracking requirement is part of that freedom. The weekly calorie structure is built to absorb one untracked meal. Clients who find themselves significantly overeating on cheat meals week after week are encouraged to have a loose awareness of what they are consuming — not full tracking, but enough awareness to keep it in the realm of reasonable rather than excessive.
Planned refeeds are a separate and more advanced tool. A refeed is a structured higher carbohydrate day built into the programme for clients in prolonged aggressive calorie deficits who are experiencing significant fatigue, strength loss or psychological burnout from sustained restriction. Unlike a cheat meal a refeed is calculated — calories are set at or slightly above maintenance with carbohydrates significantly increased and fat kept low. Refeeds replenish muscle glycogen, temporarily boost leptin levels and provide a physiological and psychological reset. They are not the same as a cheat meal and are not the default for most clients — they are an advanced intervention for specific circumstances and are introduced by the coach when the signs indicate they are needed.

Collagen peptides — include but only if client asks, do not promote proactively
Ashwagandha — removed

6.4 — Your view on supplements
Supplements are exactly what the name suggests — supplementary. They support a good diet and a good training programme. They do not replace either. A client with poor nutrition and inconsistent training who invests heavily in supplements is wasting their money. A client with excellent nutrition and consistent training who uses the right supplements intelligently will see a meaningful additional benefit. The order of priority is always: diet first, training second, supplements third.
The non-negotiable supplements recommended for every client regardless of goal are:
Protein powder — essential for hitting daily protein targets. Whole food protein sources are always preferred but protein powder bridges the gap when whole food intake falls short. It is a convenient, cost effective and well evidenced tool for supporting muscle growth and retention. Whey protein is the default recommendation — fast absorbing and complete amino acid profile. Plant based alternatives — pea protein, rice protein blend — for vegan and dairy free clients.
Creatine monohydrate — the single most evidence backed performance supplement available. Increases phosphocreatine stores in the muscle allowing greater output during high intensity efforts — more reps, more weight, better performance. Five grams per day, every day, no loading phase required. Creatine monohydrate is the recommended form — ignore expensive branded alternatives, the basic monohydrate is identical in efficacy at a fraction of the cost. Safe for long term use, well tolerated by the vast majority of clients.
The recommended supplement stack for all clients beyond the non-negotiables:
Vitamin D — the majority of people in the UK are deficient, particularly through winter months. Vitamin D supports immune function, bone health, mood and testosterone levels. 2000-4000 IU daily with a meal containing fat for optimal absorption.
Vitamin C — supports immune function and collagen synthesis. Particularly relevant for clients under high training stress. 500-1000mg daily.
Zinc and magnesium glycinate — zinc supports testosterone production and immune function. Magnesium glycinate — the preferred form over magnesium oxide which is poorly absorbed — supports sleep quality, muscle recovery and reduces cortisol. ZMA supplements combine both and are a convenient option. Taken before bed.
Omega 3 fish oil — strong evidence for reducing training induced inflammation, supporting joint health and cardiovascular health. Two to three grams of combined EPA and DHA daily. Particularly important for clients with joint issues or those training under heavy load over a sustained period.
EAAs — essential amino acids support muscle protein synthesis, particularly useful around training sessions and for clients who struggle to hit protein targets from whole food sources alone.
Electrolytes — support hydration and performance, particularly for clients doing significant cardio, training in warm environments or anyone who sweats heavily during sessions. Often overlooked but important for maintaining performance and recovery.
Goal specific additions:
Fat loss — caffeine before training is well evidenced for improved performance, increased focus and enhanced fat oxidation. A strong black coffee or a caffeine supplement at 150-200mg is the recommendation — pre-workouts are not necessary and most are overpriced for what they deliver. Green tea extract provides a mild additional thermogenic effect. L-carnitine has some evidence for fat metabolism support though the evidence is weaker than caffeine.
Muscle building — beta alanine is an optional addition for clients who want to support training performance and endurance during high volume sessions. The tingling sensation — paraesthesia — is harmless and reduces with consistent use. Not essential but a legitimate addition for clients who want to optimise their stack.
On collagen peptides — there is emerging evidence for the role of collagen peptides in supporting joint health and connective tissue integrity under heavy training load. This is not proactively recommended as part of the standard supplement stack — the research is still developing. However if a client asks specifically about joint support or connective tissue health collagen peptides are a reasonable addition to discuss.
On pre-workouts — caffeine before training is a personal choice and a legitimate one. The evidence for caffeine as a performance enhancer is strong. Most commercial pre-workouts are built around caffeine with additional ingredients that are either underdosed, unproven or both. A strong black coffee achieves the same result at a fraction of the cost. If a client insists on a branded pre-workout the advice is to check the caffeine content — 150-200mg is the effective dose — and treat everything else as marketing.
On fat burners — not recommended. The vast majority of fat burning supplements are ineffective and overpriced. The ingredients that do have evidence — caffeine, green tea extract — are available individually at a fraction of the cost of a branded fat burner. No supplement burns fat. A calorie deficit burns fat. Supplements can support the process marginally — they cannot replace it.
On SARMs and substances to avoid — SARMs, prohormones, testosterone boosters containing unlisted active compounds, DNP and any supplement making dramatic promises in a short timeframe are actively discouraged. DNP is particularly dangerous — several deaths have been attributed to its use. The rule is simple: if it promises dramatic results quickly it is dangerous, illegal or ineffective. Usually all three. Clients are advised to research any supplement thoroughly before using it and to avoid anything that cannot be purchased transparently from a reputable retailer.
Clients who want to use supplements beyond what is recommended here are supported rather than restricted — the recommendation is always to research the evidence, avoid anything dangerous and prioritise the fundamentals first. The supplements listed above are what the coach has found to work personally and professionally. They are recommended because they are evidence backed, safe and cost effective — not because of any commercial relationship with supplement companies.
Good — clear and honest. Let me fill the gaps:
On pre workout meal specifics
Here's what the evidence supports for a pre workout meal 2 hours before training:
High carbohydrate — carbs are the primary fuel source for weight training. Oats, rice, sweet potato, banana, toast are all excellent options
Moderate protein — supports muscle protein synthesis before and during training. Chicken, eggs, Greek yogurt, protein shake
Low fat — fat slows digestion and can cause discomfort during training
Low fibre — same reason, high fibre foods before training can cause GI discomfort
If training first thing in the morning and a full meal 2 hours before is not practical — a smaller snack 30-45 minutes before works. Something simple — banana and a protein shake, toast with peanut butter, a small bowl of oats. Enough to fuel the session without sitting heavily in the stomach.
On the post workout anabolic window
The anabolic window is real but much wider than the fitness industry has traditionally suggested. It is not a 30 minute window that slams shut — it is more accurately a 2-3 hour window post training where protein synthesis is elevated and the body is primed to use nutrients effectively. Getting protein and carbohydrates in within this window is beneficial but it does not need to happen the moment the last set is finished. Cereal and a protein shake is a solid post workout meal — fast digesting carbs replenish glycogen, protein powder delivers amino acids quickly. Practical, effective and easy to prepare.
On intermittent fasting
The evidence for intermittent fasting as a superior fat loss method is weak. It works for some people because it naturally reduces the eating window and therefore makes hitting a calorie deficit easier — not because fasting has any metabolic magic. The problem with recommending it broadly is that it often leads to undereating protein — cramming sufficient protein into a restricted eating window is harder than spreading it across a full day. For clients who genuinely enjoy the IF approach and find it sustainable there is no reason to discourage it — but it is not recommended as a default because most clients do not need the additional restriction on top of a calorie deficit.
On eating before bed
The evidence here is clear — total daily calories matter, not when they are consumed. Eating before bed does not cause fat gain if the client is within their daily calorie target. The reason eating before bed has a bad reputation is because most late night eating is additional calories on top of a full day of eating — not because the timing itself is harmful. If a client has calories remaining at the end of the day eating before bed is completely fine. The only practical consideration is avoiding very large meals immediately before sleep as this can affect sleep quality — but a moderate meal or a snack an hour or two before bed is not a problem.
