6.5 — Your view on meal timing
Meal timing is not the priority — total daily calories and total daily protein are. A client who hits their targets across the day will make progress regardless of when those targets are hit. However certain meals have standard ideal timings that reflect common sense and practical experience rather than rigid rules, and two specific timing windows — pre workout and post workout — are important enough to structure deliberately.
General meal structure follows a natural rhythm for most clients. Breakfast is oats or another slow releasing carbohydrate source — setting up stable energy levels for the morning. Mid morning and afternoon meals are typically protein led with complex carbohydrates — chicken, rice, vegetables in various combinations. The specific meal composition is detailed in the nutrition plan and adjusted based on training time. The post workout meal is the one meal that remains consistent regardless of when the client trains — it is always the meal that follows the training session.
Pre workout nutrition — a meal should be consumed approximately two hours before training. The pre workout meal is high in carbohydrates, moderate in protein and low in fat and fibre. Carbohydrates are the primary fuel source for weight training — going into a session depleted of carbohydrates reduces performance significantly. Good pre workout meal options include oats with protein powder, chicken and rice, eggs on toast or sweet potato with a protein source. Fat and fibre are kept low as both slow digestion and can cause discomfort during training.
For clients training first thing in the morning where a full meal two hours before is not practical — a smaller snack 30 to 45 minutes before training is sufficient. A banana and a protein shake, toast with peanut butter or a small bowl of oats provides enough fuel for the session without sitting heavily in the stomach.
Post workout nutrition — protein and carbohydrates should be consumed within two to three hours of completing a training session. The post workout window is real but wider than the fitness industry traditionally suggests — it is not a 30 minute window that closes and eliminates the benefit of the session. It is a two to three hour period where protein synthesis is elevated and the body is primed to use nutrients effectively. The recommended post workout meal is simple and practical — cereal and a protein shake. Fast digesting carbohydrates replenish muscle glycogen, protein powder delivers amino acids quickly and the combination is easy to prepare and consume immediately after training. Clients who prefer whole food post workout meals — chicken and rice, eggs and toast — are equally well served as long as both protein and carbohydrates are present.
On intermittent fasting — not recommended as a default approach. The evidence for intermittent fasting as a superior fat loss method is weak — it works for some people because restricting the eating window naturally reduces total calorie intake, not because fasting has any metabolic advantage. The practical problem with intermittent fasting for clients in this programme is protein distribution — hitting sufficient daily protein within a restricted eating window is harder than spreading intake across a full day, and protein distribution across multiple meals supports muscle protein synthesis more effectively than cramming the same amount into two or three meals. Clients who enjoy intermittent fasting and find it sustainable are not discouraged — but it is not recommended proactively and protein targets must still be met within whatever eating window is used.
On eating before bed — total daily calories determine body composition, not when those calories are consumed. Eating before bed does not cause fat gain if the client is within their daily calorie target. The reputation of late night eating as fattening comes from the reality that most late night eating is additional calories on top of a full day — not from any timing based mechanism. If a client has calories remaining at the end of the day eating before bed is completely acceptable. Very large meals immediately before sleep can affect sleep quality — a moderate meal or snack one to two hours before bed is the sensible recommendation. A slow digesting protein source before bed — cottage cheese, Greek yogurt, casein protein — is actually beneficial for overnight muscle protein synthesis and is actively encouraged for clients focused on muscle building.
Meal timing around training time — morning trainers and evening trainers follow the same pre and post workout nutrition principles, the timing of the rest of their meals adjusts around their session. Morning trainers eat their larger carbohydrate meals earlier in the day when training demand is highest. Evening trainers spread carbohydrates more evenly across the day with a solid pre workout meal in the afternoon. The plan adjusts meal timing recommendations based on the client's stated preferred training time from the Stage 2 inputs.
6.6 — Your view on hydration
Hydration is one of the most consistently neglected aspects of performance and body composition. Most people are chronically mildly dehydrated without realising it — and even mild dehydration of two percent of body weight has a measurable negative impact on strength, endurance, cognitive function and recovery. Clients who train hard, eat well and sleep adequately but chronically underdrink are leaving results on the table.
The daily water target for all clients is four to five litres per day. This is higher than standard general health recommendations and reflects the increased demand of regular hard training. On training days the target increases slightly to account for sweat losses — particularly for clients doing significant cardio or training in warm environments. Electrolytes support hydration efficiency — plain water in very high volumes without adequate electrolytes can actually impair hydration. Clients hitting four to five litres daily are advised to ensure their diet contains adequate sodium, potassium and magnesium or to supplement with electrolytes.
Water intake during training is actively encouraged. A water bottle is brought to every session — this is non-negotiable. During a standard 45 to 90 minute weight training session approximately 500-750ml should be consumed across the session, sipped consistently rather than all at once. Clients who train without water and feel fatigued, get headaches or experience strength drops mid session are almost certainly dehydrated.
Hydration around training follows a simple three stage framework. Before training — 500ml of water in the hour before the session to arrive at training well hydrated. During training — 500-750ml sipped consistently throughout. After training — a minimum of 500ml immediately post session to begin rehydration, more for clients who sweat heavily or have completed significant cardio.
On other drinks — coffee and tea are fine and contribute to daily fluid intake. A small amount of milk in coffee and tea is acceptable — clients are advised to avoid large milky drinks in significant quantities as the calories add up quickly without contributing meaningfully to satiety or nutrition targets. One sugar free fizzy drink per day is acceptable — sugar free options do not contribute calories and for clients who find plain water monotonous they provide a useful alternative. Regular full sugar fizzy drinks are not recommended as a daily habit — the sugar content adds empty calories without any nutritional benefit.
On alcohol — alcohol is not banned but its impact on body composition is explained clearly and honestly so clients can make informed choices. Alcohol contains seven calories per gram — more than protein or carbohydrates — and suppresses fat oxidation for up to 24 hours after consumption, meaning the body prioritises clearing alcohol over burning fat during that window. It disrupts sleep quality which undermines recovery and muscle protein synthesis. It lowers food inhibitions — the poor food choices that often follow a night of drinking are as damaging to progress as the alcohol itself. Life is for living and alcohol in moderation is understood — but its effects are not hidden from clients.
When clients choose to drink the recommendation is vodka, lime and soda — the lowest calorie mainstream option with no sugar mixers. Beer, wine and sugary cocktails carry significantly higher calorie loads and are less compatible with a fat loss or muscle building programme. Clients who drink regularly are advised to factor alcohol calories into their weekly total and to accept that drinking will slow progress — how much it slows progress is proportional to how much and how often they drink.
On sports drinks — not recommended for recreational gym goers doing standard weight training sessions. Sports drinks are formulated for endurance athletes depleting glycogen at a rate that recreational lifters do not approach during a 45-90 minute weights session. The sugar content is unnecessary and counterproductive for clients trying to manage their calorie intake. Water and electrolytes meet all hydration needs during a standard session. The exception is clients doing very long sessions of 90 minutes or more or clients combining heavy weight training with significant cardio — in these circumstances a small amount of fast digesting carbohydrate during the session is acceptable. A banana, a handful of gummy bears or a small amount of fruit juice achieves the same result as a sports drink at a fraction of the cost and with better nutritional context.

7.1 — How to calculate maintenance calories
Maintenance calories — also known as Total Daily Energy Expenditure or TDEE — represent the number of calories a client needs to consume each day to maintain their current body weight. Every nutrition plan in this programme starts with an accurate TDEE calculation. Everything else — the deficit, the surplus, the macro split — is built on top of this number.
TDEE is calculated in two stages. First the Basal Metabolic Rate is calculated — the number of calories the body burns at complete rest just to maintain basic physiological functions. Second the BMR is multiplied by an activity multiplier that accounts for the client's daily movement and exercise output.
BMR is calculated using the Mifflin St Jeor equation — the most accurate widely available BMR formula for the general population:
Male: BMR = (10 x weight in kg) + (6.25 x height in cm) - (5 x age) + 5 Female: BMR = (10 x weight in kg) + (6.25 x height in cm) - (5 x age) - 161
The activity multiplier is applied to the BMR based on the client's activity level from the Stage 1 slider input:
Activity level
Multiplier
Description
1 — Sedentary
1.2
Desk job, no exercise, mostly sitting
2 — Lightly active
1.375
Light walking, on feet at work, no structured exercise
3 — Moderately active
1.55
1-3 workouts per week, moderately active day
4 — Very active
1.725
3-5 workouts per week, active lifestyle
5 — Extremely active
1.9
Training daily or physical job on top of training
TDEE = BMR x activity multiplier
The TDEE produced by this calculation is the starting point — not a fixed number. Body weight, training intensity and daily activity all fluctuate. Clients are advised to treat their TDEE as an estimate that is refined over the first two to four weeks of the programme based on actual results. If weight is stable at the calculated TDEE the number is accurate. If weight is dropping the actual TDEE is higher than calculated. If weight is rising the actual TDEE is lower than calculated. The plan is adjusted accordingly at the monthly check-in.
The TDEE is calculated automatically from the Stage 1 inputs — age, sex, height, weight and activity level — and displayed to the client on the results page before they proceed to Stage 2. This is the free output that hooks the client into the funnel.
7.2 — How to set calorie targets by goal
Once TDEE is established the daily calorie target is set based on the client's goal. The calorie target is the single most important number in the nutrition plan — it determines whether the client loses fat, builds muscle or maintains weight.
Fat loss — the daily calorie target is set at a deficit below TDEE. The size of the deficit is determined by the rate of fat loss the client wants to achieve:
Deficit size
Daily calories
Weekly deficit
Expected fat loss
Moderate — recommended
TDEE minus 500 kcal
3,500 kcal
Approximately 0.5kg per week
Aggressive
TDEE minus 750 kcal
5,250 kcal
Approximately 0.75kg per week
Very aggressive
TDEE minus 1,000 kcal
7,000 kcal
Approximately 1kg per week
The moderate deficit of 500 calories per day is the default recommendation for fat loss clients. It produces consistent, sustainable fat loss of approximately 0.5kg per week while preserving muscle mass and maintaining performance in the gym. Larger deficits are available for clients who want faster results but come with clear caveats — increased muscle loss risk, greater fatigue, higher likelihood of adherence breakdown and reduced training performance. The very aggressive deficit is only recommended for clients who are significantly overweight and have been advised that rapid fat loss is appropriate for their situation.
The minimum daily calorie floor is 1,500 calories for male clients and 1,200 calories for female clients. No plan generated by this system will go below these floors regardless of the deficit calculation. Eating below these levels creates significant nutritional deficiency risk and is not supported by this programme.
Muscle building — the daily calorie target is set at a surplus above TDEE:
Surplus size
Daily calories
Weekly surplus
Expected weight gain
Lean bulk — recommended
TDEE plus 250 kcal
1,750 kcal
Approximately 0.25kg per week
Standard bulk
TDEE plus 500 kcal
3,500 kcal
Approximately 0.5kg per week
The lean bulk surplus of 250 calories per day is the default recommendation for muscle building clients. It produces slow, steady muscle gain with minimal fat accumulation — approximately 0.25kg per week of which roughly half is muscle and half is fat under optimal conditions. The standard bulk of 500 calories produces faster weight gain but a higher proportion of that gain will be fat. Dirty bulking — eating in a very large surplus with no calorie control — is not supported by this programme. The research is clear that muscle protein synthesis is not meaningfully increased beyond a moderate surplus and the excess fat gain from a dirty bulk requires a prolonged and significant cut to address.
Maintenance — the daily calorie target is set at TDEE. No adjustment is made. The client eats at the number calculated in section 7.1 and monitors weight weekly to confirm the calculation is accurate. Adjustments of plus or minus 100 calories are made at the monthly check-in if weight is drifting in either direction.
Calorie targets are set for both training days and rest days. Training day calories are set at the full target calculated above. Rest day calories are reduced for fat loss and maintenance clients — see section 7.5 for the full training day and rest day calorie split.
7.3 — How to calculate protein targets
Protein is the most important macro. It is set first and protected above all others. If a client can only hit one macro target consistently it must be protein. Carbohydrates and fats can flex — protein cannot.
Protein targets by goal:
Fat loss — 2.2g of protein per kg of bodyweight. The higher protein target during a deficit serves two purposes. First it maximises muscle protein synthesis during a period when the body is under caloric stress and at risk of breaking down muscle tissue for energy. Second high protein intake is satiating — it reduces hunger and makes adherence to the calorie deficit significantly easier. Research consistently shows that higher protein intakes during a calorie deficit produce better body composition outcomes than lower protein intakes at the same calorie level.
Muscle building — 2.0g of protein per kg of bodyweight. Slightly lower than the fat loss target because the calorie surplus provides sufficient energy to support muscle protein synthesis without requiring maximum protein intake. The surplus calories provide the energy — the protein provides the building material.
Maintenance — 2.0g of protein per kg of bodyweight. Supports muscle retention and recomposition at maintenance calories.
Protein targets are always calculated from current bodyweight — not target bodyweight. As the client loses or gains weight across the 12 weeks the protein target adjusts accordingly at the monthly check-in.
Protein is distributed across all meals in the plan. No single meal contains more than 40-50g of protein — research suggests the body cannot effectively utilise more than this amount for muscle protein synthesis in a single sitting. Total daily protein is spread across the client's chosen meal frequency to maximise protein synthesis across the day.
For a 80kg male fat loss client the protein target is 80 x 2.2 = 176g of protein per day. At 4 calories per gram this represents 704 calories from protein — a significant proportion of the daily calorie target that is locked in before carbohydrates and fats are assigned.
7.4 — How to calculate carbohydrate and fat targets
Once protein is set the remaining calories are divided between carbohydrates and fats. The split between carbohydrates and fats is determined by the client's goal, dietary preferences and training demands.
The calculation process:
Step 1 — establish total daily calories from section 7.2 Step 2 — calculate protein calories: protein grams x 4 Step 3 — calculate remaining calories: total calories minus protein calories Step 4 — divide remaining calories between carbohydrates and fats
Default macro split for remaining calories after protein:
Goal
Carbohydrates
Fats
Fat loss
40% of remaining calories
60% of remaining calories
Muscle building
60% of remaining calories
40% of remaining calories
Maintenance
50% of remaining calories
50% of remaining calories
Carbohydrates are prioritised higher for muscle building because carbohydrates are the primary fuel source for weight training. Higher carbohydrate intake supports training performance and glycogen replenishment — both critical for clients trying to build muscle. Fats are prioritised slightly higher for fat loss clients because dietary fat supports hormone production — including testosterone and oestrogen — which is particularly important during a calorie deficit when hormonal disruption is a risk.
Minimum fat floor — fat intake never drops below 0.8g per kg of bodyweight regardless of goal or calorie target. Below this level hormonal function is compromised. For an 80kg client this represents a minimum of 64g of fat per day.
Converting macro splits to grams: Carbohydrates — remaining calories allocated to carbs divided by 4 = grams of carbohydrates Fats — remaining calories allocated to fats divided by 9 = grams of fats
Example calculation for an 80kg male fat loss client with a TDEE of 2,500 and a 500 calorie deficit:
Daily calorie target: 2,000 calories Protein: 176g x 4 = 704 calories Remaining calories: 2,000 - 704 = 1,296 calories Carbohydrates: 1,296 x 0.40 = 518 calories divided by 4 = 130g Fats: 1,296 x 0.60 = 778 calories divided by 9 = 86g
Final macro targets: 176g protein, 130g carbohydrates, 86g fats = 2,002 calories
These targets are presented to the client in the plan as daily gram targets for each macro alongside the total calorie figure. The plan also presents these as per meal targets based on the client's chosen meal frequency — making it easy to hit the targets without needing to do calculations themselves.
7.5 — How to adjust macros for training days vs rest days
Training days and rest days have different energy demands. On training days the body requires more carbohydrates to fuel performance and replenish glycogen post session. On rest days the demand for carbohydrates is lower and the body is primarily focused on recovery and repair rather than fuel.
The training day and rest day split applies to fat loss and maintenance clients. Muscle building clients eat at their full daily target every day — the surplus must be consistent to support muscle growth and reducing calories on rest days risks dropping below the surplus needed for optimal muscle protein synthesis.
Training day and rest day calorie and macro adjustments for fat loss and maintenance clients:
Training days — full daily calorie target as calculated in section 7.2. Full carbohydrate target. Full protein target. Full fat target.
Rest days — calorie reduction of 100-200 calories below the daily target. The reduction comes entirely from carbohydrates — protein and fat targets remain the same on rest days. Reducing carbohydrates on rest days reflects the lower glycogen demand when no training session is taking place.
Rest day carbohydrate reduction:
Goal
Training day carbs
Rest day carbs
Reduction
Fat loss
Full target
Minus 25-40g
100-160 calories
Maintenance
Full target
Minus 15-25g
60-100 calories
The rest day adjustment is modest — this is not carb cycling in its full form, it is a simple and practical acknowledgement that rest days have lower energy demands. Clients who find the training day and rest day distinction confusing or difficult to manage are advised to eat at their training day target every day — the simplicity of a consistent daily target often outweighs the marginal benefit of the rest day reduction for adherence purposes.
Post workout nutrition on training days receives a specific carbohydrate allocation. Approximately 30-50g of fast digesting carbohydrates are reserved for the post workout meal — this replenishes muscle glycogen rapidly and supports recovery. The remaining carbohydrate target for the day is distributed across the other meals.
7.6 — How to adjust for dietary preferences and restrictions
Dietary preferences and restrictions from the Stage 2 inputs filter the food library before the meal plan is generated. The AI never suggests foods that conflict with the client's stated preferences. The adjustments are as follows:
Vegetarian clients — all meat sources are removed from the food library for this client. Protein sources are drawn from eggs, dairy, legumes, tofu, tempeh, Quorn and plant based protein powders. Protein targets remain the same — vegetarian protein sources are adequate to meet the target if the meal plan is structured correctly. The plan notes that hitting protein targets on a vegetarian diet requires more planning and consistency than on an omnivorous diet.
Vegan clients — all animal products including meat, fish, dairy and eggs are removed from the food library. Protein sources are drawn from tofu, tempeh, legumes, edamame, seitan, plant based protein powders and nutritional yeast. Vegan clients are advised that protein quality from plant sources is generally lower than animal sources — leucine content in particular is lower in most plant proteins, which affects muscle protein synthesis. This is addressed by setting the protein target slightly higher for vegan clients — 2.4g per kg for fat loss, 2.2g per kg for muscle building — to compensate for the lower protein quality. A vitamin B12 supplement is added to the supplement recommendations for all vegan clients as B12 is not available from plant sources.
Dairy free clients — all dairy products are removed from the food library. Dairy free protein alternatives — plant based protein powder, lactose free options — are substituted.
Gluten free clients — all gluten containing foods are removed from the food library. Gluten free carbohydrate sources — rice, potatoes, sweet potatoes, oats labelled gluten free, quinoa — are substituted.
Foods the client will not eat — any foods listed in the free text field of Stage 2 are flagged and removed from the food library for this client before the meal plan is generated. The AI substitutes alternatives with similar macro profiles from the remaining available foods.
All dietary adjustments are applied before any other meal plan generation step. The filtered food library is the only library the AI draws from for that client.
7.7 — How to build the meal plan structure
The meal plan is built around the client's chosen meal frequency from the Stage 2 inputs — 2, 3, 4 or 5 meals per day. The total daily calorie and macro targets are divided across the chosen number of meals with adjustments for training day and rest day where applicable.
General meal structure principles:
Protein is distributed as evenly as possible across all meals. No meal contains more than 40-50g of protein. If the daily protein target divided by the number of meals produces a per meal figure above 50g the meal frequency is flagged as potentially insufficient for optimal protein synthesis — the client is advised that adding a meal or a protein shake between meals would improve results.
Carbohydrates are front loaded toward training time. The largest carbohydrate meal is the pre workout meal — approximately 30-40% of daily carbohydrates are consumed in the two hours before training. The post workout meal receives 20-25% of daily carbohydrates as fast digesting sources. The remaining carbohydrates are distributed across the other meals.
Fats are distributed across meals that do not immediately surround the training session. Fat slows digestion — keeping fat low in the pre and post workout meals ensures nutrients are available quickly when demand is highest. Fats are concentrated in breakfast, evening meals and any meals that fall well away from the training window.
Meal plan structure by meal frequency:
2 meals per day — not recommended as the default for any client as distributing sufficient protein across only two meals is extremely challenging. If a client selects 2 meals the plan is built around 2 large meals plus 1-2 protein shakes that sit between the meals. The client is advised that 2 meals without additional protein supplementation will make hitting protein targets very difficult.
3 meals per day — breakfast, lunch and dinner with protein and macros distributed evenly across all three. A protein shake is recommended as a fourth mini meal for clients whose protein target per meal exceeds 50g across 3 meals. Suitable for all goals.
4 meals per day — breakfast, mid morning meal, lunch and dinner. Allows better protein distribution and more consistent energy levels throughout the day. The recommended default for most clients.
5 meals per day — breakfast, mid morning meal, lunch, afternoon meal and dinner. Best protein distribution and most consistent energy levels. Recommended for clients with high protein targets — advanced muscle building clients and heavier clients with large total calorie requirements.
Each meal in the plan is presented with:
Total calories for the meal
Protein, carbohydrate and fat targets for the meal in grams
Specific food suggestions from the food library with portion sizes
Two to three alternative food options for each suggested food with similar macro profiles
Preparation notes where relevant
7.8 — How to build alternative food options
Every food suggestion in the meal plan comes with two to three alternatives that have similar macro profiles. This serves two purposes. First it accommodates food preferences — clients who dislike a suggested food can swap to an alternative without recalculating their macros. Second it prevents meal plan fatigue — having alternatives for every food means the client can vary their meals without leaving the plan.
Alternative food matching criteria:
The alternative must match the primary food within the following tolerances per 100g:
Protein — within 5g
Calories — within 50 calories
Carbohydrates — within 10g
Fats — within 5g
Alternatives are drawn from the same food category where possible — a chicken breast alternative is another lean protein source, not a high fat protein source. A rice alternative is another complex carbohydrate, not a simple sugar. Category matching ensures the macro profile of the meal stays consistent even when the specific food changes.
The alternative food options are presented in the plan as a simple list below each food suggestion:
Example: Chicken breast 150g — 165 calories, 31g protein, 0g carbs, 3.5g fat Alternatives: Turkey breast 150g / Tuna steak 150g / Cod fillet 170g
Clients can swap any food for any of its listed alternatives at any time without needing to recalculate. The macros stay close enough to the target that the swap does not meaningfully affect the plan.
7.9 — How to build the grocery list
The grocery list is generated automatically from the meal plan. It lists every food required for the full 7 day meal plan organised by category — making the weekly shop as simple and efficient as possible.
Grocery list structure:
The list is organised into the following categories:
Proteins — meat, fish, eggs, dairy, plant proteins
Carbohydrates — grains, rice, pasta, bread, potatoes, oats
Fruits and vegetables — all fresh produce
Fats — oils, nuts, nut butters, avocado
Dairy and alternatives — milk, yogurt, cheese, plant milks
Supplements — protein powder, creatine and any other recommended supplements
Condiments and flavourings — sauces, spices, herbs
Snacks — any planned snack foods
Each item on the grocery list includes the total quantity required for the full week — calculated from the portion sizes in the meal plan multiplied by the number of times each food appears across 7 days. This removes the need for the client to calculate quantities themselves.
Example grocery list entry: Chicken breast — 1,050g total (150g x 7 days)
The grocery list accounts for the client's chosen meal plan option — full 7 day meal plan or macros only. Clients who chose macros only do not receive a grocery list as their food choices are flexible.
The grocery list is presented as a clean, printable or shareable document that the client can take directly to the supermarket or use to place an online order. It is one of the highest perceived value components of the plan — practical, time saving and immediately useful.
7.10 — How to adjust nutrition across 12 weeks
Unlike the training plan which uses a single template with progressive weight increases, the nutrition plan requires active monitoring and adjustment across 12 weeks. Body weight changes, training intensity changes and adaptation all affect the accuracy of the initial calorie calculation. The plan is therefore a living document — the targets set at the start are the best available estimate, not fixed numbers.
Adjustments are made at two points: weekly based on the client's self-logged body weight data, and monthly at the formal check-in.
Weekly monitoring — the client logs their weight in the progress tracker every week under the same conditions — morning, fasted, after using the bathroom. A single weekly weigh-in is used rather than daily weigh-ins to reduce the impact of normal day to day fluctuations. The trend over two to three weeks is what matters — not any single reading.
Weekly weight change benchmarks:
Goal
Target weekly change
Action if off target
Fat loss
Minus 0.3-0.7kg per week
See adjustment table below
Muscle building
Plus 0.15-0.35kg per week
See adjustment table below
Maintenance
Within plus or minus 0.5kg over 2 weeks
Adjust by plus or minus 100 calories
adjustment protocol for fat loss clients:
Situation
Action
Losing more than 0.7kg per week for 2+ weeks
Increase calories by 200-300 per day — loss is too fast, muscle mass at risk
Losing 0.3-0.7kg per week
No adjustment needed — on track
Losing less than 0.3kg per week for 2+ weeks
Increase steps by 1,000 per day first — if no change after one week reduce calories by 200-300 per day
Compliant, no weight loss for 2+ weeks — genuine stall
Reduce calories by 200-300 per day and increase steps by 1,000 per day simultaneously
Compliance poor — not hitting targets
Fix compliance before touching calories — no adjustment until client is consistently hitting targets
No loss despite previous reductions
Reassess TDEE calculation, consider one week diet break at maintenance before continuing
Adjustment protocol for muscle building clients:
Situation
Action
Gaining more than 0.35kg per week for 2+ weeks
Reduce calories by 100-150 per day — gaining too fast, excess fat accumulation
Gaining 0.15-0.35kg per week
No adjustment needed — on track
Gaining less than 0.15kg per week for 2+ weeks
Increase calories by 100-150 per day
No weight gain for 2+ weeks
Check compliance first, then increase calories by 150-200 per day
Monthly check-in — at the end of each month the full nutrition plan is reviewed. Body weight is reassessed and protein targets are recalculated based on current weight. Calorie targets are adjusted based on the monthly trend. If the client's goal has changed — for example they want to transition from fat loss to muscle building — the full plan is regenerated with the new goal parameters.
Nutrition adjustments are always made in small increments — 100-200 calories at a time. Large calorie swings create instability and make it impossible to identify what is actually working. Small, consistent adjustments based on real data produce the most accurate and effective nutrition plan over time.
At the end of 12 weeks the client's updated stats — current weight, current measurements, current performance data — are fed back into the system to generate the starting point for plan 2. The second plan begins from where the first plan ended — not from the original starting point.

REVIEW THIS 
Section 9 — Injury and Limitation Rules
Cover:
How you handle each common injury — knees, lower back, shoulders, hips, wrists, ankles
Which exercises are removed for each injury
Which exercises replace them
How severe vs mild limitations change the programme
Any injuries where you'd recommend they see a professional before starting
9.1 — How injuries are captured and processed
Injuries and physical limitations are captured during the Stage 2 input flow through a three part process. First the client selects from a checklist of common injury sites — knees, lower back, shoulders, hips, wrists, elbows, ankles and calves. Second the client describes in free text what the injury is and what it stops them doing. Third the system presents each potentially affected exercise individually and asks the client — can you perform this exercise? Yes or no.
The free text description carries more weight than the checklist selection. Clients frequently underestimate their limitations when selecting from a checklist — ticking mild when their description suggests moderate or severe. The AI reads both inputs and responds to the more conservative interpretation. If the checklist says mild but the description suggests significant limitation the plan is built around the more cautious interpretation.
The injury information feeds into three outputs. First — the training plan itself, where affected exercises are substituted or removed based on the prompted substitution process. Second — the exercise video library, where injury specific coaching notes are added to relevant exercises flagging the modification required. Third — the info doc, where the client receives clear guidance on training with their specific limitation including what to watch for and when to stop.
All injury related plan modifications are noted in the plan with a brief explanation — the client is always told why an exercise has been changed, not just that it has been changed. Understanding the reason builds compliance and trust.
9.2 — The substitution hierarchy
When an exercise is flagged as potentially problematic for a client's injury the system follows a strict substitution hierarchy. The goal is always to keep the client training as effectively as possible while removing the specific movement or load that causes the problem.
The hierarchy is applied in this order:
Step 1 — Substitute. Replace the affected exercise with an alternative that targets the same muscle group through a different movement pattern or range of motion that does not aggravate the injury. This is always the first attempt — removing an exercise entirely is a last resort.
Step 2 — Modify. If a direct substitute is not available or appropriate, modify the original exercise. Modifications include reducing range of motion, changing the load, adjusting the angle, switching from bilateral to unilateral or from free weight to machine. The same movement pattern is kept but the aggravating element is removed.
Step 3 — Remove. If neither substitution nor modification produces a safe version of the movement, the exercise is removed entirely. Volume is redistributed to other exercises in the session that do not affect the injured area.
The substitution hierarchy is applied per exercise — not per session. A client with a knee injury does not get a completely different leg session. They get the same session structure with knee aggravating exercises substituted or modified and everything else kept intact.
9.3 — Knee injury protocols
Knee injuries are the most common limitation presented by clients. The knee is a hinge joint under significant load during most lower body movements — proper management is essential for both safety and long term training longevity.
Exercises removed for knee injuries:
Full depth barbell squat — replaced with hack squat at reduced depth or leg press with feet high on platform
Deep lunges — replaced with step ups at reduced height or leg press single leg
Bulgarian split squat — removed entirely for moderate to severe knee issues
Leg press with feet low on platform — replaced with leg press feet high which reduces knee stress significantly
Box jumps or any plyometric lower body movement — removed entirely
Exercises kept with modifications:
Hack squat — reduce depth, keep above 90 degrees if pain occurs below
Leg press — feet high on the platform, do not allow knees to travel excessively past the toes
Leg extension — partial range only, starting from 90 degrees rather than full flexion. Reduces patella tendon stress significantly
Romanian deadlift — generally well tolerated as the knee does not flex significantly. Monitor carefully
Step ups — use a lower box height
Exercises added for knee injury rehabilitation and strengthening:
Terminal knee extensions with a resistance band — the single best exercise for VMO activation and knee joint stability. Always added for clients with knee issues
Seated hamstring curl — strengthens the hamstrings which support the knee joint from behind
Adductor machine — strong adductors reduce knee valgus and improve knee stability
Additional recommendations for knee injuries:
Knee sleeves recommended for all clients with knee issues — provides compression, warmth and proprioceptive feedback during training
Warm up extended for knee injury clients — additional hamstring curl sets and terminal knee extensions before any weighted knee work
Avoid training through sharp, localised or worsening knee pain — dull ache during training is acceptable, sharp pain is not
If pain worsens consistently across multiple sessions refer to physiotherapist before continuing
Severity classification:
Severity
Description
Approach
Mild
Occasional discomfort, no swelling, full daily function
Modify as above, monitor carefully
Moderate
Regular pain during activity, some swelling, limited daily function
Significant modification, avoid all high compression movements
Severe
Constant pain, significant swelling, limited walking
Hold plan, refer to physiotherapist, do not charge until cleared
9.4 — Lower back injury protocols
Lower back issues are the second most common limitation and require careful management. The lower back is involved in almost every compound movement — errors in managing lower back limitations create injury risk across the entire programme.
Exercises removed for lower back injuries:
Conventional barbell deadlift — already excluded from the programme as established in section 2
Barbell bent over row — replaced with chest supported row which removes all lower back demand
Good mornings — already excluded from the programme
Barbell back squat — replaced with hack squat or leg press which provide back support
Any loaded spinal flexion movement — sit ups and crunches with significant load removed, replaced with bodyweight or very light versions
Romanian deadlift — monitored carefully, removed if lower back pain occurs during the hip hinge
Exercises kept with modifications:
All chest supported rowing variations — the safest rowing option for lower back issues as the chest pad removes spinal loading entirely
Leg press — back supported throughout, generally very safe for lower back conditions
Hack squat — back pad provides support, monitor carefully
Lat pulldown and cable variations — seated with back supported, generally well tolerated
Core work — modified to avoid loaded spinal flexion. Dead bugs, bird dogs and planks replace crunches and sit ups for clients with acute lower back issues
Exercises added for lower back injury management:
Dead bug — excellent core stability exercise that does not load the spine
Bird dog — develops posterior chain stability without spinal loading
Glute bridge — strengthens the glutes which support and protect the lower back
Hip thrust — builds glute strength which directly reduces lower back stress during daily activity and training
Additional recommendations for lower back injuries:
Core strengthening is the priority for all lower back clients — a strong core protects the lower back from future injury
All hip hinge movements require strict form assessment — if the client cannot maintain a neutral spine throughout the hinge the exercise is removed until mobility improves
Sitting for long periods between sessions aggravates most lower back conditions — clients are advised to move regularly throughout the day
Heat before training and ice after training can help manage lower back discomfort
Refer to physiotherapist for anything beyond general tightness or mild discomfort
Severity classification:
Severity
Description
Approach
Mild
General tightness, no acute pain, full daily function
Modify as above, core work priority
Moderate
Regular pain during activity, limited bending or lifting in daily life
Significant modification, chest supported movements only, no hip hinge
Severe
Acute pain, radiating pain down the leg, numbness or tingling
Hold plan immediately, refer to physiotherapist, do not charge until cleared
Radiating pain down the leg — sciatica — is a red flag condition. Any client describing pain that travels from the lower back down the leg, numbness, tingling or weakness in the leg is referred to a medical professional immediately. The plan is held until medical clearance is received.
9.5 — Shoulder injury protocols
Shoulder injuries are the most complex to manage because the shoulder is involved in almost every upper body pushing and pulling movement. The shoulder is also the most commonly injured joint in weight training — poor shoulder health is one of the primary reasons experienced lifters have to reduce or stop training.
The rotator cuff warm up is non-negotiable for all clients with any shoulder history. It is the single most important injury prevention tool for shoulder health and is always performed before any upper body pressing work regardless of severity.
Exercises removed for shoulder injuries:
Behind the neck press and behind the neck pull — excluded for all clients regardless of shoulder history as established in section 2
Upright row — high impingement risk, removed for any client with shoulder issues
Overhead barbell press — replaced with dumbbell shoulder press which allows greater joint freedom
Dips — removed for AC joint and impingement issues due to the end range shoulder stress
Wide grip bench press — replaced with a neutral or closer grip to reduce shoulder stress
Exercises kept with modifications by injury type:
Rotator cuff issues:
All overhead pressing removed — replaced with lateral raises, face pulls and external rotation work
Horizontal pressing kept if pain free — flat dumbbell press at reduced range of motion
All pulling movements generally kept — rows and lat pulldowns with careful monitoring
Emphasis on external rotation exercises — band external rotation, face pulls, rear delt flies
AC joint issues:
Heavy bench press removed — replaced with incline dumbbell press or machine chest press which reduce AC joint stress
Dips removed — significant AC joint stress at the bottom position
Incline pressing generally better tolerated than flat pressing for AC joint issues
Monitor all pressing movements carefully — if pain occurs at the top of any pressing movement the AC joint is involved and the exercise is modified or removed
General shoulder impingement:
All overhead pressing removed
Lateral raises modified — reduce range of motion, stop before shoulder height if pain occurs
All pressing movements monitored — reduce range of motion to pain free range
Pulling movements generally safe and actively encouraged — strong back muscles support shoulder health
Face pulls and band pull aparts prioritised as both warm up and working exercises
Additional recommendations for shoulder injuries:
The rotator cuff warm up is extended for shoulder injury clients — additional sets and more time spent on external rotation work before any pressing
Increase pulling volume relative to pushing volume — a 2:1 pull to push ratio is recommended for clients with shoulder issues to balance the joint
Sleep position matters — clients who sleep on their shoulder are advised to try sleeping on their back or opposite side
For serious shoulder conditions — significant rotator cuff tears, labral tears, post-surgical shoulders — the plan is held and medical clearance is required before any upper body work is programmed
Severity classification:
Severity
Description
Approach
Mild
Occasional discomfort at end range, no daily function impact
Modify range of motion, prioritise rotator cuff warm up
Moderate
Regular pain during pressing movements, limited overhead reaching
Remove overhead pressing, modify all pressing, increase pulling volume
Severe
Constant pain, significant loss of range of motion, pain at rest
Hold upper body plan, refer to physiotherapist, lower body only until cleared
9.6 — Hip injury protocols
Hip issues affect squatting patterns, hinge patterns and any unilateral lower body movement. They are less common than knee and lower back issues but require careful management when present.
Exercises removed for hip injuries:
Bulgarian split squat — the rear hip flexor stretch is significant and often painful for hip conditions
Deep squatting movements — full depth hack squat and barbell squat replaced with leg press and partial depth movements
Hip thrust — the hip crease loading position may be uncomfortable depending on the hip condition
Exercises kept with modifications:
Leg press — generally well tolerated, adjust foot width and position to find a comfortable hip angle
Romanian deadlift — monitor the hip hinge carefully, reduce range of motion if hip pain occurs
Seated hamstring curl — hip is in flexion which may or may not be comfortable depending on the condition, monitor
Adductor and abductor machines — generally well tolerated, reduce range of motion if pain occurs
Additional recommendations for hip injuries:
Hip mobility work is recommended alongside training — hip flexor stretches, pigeon pose, 90/90 hip mobility drills
Refer to physiotherapist for hip labral tears, hip impingement diagnosed conditions or any hip condition that significantly limits the squat pattern
Severity classification:
Severity
Description
Approach
Mild
Occasional tightness or discomfort in deep positions
Modify depth and range of motion
Moderate
Regular pain during squatting or hinge patterns
Remove deep squatting, modify all hip dominant movements
Severe
Pain at rest, clicking with pain, diagnosed labral tear or impingement
Hold lower body plan, refer to physiotherapist
9.7 — Wrist and elbow injury protocols
Wrist and elbow issues primarily affect grip and pressing movements. They are often caused by overuse and respond well to simple modifications.
Wrist injury modifications:
Straight barbell exercises — replaced with EZ bar or dumbbell variations which allow the wrist to find a more natural position
Front squats — excluded for wrist issues due to the rack position demand
Wrist wraps recommended for all wrist injury clients during pressing movements
Neutral grip pressing preferred — dumbbell press with palms facing each other reduces wrist stress significantly
Elbow injury modifications:
Skull crushers — reduce range of motion or replace with cable tricep pushdown which is more elbow friendly
Preacher curl — never lock out at the bottom, significant bicep tendon stress at full extension
Tricep dips — monitor carefully, reduce range of motion if elbow pain occurs
All curling movements — reduce weight and range of motion, monitor carefully
Close grip bench press — replace with tricep pushdowns if elbow pain occurs
Tennis elbow — lateral epicondylitis:
Avoid all gripping exercises that involve pronation under load — barbell curls, reverse curls, upright rows
EZ bar and neutral grip preferred for all exercises
Reduce overall training volume on affected arm
Refer to physiotherapist if pain is significant or persistent
Golfer's elbow — medial epicondylitis:
Avoid exercises involving strong wrist flexion under load
Monitor all pressing movements carefully
Refer to physiotherapist if pain is significant or persistent
Severity classification:
Severity
Description
Approach
Mild
Occasional discomfort during specific movements
Modify grip and range of motion, use wraps
Moderate
Regular pain during training, affects daily gripping activities
Significant modification, EZ bar and neutral grip only
Severe
Constant pain, swelling, numbness or tingling in fingers
Hold affected limb work, refer to physiotherapist
9.8 — Ankle and calf injury protocols
Ankle and calf injuries primarily affect standing exercises, unilateral movements and calf training.
Exercises removed for ankle injuries:
Standing calf raise — replaced with seated calf raise which places less demand on the ankle joint
Bulgarian split squat — ankle stability demands are significant, remove for ankle issues
Step ups — remove if ankle stability is compromised
Any exercise requiring significant ankle dorsiflexion — reduce depth on squatting movements
Exercises kept with modifications:
Leg press — ankle is not significantly loaded, generally very safe
Seated calf raise — safe for most ankle conditions as the ankle is not under body weight load
All seated and lying exercises — unaffected by ankle limitations
Calf strain modifications:
All calf training removed during acute phase — minimum two weeks rest for mild strain, four to six weeks for moderate to severe
Return to calf training with very light weight and reduced range of motion
Avoid full stretch position at the bottom of calf raises until strain is fully healed — the stretch position is where calf strains most commonly occur or recur
Achilles tendon issues:
Avoid full stretch position in all calf exercises — reduce range of motion significantly
Eccentric calf raises on a step — a specific rehabilitation exercise with strong evidence for Achilles tendinopathy, added to the plan for clients with Achilles issues
Refer to physiotherapist for Achilles tendinopathy — this condition responds very well to specific rehabilitation protocols
Severity classification:
Severity
Description
Approach
Mild
Occasional tightness, full daily function
Modify range of motion, avoid full stretch
Moderate
Regular pain during activity, limited walking speed
Remove calf training, modify all standing exercises
Severe
Acute pain, significant swelling, inability to bear weight
Hold lower body plan entirely, refer to physiotherapist or A&E
9.9 — Multiple simultaneous injuries
Clients who present with multiple simultaneous injuries require a more significant plan modification. The approach is the same as for single injuries — apply the substitution hierarchy for each injury independently — but the cumulative effect of multiple modifications must be assessed to ensure the remaining plan is still coherent and effective.
When multiple injuries are present the following rules apply:
Rule 1 — assess the combined impact. If two injuries together remove the majority of exercises from a session type — for example a knee injury plus a lower back injury removing most leg exercises — the session is restructured around the remaining safe movements rather than trying to maintain the original session structure.
Rule 2 — prioritise the more serious injury. If two injuries conflict — for example a lower back injury that benefits from hip thrusts but a hip injury that makes hip thrusts uncomfortable — the more serious or acute injury takes priority.
Rule 3 — maintain training volume where possible. If upper body work is unaffected by lower body injuries the upper body sessions remain at full volume. The overall training stimulus is maintained wherever the injury allows.
Rule 4 — flag significantly compromised plans. If multiple injuries reduce the training plan to a point where meaningful progress is unlikely — for example a shoulder injury plus a lower back injury plus a knee injury in a client who selected a 4 day PPL split — the client is informed clearly that their injury combination significantly limits what the plan can achieve and a modified approach is suggested. A physiotherapy referral is strongly recommended before the plan begins.
Rule 5 — never force a plan that cannot be delivered safely. If the injury combination means a safe and effective plan cannot be built the plan is held, the client is not charged and they are directed to seek professional assessment.
9.10 — When not to train — red flag conditions
There are specific conditions and situations where training must stop immediately and medical advice must be sought before returning. These are non-negotiable — no plan recommendation overrides them.
Absolute red flags — stop training immediately and seek medical attention:
Chest pain or pressure during training — could indicate cardiac event. Stop immediately, seek emergency medical attention
Dizziness or loss of consciousness during training — stop immediately, do not resume the session
Severe shortness of breath disproportionate to exercise intensity — stop immediately
Numbness or tingling radiating down the arm — could indicate cardiac or cervical spine involvement
Radiating pain from the lower back down the leg with numbness or weakness — sciatica or disc involvement, stop lower back loading immediately
Joint locking — a joint that locks and cannot be moved freely, stop immediately and seek medical assessment
Sudden sharp joint pain during training — distinct from muscle soreness, stop the exercise immediately
Significant swelling appearing rapidly during or after training — stop training the affected area, ice and elevate, seek medical advice if swelling is significant
Visible deformity — any visual change in joint shape following an incident during training, seek emergency medical attention
Training modification conditions — adjust but do not stop entirely:
General illness — fever, active infection, flu symptoms. Rest is more important than training when the body is fighting illness. Return when symptom free for 48 hours
Extreme fatigue from accumulated training stress — a planned or unplanned deload is appropriate. Reduce weight by 40-50% and volume by 50% for one week. This is the deload protocol — see section 1.3
Excessive muscle soreness from training too hard for too long — do not train the excessively sore muscle group until soreness has significantly reduced. Active recovery — walking, light movement — is encouraged. A deload week may be appropriate if multiple muscle groups are simultaneously excessively sore
Poor sleep for multiple consecutive nights — training can continue but intensity expectations are reduced. Heavy compound movements under significant fatigue increase injury risk. Reduce working weights by 10-20% until sleep normalises
High stress periods — cortisol is elevated during high stress, recovery is compromised. Reduce training volume temporarily rather than pushing through at full intensity
The deload protocol for excessive soreness and accumulated fatigue:
Trigger — client reports significant soreness across multiple muscle groups, persistent fatigue that does not resolve between sessions, strength going backwards across multiple exercises for two or more weeks
Protocol — one week at 50-60% of normal working weights, two sets only per exercise, same exercises and structure
Return — full working weights the following week. The deload almost always results in a personal best the week after
Frequency — a deload is not scheduled in the 12 week plan but is available whenever the trigger conditions are met. Most clients will need one deload across a 12 week programme
9.11 — Returning to training after injury
Returning to training after an injury or a period of rest requires a structured approach. Jumping back to pre-injury weights and volume immediately is the most common cause of re-injury.
The return to training protocol:
Week 1 — re-establishment week. Treat this like a second baseline week. Light weights, full range of motion, focus entirely on movement quality. No working sets at pre-injury weight. Use the feeler set approach from section 2.9 for every exercise — multiple build up sets, no heavy working sets.
Week 2 — progressive return. Begin working sets at 50-60% of pre-injury working weight. Follow the standard rep ranges. If the injury site is pain free throughout — proceed to week 3.
Week 3 — approach pre-injury weights. Work up to 80-90% of pre-injury working weight. Monitor the injury site carefully — any pain signals a return to week 2 protocol.
Week 4 onwards — return to standard progression. Pre-injury weights are resumed and normal progressive overload resumes from this point.
For clients returning after a break of 4 weeks or more the same protocol applies regardless of whether the break was due to injury or other reasons. Muscle memory accelerates the return — most clients return to pre-break weights within 3-4 weeks.
For clients returning after surgery the return to training protocol is extended and must be cleared by the treating surgeon or physiotherapist before beginning. No plan is generated for post-surgical clients until medical clearance is received in writing — the client is not charged until this clearance is confirmed and the plan is activated.
The injury site is monitored throughout the return period. If pain returns at any point during the return protocol the client drops back one week in the protocol and reassesses. Two returns to week 1 without progress toward pain free training is a signal to seek further medical assessment before continuing.
Section 10 — Plan Presentation Rules
This tells the AI how the finished plan should look and feel.
Cover:
The tone of voice — how you speak to a client in writing
How each training session should be laid out — format, order of information
How the nutrition plan should be presented
How the grocery list should be structured
What the week overview looks like
Any motivational language you want included
What the 12 week overview page looks like
10.1 — Overall document philosophy
The plan is the product. It is the thing the client paid for and the thing they will judge Plus 4 Performance on. It must look and feel like a professional coached document — not a generated template, not a generic PDF and not an over-designed marketing piece. It should feel like a coach who knows this client sat down and built something specifically for them.
Three principles govern every presentation decision:
Personal — the client's name, stats, goal and situation are woven throughout. The plan never feels generic. Every page reinforces that this was built for this specific person.
Practical — the plan is designed to be used in the gym on a phone screen. Every session page is clean, readable at a glance and contains exactly the information needed and nothing more. Nothing that clutters, nothing that confuses.
Professional — the visual standard matches the quality of the content. Clean typography, consistent branding, logical structure. A plan that looks as good as it performs.
10.2 — Document structure and page order
The plan is delivered as a single PDF document. The document follows this structure in this exact order:
Page 1 — Personal summary page Page 2 — How to use this plan Pages 3 onwards — Training sessions, one page per session type Following training — Nutrition summary page Following nutrition summary — Meal plan or macros page Following meal plan — Grocery list Final page — What happens next
The document is designed to be read from front to back once before training begins — the client reads pages 1 and 2 in full, scans the training and nutrition sections, and then uses individual session pages as their in-gym reference throughout the 12 weeks.
The weight tracking system is entirely separate from the PDF plan. It lives on the Plus 4 Performance website in the dedicated tracker section. The plan references the tracker and directs the client to it — the plan itself contains no weight logging fields. This keeps the plan clean, readable and optimised for phone screens in the gym.
10.3 — Page 1 — Personal summary page
The personal summary page is the most important page in the document. It is the first thing the client sees and it sets the tone for everything that follows. It must feel immediately personal and impressive.
Layout and content:
Header — Plus 4 Performance logo top left, plan generation date top right.
Title — large, bold, personalised: [Client name]'s 12 Week [Goal] Plan Example: James's 12 Week Muscle Building Plan Example: Sarah's 12 Week Fat Loss Plan
Stats block — clean two column layout:
Your stats
Your targets
Age: [X]
Daily calories: [X] kcal
Height: [X] cm
Protein: [X]g
Current weight: [X] kg
Carbohydrates: [X]g
Target weight: [X] kg
Fats: [X]g
Goal: [Goal]
Training days: [X] days per week
Experience: [Level]
Training split: [Split]
Plan start date: [Date]
Plan end date: [Date]
Supplement recommendations block — brief, clean list of the recommended supplements for this client based on their goal and dietary preferences.
Personal note — 4-6 lines in the coach's voice. This is the most important piece of copy in the entire document. It acknowledges the client's specific goal, sets realistic expectations, establishes the tone and makes the client feel like a real coach is behind this plan. It is generated specifically for each client based on their inputs — not a generic paragraph with their name inserted.
Example personal note for a fat loss client:
"James — here's your plan. You've told us you want to drop to 80kg and you're training 4 days a week with access to a full gym. That's everything we need. Your calorie target gives you a steady, sustainable deficit — you'll be losing fat without burning out or losing the muscle you've built. The training is straightforward: show up, add weight when you can, hit your protein. Do that consistently for 12 weeks and the results will speak for themselves. Week 1 is about finding your numbers. From week 2 you build. Let's go."
Example personal note for a muscle building client:
"Tom — 12 weeks to add some real size. You're eating in a surplus, you're training 5 days a week and you've got the experience to push hard. The plan is built around progressive overload — every week the goal is simple, add weight to the bar when you hit the top of your rep range. The nutrition gives you what you need to grow. Do the work, eat the food, sleep properly. Everything else takes care of itself."
The personal note never uses generic motivational language. It speaks directly to this client's specific situation using the information they provided.
10.4 — Page 2 — How to use this plan
This page covers everything the client needs to know before session 1. It is written in the coach's voice — direct, clear, no fluff. Bullet point format throughout. Maximum one page.
Content:
Progressive overload — the one rule
Hit the top of your rep range on the heavy set? Add weight next week. No exceptions.
Week 1 is your baseline week — find your weights, don't push to failure
Log every weight in the tracker at plus4performance.com — this is how you measure progress
How the rep ranges work
Two working sets per exercise — one heavy, one back off
Heavy set: the first rep range shown — e.g. 6-8 reps
Back off set: the second rep range shown — e.g. 10-12 reps, slightly lighter weight
1-2 reps left in the tank on every set — not to failure except rest pause sets
Warm up
First exercise each muscle group — do as many feeler sets as you need to feel ready
Push day — always do the rotator cuff warm up before touching any weights
If time allows — 5 minutes on the bike or treadmill before you start
If something hurts
Discomfort is normal. Sharp pain is not. Stop the exercise immediately if something feels wrong
Check the injury guidance in your info doc
Swap the exercise using the video library — every exercise has alternatives listed
Nutrition
Hit your protein target first — everything else follows
Weigh your food where possible — estimating leads to errors that compound
Your cheat meal is built in — one per week, keep it reasonable
Track in MyFitnessPal or Cronometer until you know your numbers instinctively
The video library
Every exercise in this plan has a coaching video at plus4performance.com/videos
Watch it before your first session — especially for any exercise you haven't done before
Coaching cues are shown on screen — use them
The tracker
Log your weights after every session at plus4performance.com/tracker
Weigh yourself every week — same time, same conditions, first thing in the morning
Your progress is only visible if you track it
One last thing
Miss a session? Don't double up the next day. Just pick up where you left off.
Bad week? Same. One bad week changes nothing. Ten bad weeks changes everything.
Questions? [Support contact details]

10.9 — Final page — what happens next
The final page is a short closing message in the coach's voice. It acknowledges what the client is about to start, sets expectations and plants the seed for what comes after 12 weeks.
Format — short paragraphs, coach's voice, no bullet points on this page. This is the one page in the document that reads like a personal letter rather than a structured reference page.
Example:
"Right — that's everything you need. 12 weeks of training, nutrition, the full picture.
Week 1 — find your numbers. Don't go chasing big weights yet. Get the movement patterns right and log everything.
By week 4 you'll be progressing on most lifts and starting to feel the difference. By week 8 it's visible. By week 12 you'll have proof of what consistent work actually produces.
The difference between people who get results and people who don't isn't talent or genetics. It's showing up when they don't feel like it and staying consistent when progress feels slow. That's it.
When you hit week 12 — log your personal bests. Then come back for plan 2. This is where it starts, not where it ends.
Any questions — [support contact]. Video library — plus4performance.com/videos. Tracker — plus4performance.com/tracker.
Now go to work."
[Coach signature — name, Plus 4 Performance] [Plus 4 Performance logo]
10.10 — Branding and visual standards
Every page of the plan must be consistently branded. The visual standard reflects the quality of the coaching behind it — a poorly designed plan undermines trust in the product regardless of how good the content is.
Logo placement:
Plus 4 Performance logo appears on page 1 header and the final page footer
A small logo watermark appears in the footer of every page
Never placed in a position that competes with the content
Typography:
Headings — bold, clean sans-serif font. Recommended: Inter, Montserrat or similar
Body text — regular weight, same sans-serif family. Minimum 11pt for readability on phone screens
Exercise tables — 10-11pt, tight but readable
Never use more than two font weights — regular and bold. No italics except for injury modification notes
Colour scheme:
Primary colour — to be defined by the Plus 4 Performance brand identity
Table headers and section dividers use the primary colour
Body text — near black, not pure black — easier on screen
White background throughout — no dark backgrounds
Accent colour for callout boxes — injury notes, important reminders
Page layout:
Consistent margins throughout — 15mm on all sides
Clear section breaks between exercises in tables — alternating very light grey rows for readability
Adequate white space — a cluttered plan is an unused plan
Every page has a clear header identifying the page content and the client name
Mobile optimisation:
The PDF is optimised for phone screen viewing — the primary device used in the gym
Font sizes are never below 10pt
Tables are designed to fit a phone screen without horizontal scrolling
The document is tested on a phone screen before finalising the template
Personalisation throughout:
The client's name appears on every page in the header or footer
The plan title on page 1 uses their name and their goal
The personal note on page 1 is generated specifically for their inputs
Injury modifications are noted in-line with the client's specific condition referenced
The nutrition targets on every nutrition page are their specific numbers — never generic ranges
10.11 — Plan delivery
The plan is delivered to the client immediately on payment confirmation. Delivery is automatic — no manual intervention required.
Delivery method:
PDF download link sent via automated email immediately on payment
The PDF is also available in the client's account on the Plus 4 Performance website
The email contains the download link, a link to the video library and a link to the tracker
A second email is sent 24 hours later — a short welcome message in the coach's voice checking they have opened the plan and reminding them of the key resources
Email subject lines:
Delivery email: "Your Plus 4 Performance plan is ready, [Name]"
24 hour follow up: "Did you get a chance to look through your plan, [Name]?"
File format and naming:
PDF format — universally compatible, maintains formatting across all devices
File name: Plus4Performance_[ClientName]12WeekPlan[Date].pdf
File size optimised — large enough to be high quality, small enough to download quickly on mobile
Plan access:
The download link is available for 12 months from the date of purchase
Clients who lose access to their plan can request a re-send through the support contact
Subscribers on the monthly plan always have access to their current plan through their account
10.12 — Things to build — flagged during Section 10
The following items were identified during the drafting of Section 10 as requiring action during the product build phase. These are flagged here to ensure nothing is missed:
Video library — every exercise in section 4.10 requires a coaching video to be filmed and uploaded to plus4performance.com/videos. The coaching cues in section 4.10 are the scripts for these videos.
Tracker page — a dedicated progress tracking page on the website where clients log weights after every session and body weight weekly. Separate from the PDF plan.
PDF template — the plan document template needs to be designed to the visual standards in section 10.10 before the plan generator is built. The template is what the AI populates with client specific data.
Email automation — delivery email and 24 hour follow up email need to be written and set up in the email automation system as established in the marketing section.
Account system — clients need an account on the website to access their plan, the video library and the tracker. This requires a login system to be built.
Mobile optimisation testing — the PDF template must be tested on multiple phone screens before launch to ensure readability in a gym environment.
Nutrition API integration — connect Nutritionix, Edamam or Open Food Facts for verified macro data as agreed in section 8.
Branded creator pages — the theming system that allows creator logos, colours and tone to be applied to the plan template for the white label product.
